\n\t\t\t\tNOTE:\xe2\x80\x82Where it is feasible, a syllabus (headnote) will be released, as is being done in connection with this case, at the time the opinion is issued. The syllabus constitutes no part of the opinion of the Court but has been prepared by the Reporter of Decisions for the convenience of the reader. See United States v. Detroit Timber & Lumber Co., \n\n200 U.\xc2\xa0S. 321\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tSyllabus\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tNATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT BUSINESS et\xc2\xa0al. v. SEBELIUS, SECRETARY OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES, et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tcertiorari to the united states court of appeals for the eleventh circuit\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tNo. 11\xe2\x80\x93393.\xe2\x80\x83Argued March 26, 27, 28, 2012\xe2\x80\x94Decided June 28, 2012\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIn 2010, Congress enacted the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act in order to increase the number of Americans covered by health insurance and decrease the cost of health care. One key provision is the individual mandate, which requires most Americans to maintain \xe2\x80\x9cminimum essential\xe2\x80\x9d health insurance coverage. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A. For individuals who are not exempt, and who do not receive health insurance through an employer or government program, the means of satisfying the requirement is to purchase insurance from a private company. Beginning in 2014, those who do not comply with the mandate must make a \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hared responsibility payment\xe2\x80\x9d to the Federal Government. \xc2\xa75000A(b)(1). The Act provides that this \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d will be paid to the Internal Revenue Service with an individual\xe2\x80\x99s taxes, and \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collected in the same manner\xe2\x80\x9d as tax penalties. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa75000A(c), (g)(1).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAnother key provision of the Act is the Medicaid expansion. The current Medicaid program offers federal funding to States to assist pregnant women, children, needy families, the blind, the elderly, and the disabled in obtaining medical care. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396d(a). The Affordable Care Act expands the scope of the Medicaid program and increases the number of individuals the States must cover. For example, the Act requires state programs to provide Medicaid coverage by 2014 to adults with incomes up to 133 percent of the federal poverty level, whereas many States now cover adults with children only if their income is considerably lower, and do not cover childless adults at all. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(i)(VIII). The Act increases federal funding to cover the States\xe2\x80\x99 costs in expanding Medicaid coverage. \xc2\xa71396d(y)(1). But if a State does not comply with the Act\xe2\x80\x99s new coverage requirements, it may lose not only the federal funding for those requirements, but all of its federal Medicaid funds. \xc2\xa71396c.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTwenty-six States, several individuals, and the National Federation of Independent Business brought suit in Federal District Court, challenging the constitutionality of the individual mandate and the Medicaid expansion. The Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit upheld the Medicaid expansion as a valid exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s spending power, but concluded that Congress lacked authority to enact the individual mandate. Finding the mandate severable from the Act\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions, the Eleventh Circuit left the rest of the Act intact.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tHeld:\xc2\xa0The judgment is affirmed in part and reversed in part.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t648 F.\xc2\xa03d 1235, affirmed in part and reversed in part.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t1.\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Part II, concluding that the Anti-Injunction Act does not bar this suit.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Anti-Injunction Act provides that \xe2\x80\x9cno suit for the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax shall be maintained in any court by any person,\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa77421(a), so that those subject to a tax must first pay it and then sue for a refund. The present challenge seeks to restrain the collection of the shared responsibility payment from those who do not comply with the individual mandate. But Congress did not intend the payment to be treated as a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act. The Affordable Care Act describes the payment as a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty,\xe2\x80\x9d not a \xe2\x80\x9ctax.\xe2\x80\x9d That label cannot control whether the payment is a tax for purposes of the Constitution, but it does determine the application of the Anti-Injunction Act. The Anti-Injunction Act therefore does not bar this suit. Pp.\xc2\xa011\xe2\x80\x9315.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t2.\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts concluded in Part III\xe2\x80\x93A that the individual mandate is not a valid exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Commerce Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause. Pp.\xc2\xa016\xe2\x80\x9330.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(a)\xc2\xa0The Constitution grants Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa03 (emphasis added). The power to regulate commerce presupposes the existence of commercial activity to be regulated. This Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedent reflects this understanding: As expansive as this Court\xe2\x80\x99s cases construing the scope of the commerce power have been, they uniformly describe the power as reaching \xe2\x80\x9cactivity.\xe2\x80\x9d E.g., United States v. Lopez, \n\n514 U.\xc2\xa0S. 549\n. The individual mandate, however, does not regulate existing commercial activity. It instead compels individuals to become active in commerce by purchasing a product, on the ground that their failure to do so affects interstate commerce.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tConstruing the Commerce Clause to permit Congress to regulate individuals precisely because they are doing nothing would open a new and potentially vast domain to congressional authority. Congress already possesses expansive power to regulate what people do. Upholding the Affordable Care Act under the Commerce Clause would give Congress the same license to regulate what people do not do. The Framers knew the difference between doing something and doing nothing. They gave Congress the power to regulate commerce, not to compel it. Ignoring that distinction would undermine the principle that the Federal Government is a government of limited and enumerated powers. The individual mandate thus cannot be sustained under Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Pp.\xc2\xa016\xe2\x80\x9327.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(b)\xc2\xa0Nor can the individual mandate be sustained under the Necessary and Proper Clause as an integral part of the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s other reforms. Each of this Court\xe2\x80\x99s prior cases upholding laws under that Clause involved exercises of authority derivative of, and in service to, a granted power. E.g., United States v. Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___. The individual mandate, by contrast, vests Congress with the extraordinary ability to create the necessary predicate to the exercise of an enumerated power and draw within its regulatory scope those who would otherwise be outside of it. Even if the individual mandate is \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary\xe2\x80\x9d to the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s other reforms, such an expansion of federal power is not a \xe2\x80\x9cproper\xe2\x80\x9d means for making those reforms effective. Pp.\xc2\xa027\xe2\x80\x9330.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t3.\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts concluded in Part III\xe2\x80\x93B that the individual mandate must be construed as imposing a tax on those who do not have health insurance, if such a construction is reasonable.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe most straightforward reading of the individual mandate is that it commands individuals to purchase insurance. But, for the reasons explained, the Commerce Clause does not give Congress that power. It is therefore necessary to turn to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s alternative argument: that the mandate may be upheld as within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to \xe2\x80\x9clay and collect Taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. In pressing its taxing power argument, the Government asks the Court to view the mandate as imposing a tax on those who do not buy that product. Because \xe2\x80\x9cevery reasonable construction must be resorted to, in order to save a statute from unconstitutionality,\xe2\x80\x9d Hooper v. California, \n\n155 U.\xc2\xa0S. 648\n, the question is whether it is \xe2\x80\x9cfairly possible\xe2\x80\x9d to interpret the mandate as imposing such a tax, Crowell v. Benson, \n\n285 U.\xc2\xa0S. 22\n. Pp.\xc2\xa031\xe2\x80\x9332.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t4.\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Part III\xe2\x80\x93C, concluding that the individual mandate may be upheld as within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Taxing Clause. Pp.\xc2\xa033\xe2\x80\x9344.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(a)\xc2\xa0The Affordable Care Act describes the \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hared responsibility payment\xe2\x80\x9d as a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty,\xe2\x80\x9d not a \xe2\x80\x9ctax.\xe2\x80\x9d That label is fatal to the application of the Anti-Injunction Act. It does not, however, control whether an exaction is within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax. In answering that constitutional question, this Court follows a functional approach, \xe2\x80\x9c[d]isregarding the designation of the exaction, and viewing its substance and application.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Constantine, \n\n296 U.\xc2\xa0S. 287\n. Pp.\xc2\xa033\xe2\x80\x9335.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(b)\xc2\xa0Such an analysis suggests that the shared responsibility payment may for constitutional purposes be considered a tax. The payment is not so high that there is really no choice but to buy health insurance; the payment is not limited to willful violations, as penalties for unlawful acts often are; and the payment is collected solely by the IRS through the normal means of taxation. Cf. Bailey v. Drexel Furniture Co., \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 20\n\xe2\x80\x9337. None of this is to say that payment is not intended to induce the purchase of health insurance. But the mandate need not be read to declare that failing to do so is unlawful. Neither the Affordable Care Act nor any other law attaches negative legal consequences to not buying health insurance, beyond requiring a payment to the IRS. And Congress\xe2\x80\x99s choice of language\xe2\x80\x94stating that individuals \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d obtain insurance or pay a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94does not require reading \xc2\xa75000A as punishing unlawful conduct. It may also be read as imposing a tax on those who go without insurance. See New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144\n\xe2\x80\x93174. Pp.\xc2\xa035\xe2\x80\x9340.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(c)\xc2\xa0Even if the mandate may reasonably be characterized as a tax, it must still comply with the Direct Tax Clause, which provides: \xe2\x80\x9cNo Capitation, or other direct, Tax shall be laid, unless in Proportion to the Census or Enumeration herein before directed to be taken.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa79, cl.\xc2\xa04. A tax on going without health insurance is not like a capitation or other direct tax under this Court\xe2\x80\x99s precedents. It therefore need not be apportioned so that each State pays in proportion to its population. Pp.\xc2\xa040\xe2\x80\x9341.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t5.\xc2\xa0Chief Justice Roberts, joined by Justice Breyer and Justice Kagan, concluded in Part IV that the Medicaid expansion violates the Constitution by threatening States with the loss of their existing Medicaid funding if they decline to comply with the expansion. Pp.\xc2\xa045\xe2\x80\x9358.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(a)\xc2\xa0The Spending Clause grants Congress the power \xe2\x80\x9cto pay the Debts and provide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. Congress may use this power to establish cooperative state-federal Spending Clause programs. The legitimacy of Spending Clause legislation, however, depends on whether a State voluntarily and knowingly accepts the terms of such programs. Pennhurst State School and Hospital v. Halderman, \n\n451 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Constitution simply does not give Congress the authority to require the States to regulate.\xe2\x80\x9d New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144\n. When Congress threatens to terminate other grants as a means of pressuring the States to accept a Spending Clause program, the legislation runs counter to this Nation\xe2\x80\x99s system of federalism. Cf. South Dakota v. Dole, \n\n483 U.\xc2\xa0S. 203\n. Pp.\xc2\xa045\xe2\x80\x9351.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(b)\xc2\xa0Section 1396c gives the Secretary of Health and Human Services the authority to penalize States that choose not to participate in the Medicaid expansion by taking away their existing Medicaid funding. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396c. The threatened loss of over 10 percent of a State\xe2\x80\x99s overall budget is economic dragooning that leaves the States with no real option but to acquiesce in the Medicaid expansion. The Government claims that the expansion is properly viewed as only a modification of the existing program, and that this modification is permissible because Congress reserved the \xe2\x80\x9cright to alter, amend, or repeal any provision\xe2\x80\x9d of Medicaid. \xc2\xa71304. But the expansion accomplishes a shift in kind, not merely degree. The original program was designed to cover medical services for particular categories of vulnerable individuals. Under the Affordable Care Act, Medicaid is transformed into a program to meet the health care needs of the entire nonelderly population with income below 133 percent of the poverty level. A State could hardly anticipate that Congress\xe2\x80\x99s reservation of the right to \xe2\x80\x9calter\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9camend\xe2\x80\x9d the Medicaid program included the power to transform it so dramatically. The Medicaid expansion thus violates the Constitution by threatening States with the loss of their existing Medicaid funding if they decline to comply with the expansion. Pp.\xc2\xa051\xe2\x80\x9355.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t(c)\xc2\xa0The constitutional violation is fully remedied by precluding the Secretary from applying \xc2\xa71396c to withdraw existing Medicaid funds for failure to comply with the requirements set out in the expansion. See \xc2\xa71303. The other provisions of the Affordable Care Act are not affected. Congress would have wanted the rest of the Act to stand, had it known that States would have a genuine choice whether to participate in the Medicaid expansion. Pp.\xc2\xa055\xe2\x80\x9358.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t6.\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg, joined by Justice Sotomayor, is of the view that the Spending Clause does not preclude the Secretary from withholding Medicaid funds based on a State\xe2\x80\x99s refusal to comply with the expanded Medicaid program. But given the majority view, she agrees with The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s conclusion in Part IV\xe2\x80\x93B that the Medicaid Act\xe2\x80\x99s severability clause, \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71303, determines the appropriate remedy. Because The Chief Justice finds the withholding\xe2\x80\x94not the granting\xe2\x80\x94of federal funds incompatible with the Spending Clause, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 extension of Medicaid remains available to any State that affirms its willingness to participate. Even absent \xc2\xa71303\xe2\x80\x99s command, the Court would have no warrant to invalidate the funding offered by the Medicaid expansion, and surely no basis to tear down the ACA in its entirety. When a court confronts an unconstitutional statute, its endeavor must be to conserve, not destroy, the legislation. See, e.g., Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., \n\n546 U.\xc2\xa0S. 320\n\xe2\x80\x93330. Pp.\xc2\xa060\xe2\x80\x9361.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tRoberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., announced the judgment of the Court and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I, II, and III\xe2\x80\x93C, in which Ginsburg, Breyer, Sotomayor, and Kagan, JJ., joined; an opinion with respect to Part IV, in which Breyer and Kagan, JJ., joined; and an opinion with respect to Parts III\xe2\x80\x93A, III\xe2\x80\x93B, and III\xe2\x80\x93D. Ginsburg, J., filed an opinion concurring in part, concurring in the judgment in part, and dissenting in part, in which Sotomayor, J., joined, and in which Breyer and Kagan, JJ., joined as to Parts I, II, III, and IV. Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., filed a dissenting opinion. Thomas, J., filed a dissenting opinion.\n\t\t\t

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tChief Justice Roberts announced the judgment of the Court and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I, II, and III\xe2\x80\x93C, an opinion with respect to Part IV, in which Justice Breyer and Justice Kagan join, and an opinion with respect to Parts III\xe2\x80\x93A, III\xe2\x80\x93B, and III\xe2\x80\x93D.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tToday we resolve constitutional challenges to two provisions of the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act of 2010: the individual mandate, which requires individuals to purchase a health insurance policy providing a minimum level of coverage; and the Medicaid expansion, which gives funds to the States on the condition that they provide specified health care to all citizens whose income falls below a certain threshold. We do not consider whether the Act embodies sound policies. That judgment is entrusted to the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s elected leaders. We ask only whether Congress has the power under the Constitution to enact the challenged provisions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn our federal system, the National Government possesses only limited powers; the States and the people retain the remainder. Nearly two centuries ago, Chief Justice Marshall observed that \xe2\x80\x9cthe question respecting the extent of the powers actually granted\xe2\x80\x9d to the Federal Government \xe2\x80\x9cis perpetually arising, and will probably continue to arise, as long as our system shall exist.\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch v. Maryland, 4 Wheat. 316, 405 (1819). In this case we must again determine whether the Constitution grants Congress powers it now asserts, but which many States and individuals believe it does not possess. Resolving this controversy requires us to examine both the limits of the Government\xe2\x80\x99s power, and our own limited role in policing those boundaries.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Federal Government \xe2\x80\x9cis acknowledged by all to be one of enumerated powers.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. That is, rather than granting general authority to perform all the conceiv-able functions of government, the Constitution lists, or enumerates, the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s powers. Congress may, for example, \xe2\x80\x9ccoin Money,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cestablish Post Offices,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9craise and support Armies.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cls.\xc2\xa05, 7, 12. The enumeration of powers is also a limitation of pow- ers, because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he enumeration presupposes something not enumerated.\xe2\x80\x9d Gibbons v. Ogden, 9 Wheat. 1, 195 (1824). The Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s express conferral of some powers makes clear that it does not grant others. And the Federal Government \xe2\x80\x9ccan exercise only the powers granted to it.\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch, supra, at 405.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tToday, the restrictions on government power foremost in many Americans\xe2\x80\x99 minds are likely to be affirmative pro-hibitions, such as contained in the Bill of Rights. These affirmative prohibitions come into play, however, only where the Government possesses authority to act in the first place. If no enumerated power authorizes Congress to pass a certain law, that law may not be enacted, even if it would not violate any of the express prohibitions in the Bill of Rights or elsewhere in the Constitution.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIndeed, the Constitution did not initially include a Bill of Rights at least partly because the Framers felt the enu-meration of powers sufficed to restrain the Government. As Alexander Hamilton put it, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Constitution is itself, in every rational sense, and to every useful purpose, a bill of rights.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No.\xc2\xa084, p.\xc2\xa0515 (C. Ros-siter ed. 1961). And when the Bill of Rights was ratified, it made express what the enumeration of powers necessarily implied: \xe2\x80\x9cThe powers not delegated to the United States by the Constitution .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. are reserved to the States respectively, or to the people.\xe2\x80\x9d U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Amdt.\xc2\xa010. The Federal Government has expanded dramatically over the past two centuries, but it still must show that a constitutional grant of power authorizes each of its actions. See, e.g., United States v. Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___ (2010).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe same does not apply to the States, because the Con-stitution is not the source of their power. The Consti-tution may restrict state governments\xe2\x80\x94as it does, for example, by forbidding them to deny any person the equal protection of the laws. But where such prohibitions do not apply, state governments do not need constitutional au-thorization to act. The States thus can and do perform many of the vital functions of modern government\xe2\x80\x94punishing street crime, running public schools, and zoning property for development, to name but a few\xe2\x80\x94even though the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s text does not authorize any government to do so. Our cases refer to this general power of governing, possessed by the States but not by the Federal Government, as the \xe2\x80\x9cpolice power.\xe2\x80\x9d See, e.g., United States v. Morrison, \n\n529 U.\xc2\xa0S. 598\n\xe2\x80\x93619 (2000).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\xe2\x80\x9cState sovereignty is not just an end in itself: Rather, federalism secures to citizens the liberties that derive from the diffusion of sovereign power.\xe2\x80\x9d New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144, \n181 (1992)\n (internal quotation marks omitted). Because the police power is controlled by 50 different States instead of one national sovereign, the facets of governing that touch on citizens\xe2\x80\x99 daily lives are normally administered by smaller governments closer to the governed. The Framers thus ensured that powers which \xe2\x80\x9cin the ordinary course of affairs, concern the lives, liberties, and properties of the people\xe2\x80\x9d were held by governments more local and more accountable than a dis- tant federal bureaucracy. The Federalist No.\xc2\xa045, at 293 (J. Madison). The independent power of the States also serves as a check on the power of the Federal Government: \xe2\x80\x9cBy denying any one government complete jurisdiction over all the concerns of public life, federalism protects the liberty of the individual from arbitrary power.\xe2\x80\x9d Bond v. United States, 564 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___, ___ (2011) (slip op., at 9\xe2\x80\x9310).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis case concerns two powers that the Constitution does grant the Federal Government, but which must be read carefully to avoid creating a general federal authority akin to the police power. The Constitution authorizes Congress to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce with foreign Nations, and among the several States, and with the Indian Tribes.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa03. Our precedents read that to mean that Congress may regulate \xe2\x80\x9cthe channels of interstate commerce,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cpersons or things in interstate commerce,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cthose activities that substantially affect interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Morrison, supra, at 609 (internal quotation marks omitted). The power over activities that substantially affect interstate commerce can be expansive. That power has been held to authorize federal regulation of such seem-ingly local matters as a farmer\xe2\x80\x99s decision to grow wheat for himself and his livestock, and a loan shark\xe2\x80\x99s extor-tionate collections from a neighborhood butcher shop. See Wickard v. Filburn, \n\n317 U.\xc2\xa0S. 111 (1942)\n; Perez v. United States, \n\n402 U.\xc2\xa0S. 146 (1971)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress may also \xe2\x80\x9clay and collect Taxes, Duties, Imposts and Excises, to pay the Debts and provide for the common Defence and general Welfare of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. Put simply, Congress may tax and spend. This grant gives the Federal Government considerable influence even in areas where it cannot directly regulate. The Federal Government may enact a tax on an activity that it cannot authorize, forbid, or otherwise control. See, e.g., License Tax Cases, 5 Wall. 462, 471 (1867). And in exercising its spending power, Congress may offer funds to the States, and may condition those offers on compliance with specified conditions. See, e.g., College Savings Bank v. Florida Prepaid Postsecondary Ed. Expense Bd., \n\n527 U.\xc2\xa0S. 666, \n686 (1999)\n. These offers may well induce the States to adopt policies that the Federal Government itself could not impose. See, e.g., South Dakota v. Dole, \n\n483 U.\xc2\xa0S. 203\n\xe2\x80\x93206 (1987) (conditioning federal highway funds on States raising their drinking age to 21).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe reach of the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated powers is broader still because the Constitution authorizes Congress to \xe2\x80\x9cmake all Laws which shall be necessary and proper for carrying into Execution the foregoing Powers.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa018. We have long read this provision to give Congress great latitude in exercising its powers: \xe2\x80\x9cLet the end be legitimate, let it be within the scope of the constitution, and all means which are appropriate, which are plainly adapted to that end, which are not prohibited, but consist with the letter and spirit of the constitution, are constitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch, 4 Wheat., at 421.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur permissive reading of these powers is explained in part by a general reticence to invalidate the acts of the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s elected leaders. \xe2\x80\x9cProper respect for a co-ordinate branch of the government\xe2\x80\x9d requires that we strike down an Act of Congress only if \xe2\x80\x9cthe lack of constitutional authority to pass [the] act in question is clearly demonstrated.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Harris, \n\n106 U.\xc2\xa0S. 629, \n635 (1883)\n. Members of this Court are vested with the authority to interpret the law; we possess neither the expertise nor the prerogative to make policy judgments. Those decisions are entrusted to our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s elected leaders, who can be thrown out of office if the people disagree with them. It is not our job to protect the people from the consequences of their political choices.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur deference in matters of policy cannot, however, become abdication in matters of law. \xe2\x80\x9cThe powers of the legislature are defined and limited; and that those lim- its may not be mistaken, or forgotten, the constitution is written.\xe2\x80\x9d Marbury v. Madison, 1 Cranch 137, 176 (1803). Our respect for Congress\xe2\x80\x99s policy judgments thus can never extend so far as to disavow restraints on federal power that the Constitution carefully constructed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe peculiar circumstances of the moment may render a measure more or less wise, but cannot render it more or less constitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d Chief Justice John Marshall, A Friend of the Constitution No.\xc2\xa0V, Alexandria Gazette, July 5, 1819, in John Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s Defense of McCulloch v. Maryland 190\xe2\x80\x93191 (G. Gunther ed. 1969). And there can be no question that it is the responsibility of this Court to enforce the limits on federal power by striking down acts of Congress that transgress those limits. Marbury v. Madison, supra, at 175\xe2\x80\x93176.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe questions before us must be considered against the background of these basic principles.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tI\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn 2010, Congress enacted the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act, \n\n124Stat.\n119. The Act aims to increase the number of Americans covered by health in-surance and decrease the cost of health care. The Act\xe2\x80\x99s 10 titles stretch over 900 pages and contain hundreds of provisions. This case concerns constitutional challenges to two key provisions, commonly referred to as the individual mandate and the Medicaid expansion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe individual mandate requires most Americans to maintain \xe2\x80\x9cminimum essential\xe2\x80\x9d health insurance coverage. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A. The mandate does not apply to some individuals, such as prisoners and undocumented aliens. \xc2\xa75000A(d). Many individuals will receive the required cov-erage through their employer, or from a government program such as Medicaid or Medicare. See \xc2\xa75000A(f). But for individuals who are not exempt and do not receive health insurance through a third party, the means of satisfying the requirement is to purchase insurance from a private company.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBeginning in 2014, those who do not comply with the mandate must make a \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hared responsibility payment\xe2\x80\x9d to the Federal Government. \xc2\xa75000A(b)(1). That payment, which the Act describes as a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty,\xe2\x80\x9d is calculated as a percentage of household income, subject to a floor based on a specified dollar amount and a ceiling based on the average annual premium the individual would have to pay for qualifying private health insurance. \xc2\xa75000A(c). In 2016, for example, the penalty will be 2.5 percent of an individ-ual\xe2\x80\x99s household income, but no less than $695 and no more than the average yearly premium for insurance that covers 60 percent of the cost of 10 specified services (e.g., prescription drugs and hospitalization). Ibid.; \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718022. The Act provides that the penalty will be paid to the Internal Revenue Service with an individual\xe2\x80\x99s taxes, and \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collected in the same manner\xe2\x80\x9d as tax penalties, such as the penalty for claiming too large an income tax refund. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(g)(1). The Act, however, bars the IRS from using several of its normal enforcement tools, such as criminal prosecutions and levies. \xc2\xa75000A(g)(2). And some individuals who are subject to the mandate are nonetheless exempt from the penalty\xe2\x80\x94for example, those with income below a certain threshold and members of Indian tribes. \xc2\xa75000A(e).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOn the day the President signed the Act into law, Florida and 12 other States filed a complaint in the Federal District Court for the Northern District of Florida. Those plaintiffs\xe2\x80\x94who are both respondents and petitioners here, depending on the issue\xe2\x80\x94were subsequently joined by 13 more States, several individuals, and the National Fed-eration of Independent Business. The plaintiffs alleged, among other things, that the individual mandate provisions of the Act exceeded Congress\xe2\x80\x99s powers under Article I of the Constitution. The District Court agreed, holding that Congress lacked constitutional power to enact the individual mandate. 780 F.\xc2\xa0Supp. 2d 1256 (ND Fla. 2011). The District Court determined that the individual mandate could not be severed from the remainder of the Act, and therefore struck down the Act in its entirety. Id., at 1305\xe2\x80\x931306.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court affirmed the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding that the individual mandate exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power. 648 F.\xc2\xa03d 1235 (2011). The panel unanimously agreed that the individual mandate did not impose a tax, and thus could not be authorized by Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to \xe2\x80\x9clay and collect Taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. A majority also held that the individual mandate was not supported by Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. among the several States.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., cl.\xc2\xa03. According to the majority, the Commerce Clause does not empower the Federal Government to order individuals to engage in commerce, and the Government\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to cast the individual mandate in a different light were unpersuasive. Judge Marcus dissented, reasoning that the individual mandate regulates economic activity that has a clear effect on interstate commerce.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tHaving held the individual mandate to be unconstitutional, the majority examined whether that provision could be severed from the remainder of the Act. The ma-jority determined that, contrary to the District Court\xe2\x80\x99s view, it could. The court thus struck down only the individual mandate, leaving the Act\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions intact. 648 F. 3d, at 1328.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOther Courts of Appeals have also heard challenges to the individual mandate. The Sixth Circuit and the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit upheld the mandate as a valid exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s commerce power. See Thomas More Law Center v. Obama, 651 F.\xc2\xa03d 529 (CA6 2011); Seven-Sky v. Holder, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d 1 (CADC 2011). The Fourth Circuit determined that the Anti-Injunction Act prevents courts from considering the merits of that question. See Liberty Univ., Inc. v. Geithner, 671 F.\xc2\xa03d 391 (2011). That statute bars suits \xe2\x80\x9cfor the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa77421(a). A majority of the Fourth Circuit panel reasoned that the individual mandate\xe2\x80\x99s penalty is a tax within the meaning of the Anti-Injunction Act, because it is a financial assessment collected by the IRS through the normal means of taxation. The majority therefore determined that the plaintiffs could not challenge the individual mandate until after they paid the penalty.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe second provision of the Affordable Care Act directly challenged here is the Medicaid expansion. Enacted in 1965, Medicaid offers federal funding to States to assist pregnant women, children, needy families, the blind, the elderly, and the disabled in obtaining medical care. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10). In order to receive that funding, States must comply with federal criteria governing matters such as who receives care and what services are provided at what cost. By 1982 every State had chosen to participate in Medicaid. Federal funds received through the Medicaid program have become a substantial part of state budgets, now constituting over 10 percent of most States\xe2\x80\x99 total revenue.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act expands the scope of the Medicaid program and increases the number of individuals the States must cover. For example, the Act requires state programs to provide Medicaid coverage to adults with incomes up to 133 percent of the federal poverty level, whereas many States now cover adults with children only if their income is considerably lower, and do not cover childless adults at all. See \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(i)(VIII). The Act increases federal funding to cover the States\xe2\x80\x99 costs in expanding Medicaid coverage, although States will bear a portion of the costs on their own. \xc2\xa71396d(y)(1). If a State does not comply with the Act\xe2\x80\x99s new coverage requirements, it may lose not only the federal funding for those requirements, but all of its federal Medicaid funds. See \xc2\xa71396c.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAlong with their challenge to the individual mandate, the state plaintiffs in the Eleventh Circuit argued that the Medicaid expansion exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional powers. The Court of Appeals unanimously held that the Medicaid expansion is a valid exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Spending Clause. U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. And the court rejected the States\xe2\x80\x99 claim that the threatened loss of all federal Medicaid funding violates the \n \nTenth Amendment by coercing them into complying with the Medicaid expansion. 648 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 1264, 1268.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe granted certiorari to review the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit with respect to both the individual mandate and the Medicaid expansion. 565 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___ (2011). Because no party supports the Eleventh Circuit\xe2\x80\x99s holding that the individual mandate can be completely severed from the remainder of the Affordable Care Act, we appointed an amicus curiae to defend that aspect of the judgment below. And because there is a reasonable argument that the Anti-Injunction Act deprives us of jurisdiction to hear challenges to the individ-ual mandate, but no party supports that proposition, we appointed an amicus curiae to advance it.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBefore turning to the merits, we need to be sure we have the authority to do so. The Anti-Injunction Act provides that \xe2\x80\x9cno suit for the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax shall be maintained in any court by any person, whether or not such person is the per- son against whom such tax was assessed.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa77421(a). This statute protects the Government\xe2\x80\x99s ability to collect a consistent stream of revenue, by barring litigation to enjoin or otherwise obstruct the collection of taxes. Because of the Anti-Injunction Act, taxes can ordinarily be challenged only after they are paid, by suing for a refund. See Enochs v. Williams Packing & Nav. Co., \n\n370 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n\xe2\x80\x938 (1962).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe penalty for not complying with the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s individual mandate first becomes enforceable in 2014. The present challenge to the mandate thus seeks to restrain the penalty\xe2\x80\x99s future collection. Amicus contends that the Internal Revenue Code treats the penalty as a tax, and that the Anti-Injunction Act therefore bars this suit.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe text of the pertinent statutes suggests otherwise. The Anti-Injunction Act applies to suits \xe2\x80\x9cfor the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa77421(a) (emphasis added). Congress, however, chose to describe the \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hared responsibility payment\xe2\x80\x9d imposed on those who forgo health insurance not as a \xe2\x80\x9ctax,\xe2\x80\x9d but as a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa75000A(b), (g)(2). There is no immediate reason to think that a statute applying to \xe2\x80\x9cany tax\xe2\x80\x9d would apply to a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress\xe2\x80\x99s decision to label this exaction a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d rather than a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d is significant because the Affordable Care Act describes many other exactions it creates as \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes.\xe2\x80\x9d See Thomas More, 651 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 551. Where Congress uses certain language in one part of a statute and different language in another, it is generally presumed that Congress acts intentionally. See Russello v. United States, \n\n464 U.\xc2\xa0S. 16, \n23 (1983)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAmicus argues that even though Congress did not label the shared responsibility payment a tax, we should treat it as such under the Anti-Injunction Act because it functions like a tax. It is true that Congress cannot change whether an exaction is a tax or a penalty for constitutional pur-poses simply by describing it as one or the other. Congress may not, for example, expand its power under the Taxing Clause, or escape the Double Jeopardy Clause\xe2\x80\x99s constraint on criminal sanctions, by labeling a severe financial pun-ishment a \xe2\x80\x9ctax.\xe2\x80\x9d See Bailey v. Drexel Furniture Co., \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 20\n\xe2\x80\x9337 (1922); Department of Revenue of Mont. v. Kurth Ranch, \n\n511 U.\xc2\xa0S. 767, \n779 (1994)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Anti-Injunction Act and the Affordable Care Act, however, are creatures of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s own creation. How they relate to each other is up to Congress, and the best evidence of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s intent is the statutory text. We have thus applied the Anti-Injunction Act to statutorily described \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes\xe2\x80\x9d even where that label was inaccurate. See Bailey v. George, \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 16 (1922)\n (Anti-Injunction Act applies to \xe2\x80\x9cChild Labor Tax\xe2\x80\x9d struck down as exceeding Congress\xe2\x80\x99s taxing power in Drexel Furniture).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress can, of course, describe something as a penalty but direct that it nonetheless be treated as a tax for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act. For example, \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa76671(a) provides that \xe2\x80\x9cany reference in this title to \xe2\x80\x98tax\xe2\x80\x99 imposed by this title shall be deemed also to refer to the penalties and liabilities provided by\xe2\x80\x9d subchapter 68B of the Internal Revenue Code. Penalties in subchapter 68B are thus treated as taxes under Title 26, which includes the Anti-Injunction Act. The individual mandate, however, is not in subchapter 68B of the Code. Nor does any other provision state that references to taxes in Title 26 shall also be \xe2\x80\x9cdeemed\xe2\x80\x9d to apply to the individual mandate.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAmicus attempts to show that Congress did render the Anti-Injunction Act applicable to the individual mandate, albeit by a more circuitous route. Section 5000A(g)(1) spec-ifies that the penalty for not complying with the man- date \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collected in the same manner as an assessable penalty under subchapter B of chapter 68.\xe2\x80\x9d Assessable penalties in subchapter 68B, in turn, \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collected in the same manner as taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76671(a). According to amicus, by directing that the penalty be \xe2\x80\x9cassessed and collected in the same man-ner as taxes,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa75000A(g)(1) made the Anti-Injunction Act applicable to this penalty.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government disagrees. It argues that \xc2\xa75000A(g)(1) does not direct courts to apply the Anti-Injunction Act, because \xc2\xa75000A(g) is a directive only to the Secretary of the Treasury to use the same \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98methodology and procedures\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to collect the penalty that he uses to collect taxes. Brief for United States 32\xe2\x80\x9333 (quoting Seven-Sky, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 11).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe think the Government has the better reading. As it observes, \xe2\x80\x9cAssessment\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cCollection\xe2\x80\x9d are chapters of the Internal Revenue Code providing the Secretary author-ity to assess and collect taxes, and generally specifying the means by which he shall do so. See \xc2\xa76201 (assess-ment authority); \xc2\xa76301 (collection authority). Section 5000A(g)(1)\xe2\x80\x99s command that the penalty be \xe2\x80\x9cassessed and collected in the same manner\xe2\x80\x9d as taxes is best read as referring to those chapters and giving the Secretary the same authority and guidance with respect to the penalty. That interpretation is consistent with the remainder of \xc2\xa75000A(g), which instructs the Secretary on the tools he may use to collect the penalty. See \xc2\xa75000A(g)(2)(A) (barring criminal prosecutions); \xc2\xa75000A(g)(2)(B) (prohibiting the Secretary from using notices of lien and levies). The Anti-Injunction Act, by contrast, says nothing about the procedures to be used in assessing and collecting taxes.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAmicus argues in the alternative that a different section of the Internal Revenue Code requires courts to treat the penalty as a tax under the Anti-Injunction Act. Section 6201(a) authorizes the Secretary to make \xe2\x80\x9cassessments of all taxes (including interest, additional amounts, additions to the tax, and assessable penalties).\xe2\x80\x9d (Emphasis added.) Amicus contends that the penalty must be a tax, because it is an assessable penalty and \xc2\xa76201(a) says that taxes include assessable penalties.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat argument has force only if \xc2\xa76201(a) is read in isolation. The Code contains many provisions treating taxes and assessable penalties as distinct terms. See, e.g., \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7860(h)(1), 6324A(a), 6601(e)(1)\xe2\x80\x93(2), 6602, 7122(b). There would, for example, be no need for \xc2\xa76671(a) to deem \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d to refer to certain assessable penalties if the Code al- ready included all such penalties in the term \xe2\x80\x9ctax.\xe2\x80\x9d Indeed, amicus\xe2\x80\x99s earlier observation that the Code requires assessable penalties to be assessed and collected \xe2\x80\x9cin the same manner as taxes\xe2\x80\x9d makes little sense if assessable penalties are themselves taxes. In light of the Code\xe2\x80\x99s consistent distinction between the terms \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cassessable penalty,\xe2\x80\x9d we must accept the Government\xe2\x80\x99s in-terpretation: \xc2\xa76201(a) instructs the Secretary that his authority to assess taxes includes the authority to assess penalties, but it does not equate assessable penalties to taxes for other purposes.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act does not require that the penalty for failing to comply with the individual mandate be treated as a tax for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act. The Anti-Injunction Act therefore does not apply to this suit, and we may proceed to the merits.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government advances two theories for the proposition that Congress had constitutional authority to enact the individual mandate. First, the Government argues that Congress had the power to enact the mandate under the Commerce Clause. Under that theory, Congress may order individuals to buy health insurance because the failure to do so affects interstate commerce, and could un-dercut the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s other reforms. Second, the Government argues that if the commerce power does not support the mandate, we should nonetheless uphold it as an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax. According to the Government, even if Congress lacks the power to direct individuals to buy insurance, the only effect of the individual mandate is to raise taxes on those who do not do so, and thus the law may be upheld as a tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government\xe2\x80\x99s first argument is that the individual mandate is a valid exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Commerce Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause. According to the Government, the health care market is characterized by a significant cost-shifting problem. Everyone will eventually need health care at a time and to an extent they cannot predict, but if they do not have insurance, they often will not be able to pay for it. Because state and federal laws nonetheless require hospitals to provide a certain degree of care to individuals without regard to their ability to pay, see, e.g., \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71395dd; Fla. Stat. Ann. \xc2\xa7395.1041, hospitals end up receiving compensation for only a portion of the services they provide. To recoup the losses, hospitals pass on the cost to insurers through higher rates, and insurers, in turn, pass on the cost to policy holders in the form of higher premiums. Congress estimated that the cost of uncompensated care raises family health insurance premiums, on average, by over $1,000 per year. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(F).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the Affordable Care Act, Congress addressed the problem of those who cannot obtain insurance coverage because of preexisting conditions or other health issues. It did so through the Act\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cguaranteed-issue\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ccommunity- rating\xe2\x80\x9d provisions. These provisions together prohibit insurance companies from denying coverage to those with such conditions or charging unhealthy individuals higher premiums than healthy individuals. See \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg, 300gg\xe2\x80\x931, 300gg\xe2\x80\x933, 300gg\xe2\x80\x934.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe guaranteed-issue and community-rating reforms do not, however, address the issue of healthy individuals who choose not to purchase insurance to cover potential health care needs. In fact, the reforms sharply exacerbate that problem, by providing an incentive for individuals to delay purchasing health insurance until they become sick, relying on the promise of guaranteed and affordable coverage. The reforms also threaten to impose massive new costs on insurers, who are required to accept unhealthy individuals but prohibited from charging them rates necessary to pay for their coverage. This will lead insurers to significantly increase premiums on everyone. See Brief for America\xe2\x80\x99s Health Insurance Plans et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93393 etc. 8\xe2\x80\x939.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe individual mandate was Congress\xe2\x80\x99s solution to these problems. By requiring that individuals purchase health insurance, the mandate prevents cost-shifting by those who would otherwise go without it. In addition, the mandate forces into the insurance risk pool more healthy individuals, whose premiums on average will be higher than their health care expenses. This allows insurers to subsidize the costs of covering the unhealthy individuals the reforms require them to accept. The Government claims that Congress has power under the Commerce and Necessary and Proper Clauses to enact this solution.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government contends that the individual mandate is within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power because the failure to pur-chase insurance \xe2\x80\x9chas a substantial and deleterious effect on interstate commerce\xe2\x80\x9d by creating the cost-shifting prob-lem. Brief for United States 34. The path of our Commerce Clause decisions has not always run smooth, see United States v. Lopez, \n\n514 U.\xc2\xa0S. 549\n\xe2\x80\x93559 (1995), but it is now well established that Congress has broad authority under the Clause. We have recognized, for example, that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he power of Congress over interstate commerce is not confined to the regulation of commerce among the states,\xe2\x80\x9d but extends to activities that \xe2\x80\x9chave a substantial effect on interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Darby, \n\n312 U.\xc2\xa0S. 100\n\xe2\x80\x93119 (1941). Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power, more-over, is not limited to regulation of an activity that by itself substantially affects interstate commerce, but also extends to activities that do so only when aggregated with similar activities of others. See Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 127\xe2\x80\x93128.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tGiven its expansive scope, it is no surprise that Congress has employed the commerce power in a wide variety of ways to address the pressing needs of the time. But Congress has never attempted to rely on that power to compel individuals not engaged in commerce to purchase an unwanted product.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Legislative novelty is not nec-essarily fatal; there is a first time for everything. But sometimes \xe2\x80\x9cthe most telling indication of [a] severe con-stitutional problem .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. is the lack of historical precedent\xe2\x80\x9d for Congress\xe2\x80\x99s action. Free Enterprise Fund v. Public Company Accounting Oversight Bd., 561 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___, ___ (2010) (slip op., at 25) (internal quotation marks omitted). At the very least, we should \xe2\x80\x9cpause to consider the implications of the Government\xe2\x80\x99s arguments\xe2\x80\x9d when confronted with such new conceptions of federal power. Lopez, supra, at 564.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Constitution grants Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa03 (emphasis added). The power to regulate commerce presupposes the existence of commercial activity to be regulated. If the power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate\xe2\x80\x9d something included the power to create it, many of the provisions in the Constitution would be superfluous. For example, the Constitution gives Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x9ccoin Money,\xe2\x80\x9d in addition to the power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate the Value thereof.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., cl.\xc2\xa05. And it gives Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x9craise and support Armies\xe2\x80\x9d and to \xe2\x80\x9cprovide and maintain a Navy,\xe2\x80\x9d in addition to the power to \xe2\x80\x9cmake Rules for the Government and Regulation of the land and naval Forces.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., cls.\xc2\xa012\xe2\x80\x9314. If the power to regulate the armed forces or the value of money included the power to bring the subject of the regulation into existence, the specific grant of such powers would have been unnecessary. The language of the Constitution reflects the natural understanding that the power to regulate assumes there is already something to be regulated. See Gibbons, 9 Wheat., at 188 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he enlightened patriots who framed our constitution, and the people who adopted it, must be understood to have employed words in their natural sense, and to have intended what they have said\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur precedent also reflects this understanding. As expansive as our cases construing the scope of the commerce power have been, they all have one thing in common: They uniformly describe the power as reaching \xe2\x80\x9cactivity.\xe2\x80\x9d It is nearly impossible to avoid the word when quoting them. See, e.g., Lopez, supra, at 560 (\xe2\x80\x9cWhere economic activity substantially affects interstate commerce, legislation regulating that activity will be sustained\xe2\x80\x9d); Perez, 402 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 154 (\xe2\x80\x9cWhere the class of activities is regulated and that class is within the reach of federal power, the courts have no power to excise, as trivial, individual instances of the class\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original; internal quotation marks omitted)); Wickard, supra, at 125 (\xe2\x80\x9c[E]ven if appellee\xe2\x80\x99s activity be local and though it may not be regarded as commerce, it may still, whatever its nature, be reached by Congress if it exerts a substantial economic effect on interstate commerce\xe2\x80\x9d); NLRB v. Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp., \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n37 (1937)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cAlthough activities may be intrastate in character when separately considered, if they have such a close and substantial relation to interstate commerce that their control is essential or appropriate to protect that commerce from burdens and obstructions, Congress cannot be denied the power to exercise that control\xe2\x80\x9d); see also post, at 15, 25\xe2\x80\x9326, 28, 32 (Ginsburg, J., concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe individual mandate, however, does not regulate existing commercial activity. It instead compels individ-uals to become active in commerce by purchasing a product, on the ground that their failure to do so affects interstate commerce. Construing the Commerce Clause to permit Con-gress to regulate individuals precisely because they are doing nothing would open a new and potentially vast do-main to congressional authority. Every day individuals do not do an infinite number of things. In some cases they decide not to do something; in others they simply fail to do it. Allowing Congress to justify federal regulation by pointing to the effect of inaction on commerce would bring countless decisions an individual could potentially make within the scope of federal regulation, and\xe2\x80\x94under the Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory\xe2\x80\x94empower Congress to make those decisions for him.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tApplying the Government\xe2\x80\x99s logic to the familiar case of Wickard v. Filburn shows how far that logic would carry us from the notion of a government of limited powers. In Wickard, the Court famously upheld a federal penalty im-posed on a farmer for growing wheat for consumption on his own farm. 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 114\xe2\x80\x93115, 128\xe2\x80\x93129. That amount of wheat caused the farmer to exceed his quota under a program designed to support the price of wheat by limiting supply. The Court rejected the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s argument that growing wheat for home consumption was beyond the reach of the commerce power. It did so on the ground that the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s decision to grow wheat for his own use allowed him to avoid purchasing wheat in the market. That decision, when considered in the aggregate along with sim-ilar decisions of others, would have had a substantial ef-fect on the interstate market for wheat. Id., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWickard has long been regarded as \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps the most far reaching example of Commerce Clause authority over intrastate activity,\xe2\x80\x9d Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 560, but the Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory in this case would go much further. Under Wickard it is within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to regulate the market for wheat by supporting its price. But price can be supported by increasing demand as well as by decreasing supply. The aggregated decisions of some consumers not to purchase wheat have a substantial effect on the price of wheat, just as decisions not to purchase health insurance have on the price of insurance. Congress can therefore command that those not buying wheat do so, just as it argues here that it may command that those not buying health insurance do so. The farmer in Wickard was at least actively engaged in the production of wheat, and the Government could regulate that activity because of its effect on commerce. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory here would effectively override that limitation, by establishing that individuals may be regulated under the Commerce Clause whenever enough of them are not doing something the Government would have them do.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIndeed, the Government\xe2\x80\x99s logic would justify a mandatory purchase to solve almost any problem. See Seven-Sky, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 14\xe2\x80\x9315 (noting the Government\xe2\x80\x99s inability to \xe2\x80\x9cidentify any mandate to purchase a product or ser- vice in interstate commerce that would be unconstitu-tional\xe2\x80\x9d under its theory of the commerce power). To consider a different example in the health care market, many Americans do not eat a balanced diet. That group makes up a larger percentage of the total population than those without health insurance. See, e.g., Dept. of Agriculture and Dept. of Health and Human Services, Dietary Guidelines for Americans 1 (2010). The failure of that group to have a healthy diet increases health care costs, to a greater extent than the failure of the uninsured to purchase insurance. See, e.g., Finkelstein, Trogdon, Cohen, & Dietz, Annual Medical Spending Attributable to Obesity: Payer- and Service-Specific Estimates, 28 Health Affairs w822 (2009) (detailing the \xe2\x80\x9cundeniable link between ris-ing rates of obesity and rising medical spending,\xe2\x80\x9d and esti-mating that \xe2\x80\x9cthe annual medical burden of obesity has risen to almost 10 percent of all medical spending and could amount to $147 billion per year in 2008\xe2\x80\x9d). Those in-creased costs are borne in part by other Americans who must pay more, just as the uninsured shift costs to the insured. See Center for Applied Ethics, Voluntary Health Risks: Who Should Pay?, 6 Issues in Ethics 6 (1993) (noting \xe2\x80\x9coverwhelming evidence that individuals with unhealthy habits pay only a fraction of the costs associated with their behaviors; most of the expense is borne by the rest of society in the form of higher insurance premiums, government expenditures for health care, and disability benefits\xe2\x80\x9d). Congress addressed the insurance problem by ordering everyone to buy insurance. Under the Gov-ernment\xe2\x80\x99s theory, Congress could address the diet problem by ordering everyone to buy vegetables. See Dietary Guidelines, supra, at 19 (\xe2\x80\x9cImproved nutrition, appropriate eating behaviors, and increased physical activity have tre-mendous potential to .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. reduce health care costs\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tPeople, for reasons of their own, often fail to do things that would be good for them or good for society. Those failures\xe2\x80\x94joined with the similar failures of others\xe2\x80\x94can readily have a substantial effect on interstate commerce. Under the Government\xe2\x80\x99s logic, that authorizes Congress to use its commerce power to compel citizens to act as the Government would have them act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat is not the country the Framers of our Constitution envisioned. James Madison explained that the Commerce Clause was \xe2\x80\x9can addition which few oppose and from which no apprehensions are entertained.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No.\xc2\xa045, at 293. While Congress\xe2\x80\x99s authority under the Commerce Clause has of course expanded with the growth of the national economy, our cases have \xe2\x80\x9calways recognized that the power to regulate commerce, though broad indeed, has limits.\xe2\x80\x9d Maryland v. Wirtz, \n\n392 U.\xc2\xa0S. 183, \n196 (1968)\n. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory would erode those limits, permitting Congress to reach beyond the natural extent of its authority, \xe2\x80\x9ceverywhere extending the sphere of its activity and drawing all power into its impetuous vortex.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No.\xc2\xa048, at 309 (J. Madison). Congress already enjoys vast power to regulate much of what we do. Accepting the Government\xe2\x80\x99s theory would give Congress the same license to regulate what we do not do, fundamentally changing the relation between the citizen and the Federal Government.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTo an economist, perhaps, there is no difference between activity and inactivity; both have measurable economic effects on commerce. But the distinction between doing something and doing nothing would not have been lost on the Framers, who were \xe2\x80\x9cpractical statesmen,\xe2\x80\x9d not metaphysical philosophers. Industrial Union Dept., AFL\xe2\x80\x93CIO v. American Petroleum Institute, \n\n448 U.\xc2\xa0S. 607, \n673 (1980)\n (Rehnquist, J., concurring in judgment). As we have ex-plained, \xe2\x80\x9cthe framers of the Constitution were not mere visionaries, toying with speculations or theories, but practical men, dealing with the facts of political life as they understood them, putting into form the government they were creating, and prescribing in language clear and intelligible the powers that government was to take.\xe2\x80\x9d South Carolina v. United States, \n\n199 U.\xc2\xa0S. 437, \n449 (1905)\n. The Framers gave Congress the power to regulate commerce, not to compel it, and for over 200 years both our decisions and Congress\xe2\x80\x99s actions have reflected this understanding. There is no reason to depart from that understanding now.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government sees things differently. It argues that because sickness and injury are unpredictable but unavoidable, \xe2\x80\x9cthe uninsured as a class are active in the market for health care, which they regularly seek and obtain.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for United States 50. The individual mandate \xe2\x80\x9cmerely regulates how individuals finance and pay for that active participation\xe2\x80\x94requiring that they do so through insurance, rather than through attempted self-insurance with the back-stop of shifting costs to others.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government repeats the phrase \xe2\x80\x9cactive in the market for health care\xe2\x80\x9d throughout its brief, see id., at 7, 18, 34, 50, but that concept has no constitutional significance. An individual who bought a car two years ago and may buy another in the future is not \xe2\x80\x9cactive in the car market\xe2\x80\x9d in any pertinent sense. The phrase \xe2\x80\x9cactive in the market\xe2\x80\x9d cannot obscure the fact that most of those regulated by the individual mandate are not currently engaged in any commercial activity involving health care, and that fact is fatal to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s effort to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate the uninsured as a class.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 42. Our precedents recognize Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to regulate \xe2\x80\x9cclass[es] of activities,\xe2\x80\x9d Gonzales v. Raich, \n\n545 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n17 (2005)\n (emphasis added), not classes of individuals, apart from any activity in which they are engaged, see, e.g., Perez, 402 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 153 (\xe2\x80\x9cPetitioner is clearly a member of the class which engages in \xe2\x80\x98extortionate credit transactions\xe2\x80\x99 .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis deleted)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe individual mandate\xe2\x80\x99s regulation of the uninsured as a class is, in fact, particularly divorced from any link to existing commercial activity. The mandate primarily affects healthy, often young adults who are less likely to need significant health care and have other priorities for spending their money. It is precisely because these individuals, as an actuarial class, incur relatively low health care costs that the mandate helps counter the effect of forcing insurance companies to cover others who impose greater costs than their premiums are allowed to reflect. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(I) (recognizing that the mandate would \xe2\x80\x9cbroaden the health insurance risk pool to include healthy individuals, which will lower health insurance premiums\xe2\x80\x9d). If the individual mandate is targeted at a class, it is a class whose commercial inactivity rather than activity is its defining feature.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government, however, claims that this does not matter. The Government regards it as sufficient to trigger Congress\xe2\x80\x99s authority that almost all those who are uninsured will, at some unknown point in the future, engage in a health care transaction. Asserting that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]here is no temporal limitation in the Commerce Clause,\xe2\x80\x9d the Government argues that because \xe2\x80\x9c[e]veryone subject to this regulation is in or will be in the health care market,\xe2\x80\x9d they can be \xe2\x80\x9cregulated in advance.\xe2\x80\x9d Tr. of Oral Arg. 109 (Mar. 27, 2012).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe proposition that Congress may dictate the conduct of an individual today because of prophesied future ac-tivity finds no support in our precedent. We have said that Congress can anticipate the effects on commerce of an eco-nomic activity. See, e.g., Consolidated Edison Co. v. NLRB, \n\n305 U.\xc2\xa0S. 197 (1938)\n (regulating the labor practices of utility companies); Heart of Atlanta Motel, Inc. v. United States, \n\n379 U.\xc2\xa0S. 241 (1964)\n (prohibiting discrimination by hotel operators); Katzenbach v. McClung, \n\n379 U.\xc2\xa0S. 294 (1964)\n (prohibiting discrimination by restaurant owners). But we have never permitted Congress to anticipate that activity itself in order to regulate individuals not currently engaged in commerce. Each one of our cases, including those cited by Justice Ginsburg, post, at 20\xe2\x80\x9321, involved preexisting economic activity. See, e.g., Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129 (producing wheat); Raich, supra, at 25 (growing marijuana).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tEveryone will likely participate in the markets for food, clothing, transportation, shelter, or energy; that does not authorize Congress to direct them to purchase particular products in those or other markets today. The Commerce Clause is not a general license to regulate an individual from cradle to grave, simply because he will predictably engage in particular transactions. Any police power to regulate individuals as such, as opposed to their activities, remains vested in the States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government argues that the individual mandate can be sustained as a sort of exception to this rule, because health insurance is a unique product. According to the Government, upholding the individual mandate would not justify mandatory purchases of items such as cars or broccoli because, as the Government puts it, \xe2\x80\x9c[h]ealth in-surance is not purchased for its own sake like a car or broccoli; it is a means of financing health-care consumption and covering universal risks.\xe2\x80\x9d Reply Brief for United States 19. But cars and broccoli are no more purchased for their \xe2\x80\x9cown sake\xe2\x80\x9d than health insurance. They are purchased to cover the need for transportation and food.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government says that health insurance and health care financing are \xe2\x80\x9cinherently integrated.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for United States 41. But that does not mean the compelled purchase of the first is properly regarded as a regulation of the second. No matter how \xe2\x80\x9cinherently integrated\xe2\x80\x9d health insurance and health care consumption may be, they are not the same thing: They involve different transactions, entered into at different times, with different providers. And for most of those targeted by the mandate, significant health care needs will be years, or even decades, away. The proximity and degree of connection between the mandate and the subsequent commercial activity is too lack-ing to justify an exception of the sort urged by the Gov- ernment. The individual mandate forces individuals into commerce precisely because they elected to refrain from commercial activity. Such a law cannot be sus- tained under a clause authorizing Congress to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government next contends that Congress has the power under the Necessary and Proper Clause to enact the individual mandate because the mandate is an \xe2\x80\x9cintegral part of a comprehensive scheme of economic regulation\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the guaranteed-issue and community-rating insurance reforms. Brief for United States 24. Under this argument, it is not necessary to consider the effect that an individual\xe2\x80\x99s inactivity may have on interstate commerce; it is enough that Congress regulate commercial activity in a way that requires regulation of inactivity to be effective.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe power to \xe2\x80\x9cmake all Laws which shall be necessary and proper for carrying into Execution\xe2\x80\x9d the powers enumerated in the Constitution, Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa018, vests Congress with authority to enact provisions \xe2\x80\x9cincidental to the [enumerated] power, and conducive to its beneficial exercise,\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch, 4 Wheat., at 418. Although the Clause gives Congress authority to \xe2\x80\x9clegislate on that vast mass of incidental powers which must be involved in the con-stitution,\xe2\x80\x9d it does not license the exercise of any \xe2\x80\x9cgreat substantive and independent power[s]\xe2\x80\x9d beyond those specifi-cally enumerated. Id., at 411, 421. Instead, the Clause is \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98merely a declaration, for the removal of all uncertainty, that the means of carrying into execution those [powers] otherwise granted are included in the grant.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Kinsella v. United States ex\xc2\xa0rel. Singleton, \n\n361 U.\xc2\xa0S. 234, \n247 (1960)\n (quoting VI Writings of James Madison 383 (G. Hunt ed. 1906)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs our jurisprudence under the Necessary and Proper Clause has developed, we have been very deferential to Congress\xe2\x80\x99s determination that a regulation is \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary.\xe2\x80\x9d We have thus upheld laws that are \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98convenient, or use-ful\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98conducive\xe2\x80\x99 to the authority\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98beneficial exercise.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S., at ___ (slip op., at 5) (quoting McCulloch, supra, at 413, 418). But we have also carried out our responsibility to declare unconstitutional those laws that undermine the structure of government established by the Constitution. Such laws, which are not \xe2\x80\x9cconsist[ent] with the letter and spirit of the constitution,\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch, supra, at 421, are not \xe2\x80\x9cproper [means] for carrying into Execution\xe2\x80\x9d Congress\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated powers. Rather, they are, \xe2\x80\x9cin the words of The Federalist, \xe2\x80\x98merely acts of usurpation\xe2\x80\x99 which \xe2\x80\x98deserve to be treated as such.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Printz v. United States, \n\n521 U.\xc2\xa0S. 898, \n924 (1997)\n (alterations omitted) (quoting The Federalist No.\xc2\xa033, at 204 (A. Hamilton)); see also New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 177; Comstock, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 5) (Kennedy, J., concurring in judgment) (\xe2\x80\x9cIt is of fundamental importance to consider whether essential attributes of state sovereignty are compromised by the assertion of federal power under the Necessary and Proper Clause .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n
