\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tApplying these principles, the individual mandate cannot be sustained under the Necessary and Proper Clause as an essential component of the insurance reforms. Each of our prior cases upholding laws under that Clause involved exercises of authority derivative of, and in service to, a granted power. For example, we have upheld provisions permitting continued confinement of those already in federal custody when they could not be safely released, Comstock, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 1\xe2\x80\x932); criminaliz- ing bribes involving organizations receiving federal funds, Sabri v. United States, \n\n541 U.\xc2\xa0S. 600, \n602, \n605 (2004)\n; and tolling state statutes of limitations while cases are pending in federal court, Jinks v. Richland County, \n\n538 U.\xc2\xa0S. 456, \n459, \n462 (2003)\n. The individual mandate, by con-trast, vests Congress with the extraordinary ability to create the necessary predicate to the exercise of an enumerated power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis is in no way an authority that is \xe2\x80\x9cnarrow in scope,\xe2\x80\x9d Comstock, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 20), or \xe2\x80\x9cincidental\xe2\x80\x9d to the exercise of the commerce power, McCulloch, supra, at 418. Rather, such a conception of the Necessary and Proper Clause would work a substantial expansion of federal authority. No longer would Congress be limited to regulating under the Commerce Clause those who by some preexisting activity bring themselves within the sphere of federal regulation. Instead, Congress could reach beyond the natural limit of its authority and draw within its regulatory scope those who otherwise would be outside of it. Even if the individual mandate is \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary\xe2\x80\x9d to the Act\xe2\x80\x99s insurance reforms, such an expansion of federal power is not a \xe2\x80\x9cproper\xe2\x80\x9d means for making those reforms effective.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government relies primarily on our decision in Gonzales v. Raich. In Raich, we considered \xe2\x80\x9ccomprehensive legislation to regulate the interstate market\xe2\x80\x9d in marijuana. 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 22. Certain individuals sought an exemption from that regulation on the ground that they engaged in only intrastate possession and consumption. We denied any exemption, on the ground that marijuana is a fungible commodity, so that any marijuana could be readily diverted into the interstate market. Congress\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to regulate the interstate market for marijuana would therefore have been substantially undercut if it could not also regulate intrastate possession and consumption. Id., at 19. Accordingly, we recognized that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress was acting well within its authority\xe2\x80\x9d under the Necessary and Proper Clause even though its \xe2\x80\x9cregulation ensnare[d] some purely intrastate activity.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 22; see also Perez, 402 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 154. Raich thus did not involve the exercise of any \xe2\x80\x9cgreat substantive and independent power,\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch, supra, at 411, of the sort at issue here. Instead, it concerned only the constitutionality of \xe2\x80\x9cindividual applications of a concededly valid statutory scheme.\xe2\x80\x9d Raich, supra, at 23 (emphasis added).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJust as the individual mandate cannot be sustained as a law regulating the substantial effects of the failure to purchase health insurance, neither can it be upheld as a \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary and proper\xe2\x80\x9d component of the insurance re-forms. The commerce power thus does not authorize the mandate. Accord, post, at 4\xe2\x80\x9316 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat is not the end of the matter. Because the Commerce Clause does not support the individual mandate, it is necessary to turn to the Government\xe2\x80\x99s second argument: that the mandate may be upheld as within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated power to \xe2\x80\x9clay and collect Taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government\xe2\x80\x99s tax power argument asks us to view the statute differently than we did in considering its commerce power theory. In making its Commerce Clause argument, the Government defended the mandate as a regulation requiring individuals to purchase health in-surance. The Government does not claim that the taxing power allows Congress to issue such a command. Instead, the Government asks us to read the mandate not as ordering individuals to buy insurance, but rather as imposing a tax on those who do not buy that product.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe text of a statute can sometimes have more than one possible meaning. To take a familiar example, a law that reads \xe2\x80\x9cno vehicles in the park\xe2\x80\x9d might, or might not, ban bicycles in the park. And it is well established that if a statute has two possible meanings, one of which violates the Constitution, courts should adopt the meaning that does not do so. Justice Story said that 180 years ago: \xe2\x80\x9cNo court ought, unless the terms of an act rendered it unavoidable, to give a construction to it which should involve a violation, however unintentional, of the constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Parsons v. Bedford, 3 Pet. 433, 448\xe2\x80\x93449 (1830). Justice Holmes made the same point a century later: \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he rule is settled that as between two possible interpretations of a statute, by one of which it would be unconstitutional and by the other valid, our plain duty is to adopt that which will save the Act.\xe2\x80\x9d Blodgett v. Holden, \n\n275 U.\xc2\xa0S. 142, \n148 (1927)\n (concurring opinion).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe most straightforward reading of the mandate is that it commands individuals to purchase insurance. After all, it states that individuals \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d maintain health insurance. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(a). Congress thought it could enact such a command under the Commerce Clause, and the Government primarily defended the law on that basis. But, for the reasons explained above, the Commerce Clause does not give Congress that power. Under our precedent, it is therefore necessary to ask whether the Government\xe2\x80\x99s alternative reading of the statute\xe2\x80\x94that it only imposes a tax on those without insurance\xe2\x80\x94is a reasonable one.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tUnder the mandate, if an individual does not maintain health insurance, the only consequence is that he must make an additional payment to the IRS when he pays his taxes. See \xc2\xa75000A(b). That, according to the Government, means the mandate can be regarded as establishing a condition\xe2\x80\x94not owning health insurance\xe2\x80\x94that triggers a tax\xe2\x80\x94the required payment to the IRS. Under that theory, the mandate is not a legal command to buy insurance. Rather, it makes going without insurance just another thing the Government taxes, like buying gasoline or earning income. And if the mandate is in effect just a tax hike on certain taxpayers who do not have health insurance, it may be within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional power to tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t The question is not whether that is the most natural interpretation of the mandate, but only whether it is a \xe2\x80\x9cfairly possible\xe2\x80\x9d one. Crowell v. Benson, \n\n285 U.\xc2\xa0S. 22, \n62 (1932)\n. As we have explained, \xe2\x80\x9cevery reasonable construction must be resorted to, in order to save a statute from unconstitutionality.\xe2\x80\x9d Hooper v. California, \n\n155 U.\xc2\xa0S. 648, \n657 (1895)\n. The Government asks us to interpret the mandate as imposing a tax, if it would otherwise violate the Constitution. Granting the Act the full measure of deference owed to federal statutes, it can be so read, for the reasons set forth below.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe exaction the Affordable Care Act imposes on those without health insurance looks like a tax in many respects. The \xe2\x80\x9c[s]hared responsibility payment,\xe2\x80\x9d as the statute entitles it, is paid into the Treasury by \xe2\x80\x9ctaxpayer[s]\xe2\x80\x9d when they file their tax returns. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(b). It does not apply to individuals who do not pay federal income taxes because their household income is less than the filing threshold in the Internal Revenue Code. \xc2\xa75000A(e)(2). For taxpayers who do owe the payment, its amount is determined by such familiar factors as taxable income, number of dependents, and joint filing status. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa75000A(b)(3), (c)(2), (c)(4). The requirement to pay is found in the Internal Revenue Code and enforced by the IRS, which\xe2\x80\x94as we previously explained\xe2\x80\x94must assess and collect it \xe2\x80\x9cin the same manner as taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d Supra, at 13\xe2\x80\x9314. This process yields the essential feature of any tax: it produces at least some revenue for the Government. United States v. Kahriger, \n\n345 U.\xc2\xa0S. 22\n, n.\xc2\xa04 (1953). Indeed, the payment is expected to raise about $4 billion per year by 2017. Congressional Budget Office, Payments of Penalties for Being Uninsured Under the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act (Apr. 30, 2010), in Selected CBO Publications Related to Health Care Legislation, 2009\xe2\x80\x932010, p.\xc2\xa071 (rev. 2010).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIt is of course true that the Act describes the payment as a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty,\xe2\x80\x9d not a \xe2\x80\x9ctax.\xe2\x80\x9d But while that label is fatal to the application of the Anti-Injunction Act, supra, at 12\xe2\x80\x9313, it does not determine whether the payment may be viewed as an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s taxing power. It is up to Con-gress whether to apply the Anti-Injunction Act to any particular statute, so it makes sense to be guided by Congress\xe2\x80\x99s choice of label on that question. That choice does not, however, control whether an exaction is within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional power to tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur precedent reflects this: In 1922, we decided two challenges to the \xe2\x80\x9cChild Labor Tax\xe2\x80\x9d on the same day. In the first, we held that a suit to enjoin collection of the so-called tax was barred by the Anti-Injunction Act. George, 259 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 20. Congress knew that suits to obstruct taxes had to await payment under the Anti-Injunction Act; Congress called the child labor tax a tax; Congress therefore intended the Anti-Injunction Act to apply. In the second case, however, we held that the same exaction, although labeled a tax, was not in fact authorized by Con-gress\xe2\x80\x99s taxing power. Drexel Furniture, 259 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 38. That constitutional question was not controlled by Congress\xe2\x80\x99s choice of label.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe have similarly held that exactions not labeled taxes nonetheless were authorized by Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax. In the License Tax Cases, for example, we held that federal licenses to sell liquor and lottery tickets\xe2\x80\x94for which the licensee had to pay a fee\xe2\x80\x94could be sustained as exercises of the taxing power. 5 Wall., at 471. And in New York v. United States we upheld as a tax a \xe2\x80\x9csurcharge\xe2\x80\x9d on out-of-state nuclear waste shipments, a portion of which was paid to the Federal Treasury. 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 171. We thus ask whether the shared responsibility payment falls within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s taxing power, \xe2\x80\x9c[d]isregarding the designa-tion of the exaction, and viewing its substance and application.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Constantine, \n\n296 U.\xc2\xa0S. 287, \n294 (1935)\n; cf. Quill Corp. v. North Dakota, \n\n504 U.\xc2\xa0S. 298, \n310 (1992)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[M]agic words or labels\xe2\x80\x9d should not \xe2\x80\x9cdisable an otherwise constitutional levy\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)); Nelson v. Sears, Roebuck & Co., \n\n312 U.\xc2\xa0S. 359, \n363 (1941)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cIn passing on the constitutionality of a tax law, we are concerned only with its practical operation, not its definition or the precise form of descriptive words which may be applied to it\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)); United States v. Sotelo, \n\n436 U.\xc2\xa0S. 268, \n275 (1978)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cThat the funds due are referred to as a \xe2\x80\x98penalty\xe2\x80\x99 .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. does not alter their essential character as taxes\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur cases confirm this functional approach. For example, in Drexel Furniture, we focused on three practical characteristics of the so-called tax on employing child laborers that convinced us the \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d was actually a penalty. First, the tax imposed an exceedingly heavy burden\xe2\x80\x9410 percent of a company\xe2\x80\x99s net income\xe2\x80\x94on those who employed children, no matter how small their infraction. Second, it imposed that exaction only on those who knowingly employed underage laborers. Such scienter require-ments are typical of punitive statutes, because Congress often wishes to punish only those who intentionally break the law. Third, this \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d was enforced in part by the Department of Labor, an agency responsible for pun-ishing violations of labor laws, not collecting revenue. 259 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 36\xe2\x80\x9337; see also, e.g., Kurth Ranch, 511 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 780\xe2\x80\x93782 (considering, inter alia, the amount of the exaction, and the fact that it was imposed for violation of a separate criminal law); Constantine, supra, at 295 (same).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe same analysis here suggests that the shared responsibility payment may for constitutional purposes be considered a tax, not a penalty: First, for most Americans the amount due will be far less than the price of insurance, and, by statute, it can never be more.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t It may often be a reasonable financial decision to make the payment rather than purchase insurance, unlike the \xe2\x80\x9cprohibitory\xe2\x80\x9d financial punishment in Drexel Furniture. 259 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 37. Second, the individual mandate contains no scienter requirement. Third, the payment is collected solely by the IRS through the normal means of taxation\xe2\x80\x94except that the Service is not allowed to use those means most suggestive of a punitive sanction, such as criminal prosecution. See \xc2\xa75000A(g)(2). The reasons the Court in Drexel Furniture held that what was called a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d there was a penalty support the conclusion that what is called a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d here may be viewed as a tax.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNone of this is to say that the payment is not intended to affect individual conduct. Although the payment will raise considerable revenue, it is plainly designed to expand health insurance coverage. But taxes that seek to influence conduct are nothing new. Some of our earliest federal taxes sought to deter the purchase of imported manufactured goods in order to foster the growth of domestic industry. See W. Brownlee, Federal Taxation in America 22 (2d ed. 2004); cf. 2 J. Story, Commentaries on the Constitution of the United States \xc2\xa7962, p.\xc2\xa0434 (1833) (\xe2\x80\x9cthe taxing power is often, very often, applied for other purposes, than revenue\xe2\x80\x9d). Today, federal and state taxes can compose more than half the retail price of cigarettes, not just to raise more money, but to encourage people to quit smoking. And we have upheld such obviously regulatory measures as taxes on selling marijuana and sawed-off shotguns. See United States v. Sanchez, \n\n340 U.\xc2\xa0S. 42\n\xe2\x80\x9345 (1950); Sonzinsky v. United States, \n\n300 U.\xc2\xa0S. 506, \n513 (1937)\n. Indeed, \xe2\x80\x9c[e]very tax is in some measure regula-tory. To some extent it interposes an economic impediment to the activity taxed as compared with others not taxed.\xe2\x80\x9d Sonzinsky, supra, at 513. That \xc2\xa75000A seeks to shape decisions about whether to buy health insurance does not mean that it cannot be a valid exercise of the taxing power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn distinguishing penalties from taxes, this Court has explained that \xe2\x80\x9cif the concept of penalty means anything, it means punishment for an unlawful act or omission.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Reorganized CF&I Fabricators of Utah, Inc., \n\n518 U.\xc2\xa0S. 213, \n224 (1996)\n; see also United States v. La Franca, \n\n282 U.\xc2\xa0S. 568, \n572 (1931)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[A] penalty, as the word is here used, is an exaction imposed by statute as punishment for an unlawful act\xe2\x80\x9d). While the individual mandate clearly aims to induce the purchase of health insurance, it need not be read to declare that failing to do so is unlawful. Neither the Act nor any other law attaches negative legal consequences to not buying health insurance, beyond requiring a payment to the IRS. The Government agrees with that reading, confirming that if someone chooses to pay rather than obtain health insurance, they have fully complied with the law. Brief for United States 60\xe2\x80\x9361; Tr. of Oral Arg. 49\xe2\x80\x9350 (Mar. 26, 2012).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIndeed, it is estimated that four million people each year will choose to pay the IRS rather than buy insurance. See Congressional Budget Office, supra, at 71. We would expect Congress to be troubled by that prospect if such conduct were unlawful. That Congress apparently regards such extensive failure to comply with the mandate as tolerable suggests that Congress did not think it was creating four million outlaws. It suggests instead that the shared responsibility payment merely imposes a tax citizens may lawfully choose to pay in lieu of buying health insurance.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe plaintiffs contend that Congress\xe2\x80\x99s choice of language\xe2\x80\x94stating that individuals \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d obtain insurance or pay a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94requires reading \xc2\xa75000A as punishing unlawful conduct, even if that interpretation would ren-der the law unconstitutional. We have rejected a similar argument before. In New York v. United States we examined a statute providing that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[e]ach State shall be responsible for providing .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. for the disposal of .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. low-level radioactive waste.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 169 (quoting \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa72021c(a)(1)(A)). A State that shipped its waste to another State was exposed to surcharges by the receiving State, a portion of which would be paid over to the Federal Government. And a State that did not adhere to the statutory scheme faced \xe2\x80\x9c[p]enalties for failure to comply,\xe2\x80\x9d including increases in the surcharge. \xc2\xa72021e(e)(2); New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 152\xe2\x80\x93153. New York urged us to read the statute as a federal command that the state legislature enact legislation to dispose of its waste, which would have violated the Constitution. To avoid that outcome, we interpreted the statute to impose only \xe2\x80\x9ca series of incentives\xe2\x80\x9d for the State to take responsibility for its waste. We then sustained the charge paid to the Federal Government as an exercise of the taxing power. Id., at 169\xe2\x80\x93174. We see no insurmountable obstacle to a similar approach here.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe joint dissenters argue that we cannot uphold \xc2\xa75000A as a tax because Congress did not \xe2\x80\x9cframe\xe2\x80\x9d it as such. Post, at 17. In effect, they contend that even if the Constitution permits Congress to do exactly what we interpret this statute to do, the law must be struck down because Congress used the wrong labels. An example may help illustrate why labels should not control here. Suppose Congress enacted a statute providing that every taxpayer who owns a house without energy efficient windows must pay $50 to the IRS. The amount due is adjusted based on factors such as taxable income and joint filing status, and is paid along with the taxpayer\xe2\x80\x99s income tax return. Those whose income is below the filing threshold need not pay. The required payment is not called a \xe2\x80\x9ctax,\xe2\x80\x9d a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty,\xe2\x80\x9d or anything else. No one would doubt that this law imposed a tax, and was within Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax. That conclusion should not change simply because Congress used the word \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d to describe the pay-ment. Interpreting such a law to be a tax would hardly \xe2\x80\x9c[i]mpos[e] a tax through judicial legislation.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 25. Rather, it would give practical effect to the Legislature\xe2\x80\x99s enactment.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur precedent demonstrates that Congress had the power to impose the exaction in \xc2\xa75000A under the taxing power, and that \xc2\xa75000A need not be read to do more than impose a tax. That is sufficient to sustain it. The \xe2\x80\x9cquestion of the constitutionality of action taken by Congress does not depend on recitals of the power which it undertakes to exercise.\xe2\x80\x9d Woods v. Cloyd W. Miller Co., \n\n333 U.\xc2\xa0S. 138, \n144 (1948)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tEven if the taxing power enables Congress to impose a tax on not obtaining health insurance, any tax must still comply with other requirements in the Constitution. Plaintiffs argue that the shared responsibility payment does not do so, citing Article I, \xc2\xa79, clause 4. That clause provides: \xe2\x80\x9cNo Capitation, or other direct, Tax shall be laid, unless in Proportion to the Census or Enumeration herein before directed to be taken.\xe2\x80\x9d This requirement means that any \xe2\x80\x9cdirect Tax\xe2\x80\x9d must be apportioned so that each State pays in proportion to its population. According to the plaintiffs, if the individual mandate imposes a tax, it is a direct tax, and it is unconstitutional because Congress made no effort to apportion it among the States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t Even when the Direct Tax Clause was written it was unclear what else, other than a capitation (also known as a \xe2\x80\x9chead tax\xe2\x80\x9d or a \xe2\x80\x9cpoll tax\xe2\x80\x9d), might be a direct tax. See Springer v. United States, \n\n102 U.\xc2\xa0S. 586\n\xe2\x80\x93598 (1881). Soon after the framing, Congress passed a tax on ownership of carriages, over James Madison\xe2\x80\x99s objection that it was an unapportioned direct tax. Id., at 597. This Court upheld the tax, in part reasoning that apportioning such a tax would make little sense, because it would have required taxing carriage owners at dramatically different rates depending on how many carriages were in their home State. See Hylton v. United States, 3 Dall. 171, 174 (1796) (opinion of Chase, J.). The Court was unanimous, and those Justices who wrote opinions either directly asserted or strongly suggested that only two forms of taxation were direct: capitations and land taxes. See id., at 175; id., at 177 (opinion of Paterson, J.); id., at 183 (opinion of Iredell, J.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat narrow view of what a direct tax might be per-sisted for a century. In 1880, for example, we explained that \xe2\x80\x9cdirect taxes, within the meaning of the Constitution, are only capitation taxes, as expressed in that instrument, and taxes on real estate.\xe2\x80\x9d Springer, supra, at 602. In 1895, we expanded our interpretation to include taxes on personal property and income from personal property, in the course of striking down aspects of the federal income tax. Pollock v. Farmers\xe2\x80\x99 Loan & Trust Co., \n\n158 U.\xc2\xa0S. 601, \n618 (1895)\n. That result was overturned by the \n \nSixteenth Amendment, although we continued to consider taxes on personal property to be direct taxes. See Eisner v. Macom-ber, \n\n252 U.\xc2\xa0S. 189\n\xe2\x80\x93219 (1920).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tA tax on going without health insurance does not fall within any recognized category of direct tax. It is not a capitation. Capitations are taxes paid by every person, \xe2\x80\x9cwithout regard to property, profession, or any other circumstance.\xe2\x80\x9d Hylton, supra, at 175 (opinion of Chase, J.) (emphasis altered). The whole point of the shared responsibility payment is that it is triggered by specific cir-cumstances\xe2\x80\x94earning a certain amount of income but not obtaining health insurance. The payment is also plainly not a tax on the ownership of land or personal property. The shared responsibility payment is thus not a direct tax that must be apportioned among the several States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThere may, however, be a more fundamental objection to a tax on those who lack health insurance. Even if only a tax, the payment under \xc2\xa75000A(b) remains a burden that the Federal Government imposes for an omission, not an act. If it is troubling to interpret the Commerce Clause as authorizing Congress to regulate those who abstain from commerce, perhaps it should be similarly troubling to permit Congress to impose a tax for not doing something.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThree considerations allay this concern. First, and most importantly, it is abundantly clear the Constitution does not guarantee that individuals may avoid taxation through inactivity. A capitation, after all, is a tax that every- one must pay simply for existing, and capitations are expressly contemplated by the Constitution. The Court today holds that our Constitution protects us from federal regulation under the Commerce Clause so long as we ab-stain from the regulated activity. But from its creation, the Constitution has made no such promise with respect to taxes. See Letter from Benjamin Franklin to M. Le Roy (Nov. 13, 1789) (\xe2\x80\x9cOur new Constitution is now established .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. but in this world nothing can be said to be certain, except death and taxes\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhether the mandate can be upheld under the Commerce Clause is a question about the scope of federal authority. Its answer depends on whether Congress can exercise what all acknowledge to be the novel course of directing individuals to purchase insurance. Congress\xe2\x80\x99s use of the Taxing Clause to encourage buying something is, by contrast, not new. Tax incentives already promote, for example, purchasing homes and professional educations. See 26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7163(h), 25A. Sustaining the mandate as a tax depends only on whether Congress has properly exercised its taxing power to encourage purchasing health insurance, not whether it can. Upholding the individual mandate under the Taxing Clause thus does not recognize any new federal power. It determines that Congress has used an existing one.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, Congress\xe2\x80\x99s ability to use its taxing power to influence conduct is not without limits. A few of our cases policed these limits aggressively, invalidating punitive exactions obviously designed to regulate behavior otherwise regarded at the time as beyond federal authority. See, e.g., United States v. Butler, \n\n297 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1 (1936)\n; Drexel Furniture, \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 20\n. More often and more recently we have declined to closely examine the regulatory motive or effect of revenue-raising measures. See Kahriger, 345 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 27\xe2\x80\x9331 (collecting cases). We have nonetheless maintained that \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98there comes a time in the extension of the penalizing features of the so-called tax when it loses its character as such and becomes a mere penalty with the characteristics of regulation and punishment.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Kurth Ranch, 511 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 779 (quoting Drexel Furniture, supra, at 38).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe have already explained that the shared responsibility payment\xe2\x80\x99s practical characteristics pass muster as a tax under our narrowest interpretations of the taxing power. Supra, at 35\xe2\x80\x9336. Because the tax at hand is within even those strict limits, we need not here decide the precise point at which an exaction becomes so punitive that the taxing power does not authorize it. It remains true, however, that the \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98power to tax is not the power to destroy while this Court sits.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Oklahoma Tax Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Texas Co., \n\n336 U.\xc2\xa0S. 342, \n364 (1949)\n (quoting Panhandle Oil Co. v. Mississippi ex\xc2\xa0rel. Knox, \n\n277 U.\xc2\xa0S. 218, \n223 (1928)\n (Holmes, J., dissenting)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThird, although the breadth of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax is greater than its power to regulate commerce, the taxing power does not give Congress the same degree of control over individual behavior. Once we recognize that Congress may regulate a particular decision under the Commerce Clause, the Federal Government can bring its full weight to bear. Congress may simply command individ-uals to do as it directs. An individual who disobeys may be subjected to criminal sanctions. Those sanctions can include not only fines and imprisonment, but all the attendant consequences of being branded a criminal: deprivation of otherwise protected civil rights, such as the right to bear arms or vote in elections; loss of employment opportunities; social stigma; and severe disabilities in other controversies, such as custody or immigration disputes.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBy contrast, Congress\xe2\x80\x99s authority under the taxing power is limited to requiring an individual to pay money into the Federal Treasury, no more. If a tax is properly paid, the Government has no power to compel or punish individuals subject to it. We do not make light of the se-vere burden that taxation\xe2\x80\x94especially taxation motivated by a regulatory purpose\xe2\x80\x94can impose. But imposition of a tax nonetheless leaves an individual with a lawful choice to do or not do a certain act, so long as he is willing to pay a tax levied on that choice.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s requirement that certain in-dividuals pay a financial penalty for not obtaining health insurance may reasonably be characterized as a tax. Be-cause the Constitution permits such a tax, it is not our role to forbid it, or to pass upon its wisdom or fairness.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tD\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Ginsburg questions the necessity of rejecting the Government\xe2\x80\x99s commerce power argument, given that \xc2\xa75000A can be upheld under the taxing power. Post, at 37. But the statute reads more naturally as a command to buy insurance than as a tax, and I would uphold it as a command if the Constitution allowed it. It is only because the Commerce Clause does not authorize such a command that it is necessary to reach the taxing power question. And it is only because we have a duty to construe a stat-ute to save it, if fairly possible, that \xc2\xa75000A can be interpreted as a tax. Without deciding the Commerce Clause question, I would find no basis to adopt such a saving construction.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Federal Government does not have the power to order people to buy health insurance. Section 5000A would therefore be unconstitutional if read as a command. The Federal Government does have the power to impose a tax on those without health insurance. Section 5000A is therefore constitutional, because it can reasonably be read as a tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIV\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe States also contend that the Medicaid expansion exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99s authority under the Spending Clause. They claim that Congress is coercing the States to adopt the changes it wants by threatening to withhold all of a State\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid grants, unless the State accepts the new expanded funding and complies with the conditions that come with it. This, they argue, violates the basic principle that the \xe2\x80\x9cFederal Government may not compel the States to enact or administer a federal regulatory program.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 188.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThere is no doubt that the Act dramatically increases state obligations under Medicaid. The current Medicaid program requires States to cover only certain discrete categories of needy individuals\xe2\x80\x94pregnant women, children, needy families, the blind, the elderly, and the dis-abled. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10). There is no mandatory coverage for most childless adults, and the States typically do not offer any such coverage. The States also enjoy considerable flexibility with respect to the coverage levels for parents of needy families. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(ii). On average States cover only those unemployed parents who make less than 37 percent of the federal poverty level, and only those employed parents who make less than 63 percent of the poverty line. Kaiser Comm\xe2\x80\x99n on Medicaid and the Uninsured, Performing Under Pressure 11, and fig.\xc2\xa011 (2012).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Medicaid provisions of the Affordable Care Act, in contrast, require States to expand their Medicaid programs by 2014 to cover all individuals under the age of 65 with incomes below 133 percent of the federal poverty line. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(i)(VIII). The Act also establishes a new \xe2\x80\x9c[e]ssential health benefits\xe2\x80\x9d package, which States must provide to all new Medicaid recipients\xe2\x80\x94a level sufficient to satisfy a recipient\xe2\x80\x99s obligations under the individual man-date. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396a(k)(1), 1396u\xe2\x80\x937(b)(5), 18022(b). The Af-fordable Care Act provides that the Federal Government will pay 100 percent of the costs of covering these newly eligible individuals through 2016. \xc2\xa71396d(y)(1). In the following years, the federal payment level gradually decreases, to a minimum of 90 percent. Ibid. In light of the expansion in coverage mandated by the Act, the Federal Government estimates that its Medicaid spending will in-crease by approximately $100 billion per year, nearly 40 percent above current levels. Statement of Douglas W. Elmendorf, CBO\xe2\x80\x99s Analysis of the Major Health Care Legislation Enacted in March 2010, p.\xc2\xa014, Table 2 (Mar. 30, 2011).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Spending Clause grants Congress the power \xe2\x80\x9cto pay the Debts and provide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. We have long recognized that Congress may use this power to grant federal funds to the States, and may condition such a grant upon the States\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9ctaking certain actions that Congress could not require them to take.\xe2\x80\x9d College Savings Bank, 527 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 686. Such measures \xe2\x80\x9cencourage a State to regulate in a particular way, [and] influenc[e] a State\xe2\x80\x99s policy choices.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, supra, at 166. The con-ditions imposed by Congress ensure that the funds are used by the States to \xe2\x80\x9cprovide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare\xe2\x80\x9d in the manner Congress intended.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAt the same time, our cases have recognized limits on Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Spending Clause to secure state compliance with federal objectives. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have repeatedly characterized .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. Spending Clause legislation as \xe2\x80\x98much in the nature of a contract.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Barnes v. Gorman, \n\n536 U.\xc2\xa0S. 181, \n186 (2002)\n (quoting Pennhurst State School and Hospital v. Halderman, \n\n451 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n17 (1981)\n). The legitimacy of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s exercise of the spending power \xe2\x80\x9cthus rests on whether the State voluntarily and knowingly accepts the terms of the \xe2\x80\x98contract.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Pennhurst, supra, at 17. Respecting this limitation is critical to ensuring that Spending Clause legislation does not undermine the status of the States as independent sovereigns in our fed-eral system. That system \xe2\x80\x9crests on what might at first seem a counterintuitive insight, that \xe2\x80\x98freedom is enhanced by the creation of two governments, not one.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Bond, 564 U.\xc2\xa0S., at ___ (slip op., at 8) (quoting Alden v. Maine, \n\n527 U.\xc2\xa0S. 706, \n758 (1999)\n). For this reason, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Constitution has never been understood to confer upon Congress the ability to require the States to govern according to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 instructions.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, supra, at 162. Otherwise the two-government system established by the Framers would give way to a system that vests power in one central government, and individual liberty would suffer.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat insight has led this Court to strike down fed- eral legislation that commandeers a State\xe2\x80\x99s legislative or administrative apparatus for federal purposes. See, e.g., Printz, 521 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 933 (striking down federal legisla- tion compelling state law enforcement officers to perform federally mandated background checks on handgun purchasers); New York, supra, at 174\xe2\x80\x93175 (invalidating provisions of an Act that would compel a State to either take title to nuclear waste or enact particular state waste regulations). It has also led us to scrutinize Spending Clause legislation to ensure that Congress is not using financial inducements to exert a \xe2\x80\x9cpower akin to undue influence.\xe2\x80\x9d Steward Machine Co. v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 548, \n590 (1937)\n. Congress may use its spending power to create incentives for States to act in accordance with federal policies. But when \xe2\x80\x9cpressure turns into compulsion,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., the legislation runs contrary to our system of federalism. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Constitution simply does not give Congress the authority to require the States to regulate.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 178. That is true whether Congress directly commands a State to regulate or indirectly coerces a State to adopt a federal regulatory system as its own.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tPermitting the Federal Government to force the States to implement a federal program would threaten the political accountability key to our federal system. \xe2\x80\x9c[W]here the Federal Government directs the States to regulate, it may be state officials who will bear the brunt of public disapproval, while the federal officials who devised the regu-latory program may remain insulated from the electoral ramifications of their decision.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 169. Spending Clause programs do not pose this danger when a State has a legitimate choice whether to accept the federal conditions in exchange for federal funds. In such a situation, state officials can fairly be held politically accountable for choosing to accept or refuse the federal offer. But when the State has no choice, the Federal Government can achieve its objectives without accountability, just as in New York and Printz. Indeed, this danger is heightened when Congress acts under the Spending Clause, because Congress can use that power to implement federal policy it could not impose directly under its enumerated powers.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe addressed such concerns in Steward Machine. That case involved a federal tax on employers that was abated if the businesses paid into a state unemployment plan that met certain federally specified conditions. An employer sued, alleging that the tax was impermissibly \xe2\x80\x9cdriv[ing] the state legislatures under the whip of economic pressure into the enactment of unemployment compensation laws at the bidding of the central government.\xe2\x80\x9d 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 587. We acknowledged the danger that the Federal Government might employ its taxing power to exert a \xe2\x80\x9cpower akin to undue influence\xe2\x80\x9d upon the States. Id., at 590. But we observed that Congress adopted the challenged tax and abatement program to channel money to the States that would otherwise have gone into the Federal Treasury for use in providing national unemployment services. Congress was willing to direct businesses to instead pay the money into state programs only on the condition that the money be used for the same purposes. Predicating tax abatement on a State\xe2\x80\x99s adoption of a particular type of un-employment legislation was therefore a means to \xe2\x80\x9csafeguard [the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s] own treasury.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 591. We held that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]n such circumstances, if in no others, inducement or persuasion does not go beyond the bounds of power.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn rejecting the argument that the federal law was a \xe2\x80\x9cweapon[\xc2\xa0] of coercion, destroying or impairing the autonomy of the states,\xe2\x80\x9d the Court noted that there was no reason to suppose that the State in that case acted other than through \xe2\x80\x9cher unfettered will.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 586, 590. Indeed, the State itself did \xe2\x80\x9cnot offer a suggestion that in passing the unemployment law she was affected by duress.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 589.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs our decision in Steward Machine confirms, Congress may attach appropriate conditions to federal taxing and spending programs to preserve its control over the use of federal funds. In the typical case we look to the States to defend their prerogatives by adopting \xe2\x80\x9cthe simple expedient of not yielding\xe2\x80\x9d to federal blandishments when they do not want to embrace the federal policies as their own. Massachusetts v. Mellon, \n\n262 U.\xc2\xa0S. 447, \n482 (1923)\n. The States are separate and independent sovereigns. Sometimes they have to act like it.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe States, however, argue that the Medicaid expansion is far from the typical case. They object that Congress has \xe2\x80\x9ccrossed the line distinguishing encouragement from coercion,\xe2\x80\x9d New York, supra, at 175, in the way it has structured the funding: Instead of simply refusing to grant the new funds to States that will not accept the new conditions, Congress has also threatened to withhold those States\xe2\x80\x99 existing Medicaid funds. The States claim that this threat serves no purpose other than to force unwilling States to sign up for the dramatic expansion in health care coverage effected by the Act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tGiven the nature of the threat and the programs at issue here, we must agree. We have upheld Congress\xe2\x80\x99s authority to condition the receipt of funds on the States\xe2\x80\x99 complying with restrictions on the use of those funds, because that is the means by which Congress ensures that the funds are spent according to its view of the \xe2\x80\x9cgeneral Welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d Conditions that do not here govern the use of the funds, however, cannot be justified on that ba- sis. When, for example, such conditions take the form of threats to terminate other significant independent grants, the conditions are properly viewed as a means of pressuring the States to accept policy changes.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn South Dakota v. Dole, we considered a challenge to a federal law that threatened to withhold five percent of a State\xe2\x80\x99s federal highway funds if the State did not raise its drinking age to 21. The Court found that the condition was \xe2\x80\x9cdirectly related to one of the main purposes for which highway funds are expended\xe2\x80\x94safe interstate travel.\xe2\x80\x9d 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 208. At the same time, the condition was not a restriction on how the highway funds\xe2\x80\x94set aside for specific highway improvement and maintenance efforts\xe2\x80\x94were to be used.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe accordingly asked whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe financial inducement offered by Congress\xe2\x80\x9d was \xe2\x80\x9cso coercive as to pass the point at which \xe2\x80\x98pressure turns into compulsion.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 211 (quoting Steward Machine, supra, at 590). By \xe2\x80\x9cfinancial inducement\xe2\x80\x9d the Court meant the threat of losing five percent of highway funds; no new money was offered to the States to raise their drinking ages. We found that the inducement was not impermissibly coercive, because Congress was offering only \xe2\x80\x9crelatively mild encouragement to the States.\xe2\x80\x9d Dole, 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 211. We observed that \xe2\x80\x9call South Dakota would lose if she adheres to her chosen course as to a suitable minimum drinking age is 5%\xe2\x80\x9d of her highway funds. Ibid. In fact, the federal funds at stake constituted less than half of one percent of South Dakota\xe2\x80\x99s budget at the time. See Nat. Assn. of State Budget Officers, The State Expenditure Report 59 (1987); South Dakota v. Dole, 791 F.\xc2\xa02d 628, 630 (CA8 1986). In consequence, \xe2\x80\x9cwe conclude[d] that [the] encouragement to state action [was] a valid use of the spending power.\xe2\x80\x9d Dole, 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 212. Whether to accept the drinking age change \xe2\x80\x9cremain[ed] the prerogative of the States not merely in theory but in fact.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 211\xe2\x80\x93212.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn this case, the financial \xe2\x80\x9cinducement\xe2\x80\x9d Congress has chosen is much more than \xe2\x80\x9crelatively mild encouragement\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94it is a gun to the head. Section 1396c of the Medicaid Act provides that if a State\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid plan does not comply with the Act\xe2\x80\x99s requirements, the Secretary of Health and Human Services may declare that \xe2\x80\x9cfurther payments will not be made to the State.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396c. A State that opts out of the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s expansion in health care coverage thus stands to lose not merely \xe2\x80\x9ca relatively small percentage\xe2\x80\x9d of its existing Medicaid funding, but all of it. Dole, supra, at 211. Medicaid spending accounts for over 20 percent of the average State\xe2\x80\x99s total budget, with federal funds covering 50 to 83 percent of those costs. See Nat. Assn. of State Budget Officers, Fiscal Year 2010 State Expenditure Report, p.\xc2\xa011, Table 5 (2011); \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396d(b). The Federal Government estimates that it will pay out approximately $3.3 trillion between 2010 and 2019 in order to cover the costs of pre-expansion Medicaid. Brief for United States 10, n.\xc2\xa06. In addition, the States have developed intricate statutory and administrative regimes over the course of many decades to implement their objectives under existing Medicaid. It is easy to see how the Dole Court could conclude that the threatened loss of less than half of one percent of South Dakota\xe2\x80\x99s budget left that State with a \xe2\x80\x9cprerogative\xe2\x80\x9d to reject Congress\xe2\x80\x99s desired policy, \xe2\x80\x9cnot merely in theory but in fact.\xe2\x80\x9d 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 211\xe2\x80\x93212. The threatened loss of over 10 percent of a State\xe2\x80\x99s overall budget, in contrast, is economic dragooning that leaves the States with no real option but to acquiesce in the Medicaid expansion.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Ginsburg claims that Dole is distinguishable because here \xe2\x80\x9cCongress has not threatened to withhold funds earmarked for any other program.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 47. But that begs the question: The States contend that the expansion is in reality a new program and that Congress is forcing them to accept it by threatening the funds for the existing Medicaid program. We cannot agree that existing Medicaid and the expansion dictated by the Affordable Care Act are all one program simply because \xe2\x80\x9cCongress styled\xe2\x80\x9d them as such. Post, at 49. If the expansion is not properly viewed as a modification of the existing Medicaid program, Congress\xe2\x80\x99s decision to so title it is irrelevant.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t13\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tHere, the Government claims that the Medicaid expansion is properly viewed merely as a modification of the ex-isting program because the States agreed that Congress could change the terms of Medicaid when they signed on in the first place. The Government observes that the Social Security Act, which includes the original Medicaid provisions, contains a clause expressly reserving \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he right to alter, amend, or repeal any provision\xe2\x80\x9d of that statute. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71304. So it does. But \xe2\x80\x9cif Congress intends to impose a condition on the grant of federal moneys, it must do so unambiguously.\xe2\x80\x9d Pennhurst, 451 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 17. A State confronted with statutory language reserving the right to \xe2\x80\x9calter\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9camend\xe2\x80\x9d the pertinent provisions of the Social Security Act might reasonably assume that Congress was entitled to make adjustments to the Medicaid program as it developed. Congress has in fact done so, sometimes conditioning only the new funding, other times both old and new. See, e.g., Social Security Amendments of 1972, \n\n86Stat.\n1381\xe2\x80\x931382, 1465 (extending Med-icaid eligibility, but partly conditioning only the new funding); Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1990, \xc2\xa74601, \n\n104Stat.\n1388\xe2\x80\x93166 (extending eligibility, and conditioning old and new funds).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Medicaid expansion, however, accomplishes a shift in kind, not merely degree. The original program was de-signed to cover medical services for four particular cat-egories of the needy: the disabled, the blind, the elderly, and needy families with dependent children. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10). Previous amendments to Medicaid eligibility merely altered and expanded the boundaries of these categories. Under the Affordable Care Act, Medicaid is transformed into a program to meet the health care needs of the entire nonelderly population with income below 133 percent of the poverty level. It is no longer a program to care for the neediest among us, but rather an element of a comprehensive national plan to provide universal health insurance coverage.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t14\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIndeed, the manner in which the expansion is structured indicates that while Congress may have styled the expansion a mere alteration of existing Medicaid, it recognized it was enlisting the States in a new health care program. Congress created a separate funding provision to cover the costs of providing services to any person made newly eligible by the expansion. While Congress pays 50 to 83 percent of the costs of covering individuals currently enrolled in Medicaid, \xc2\xa71396d(b), once the expansion is fully implemented Congress will pay 90 percent of the costs for newly eligible persons, \xc2\xa71396d(y)(1). The conditions on use of the different funds are also distinct. Congress mandated that newly eligible persons receive a level of coverage that is less comprehensive than the traditional Medicaid benefit package. \xc2\xa71396a(k)(1); see Brief for United States 9.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs we have explained, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]hough Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to legislate under the spending power is broad, it does not include surprising participating States with postacceptance or \xe2\x80\x98retroactive\xe2\x80\x99 conditions.\xe2\x80\x9d Pennhurst, supra, at 25. A State could hardly anticipate that Congress\xe2\x80\x99s reservation of the right to \xe2\x80\x9calter\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9camend\xe2\x80\x9d the Medicaid program included the power to transform it so dramatically.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Ginsburg claims that in fact this expansion is no different from the previous changes to Medicaid, such that \xe2\x80\x9ca State would be hard put to complain that it lacked fair notice.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 56. But the prior change she dis-cusses\xe2\x80\x94presumably the most dramatic alteration she could find\xe2\x80\x94does not come close to working the transformation the expansion accomplishes. She highlights an amendment requiring States to cover pregnant women and increasing the number of eligible children. Ibid. But this modification can hardly be described as a major change in a program that\xe2\x80\x94from its inception\xe2\x80\x94provided health care for \xe2\x80\x9cfamilies with dependent children.\xe2\x80\x9d Previous Medicaid amendments simply do not fall into the same category as the one at stake here.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court in Steward Machine did not attempt to \xe2\x80\x9cfix the outermost line\xe2\x80\x9d where persuasion gives way to coercion. 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 591. The Court found it \xe2\x80\x9c[e]nough for present purposes that wherever the line may be, this statute is within it.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. We have no need to fix a line either. It is enough for today that wherever that line may be, this statute is surely beyond it. Congress may not simply \xe2\x80\x9cconscript state [agencies] into the national bureaucratic army,\xe2\x80\x9d FERC v. Mississippi, \n\n456 U.\xc2\xa0S. 742, \n775 (1982)\n (O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part), and that is what it is attempting to do with the Medicaid expansion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNothing in our opinion precludes Congress from offering funds under the Affordable Care Act to expand the availability of health care, and requiring that States accepting such funds comply with the conditions on their use. What Congress is not free to do is to penalize States that choose not to participate in that new program by taking away their existing Medicaid funding. Section 1396c gives the Secretary of Health and Human Services the authority to do just that. It allows her to withhold all \xe2\x80\x9cfurther [Medicaid] payments .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. to the State\xe2\x80\x9d if she determines that the State is out of compliance with any Medicaid requirement, including those contained in the expansion. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396c. In light of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s holding, the Secretary cannot apply \xc2\xa71396c to withdraw existing Medicaid funds for failure to comply with the requirements set out in the expansion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat fully remedies the constitutional violation we have identified. The chapter of the United States Code that contains \xc2\xa71396c includes a severability clause confirming that we need go no further. That clause specifies that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]f any provision of this chapter, or the application thereof to any person or circumstance, is held invalid, the remainder of the chapter, and the application of such provision to other persons or circumstances shall not be affected thereby.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71303. Today\xe2\x80\x99s holding does not affect the continued ap-plication of \xc2\xa71396c to the existing Medicaid program. Nor does it affect the Secretary\xe2\x80\x99s ability to withdraw funds pro-vided under the Affordable Care Act if a State that has chosen to participate in the expansion fails to comply with the requirements of that Act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis is not to say, as the joint dissent suggests, that we are \xe2\x80\x9crewriting the Medicaid Expansion.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 48. Instead, we determine, first, that \xc2\xa71396c is unconstitutional when applied to withdraw existing Medicaid funds from States that decline to comply with the expansion. We then follow Congress\xe2\x80\x99s explicit textual instruction to leave unaffected \xe2\x80\x9cthe remainder of the chapter, and the application of [the challenged] provision to other persons or circumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71303. When we invalidate an application of a statute because that application is unconstitutional, we are not \xe2\x80\x9crewriting\xe2\x80\x9d the statute; we are merely enforcing the Constitution.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe question remains whether today\xe2\x80\x99s holding affects other provisions of the Affordable Care Act. In considering that question, \xe2\x80\x9c[w]e seek to determine what Congress would have intended in light of the Court\xe2\x80\x99s constitutional holding.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Booker, \n\n543 U.\xc2\xa0S. 220, \n246 (2005)\n (internal quotation marks omitted). Our \xe2\x80\x9ctouchstone for any decision about remedy is legislative intent, for a court cannot use its remedial powers to circum- vent the intent of the legislature.\xe2\x80\x9d Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., \n\n546 U.\xc2\xa0S. 320, \n330 (2006)\n (internal quotation marks omitted). The question here is whether Congress would have wanted the rest of the Act to stand, had it known that States would have a genuine choice whether to participate in the new Medicaid expansion. Unless it is \xe2\x80\x9cevident\xe2\x80\x9d that the answer is no, we must leave the rest of the Act intact. Champlin Refining Co. v. Corporation Comm\xe2\x80\x99n of Okla., \n\n286 U.\xc2\xa0S. 210, \n234 (1932)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe are confident that Congress would have wanted to preserve the rest of the Act. It is fair to say that Congress assumed that every State would participate in the Medicaid expansion, given that States had no real choice but to do so. The States contend that Congress enacted the rest of the Act with such full participation in mind; they point out that Congress made Medicaid a means for satisfying the mandate, \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(f)(1)(A)(ii), and enacted no other plan for providing coverage to many low-income individuals. According to the States, this means that the entire Act must fall.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe disagree. The Court today limits the financial pressure the Secretary may apply to induce States to accept the terms of the Medicaid expansion. As a practical matter, that means States may now choose to reject the expansion; that is the whole point. But that does not mean all or even any will. Some States may indeed decline to participate, either because they are unsure they will be able to afford their share of the new funding obligations, or because they are unwilling to commit the administrative resources necessary to support the expansion. Other States, however, may voluntarily sign up, finding the idea of expanding Medicaid coverage attractive, particularly given the level of federal funding the Act offers at the outset.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe have no way of knowing how many States will accept the terms of the expansion, but we do not believe Congress would have wanted the whole Act to fall, simply because some may choose not to participate. The other reforms Congress enacted, after all, will remain \xe2\x80\x9cfully operative as a law,\xe2\x80\x9d Champlin, supra, at 234, and will still function in a way \xe2\x80\x9cconsistent with Congress\xe2\x80\x99 basic objectives in enacting the statute,\xe2\x80\x9d Booker, supra, at 259. Confident that Congress would not have intended anything different, we conclude that the rest of the Act need not fall in light of our constitutional holding.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act is constitutional in part and unconstitutional in part. The individual mandate cannot be upheld as an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99s power under the Commerce Clause. That Clause authorizes Congress to regulate interstate commerce, not to order individuals to engage in it. In this case, however, it is reasonable to con-strue what Congress has done as increasing taxes on those who have a certain amount of income, but choose to go without health insurance. Such legislation is within Con-gress\xe2\x80\x99s power to tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs for the Medicaid expansion, that portion of the Affordable Care Act violates the Constitution by threatening existing Medicaid funding. Congress has no authority to order the States to regulate according to its instructions. Congress may offer the States grants and require the States to comply with accompanying conditions, but the States must have a genuine choice whether to accept the offer. The States are given no such choice in this case: They must either accept a basic change in the nature of Medicaid, or risk losing all Medicaid funding. The remedy for that constitutional violation is to preclude the Federal Government from imposing such a sanction. That remedy does not require striking down other portions of the Affordable Care Act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Framers created a Federal Government of limited powers, and assigned to this Court the duty of enforcing those limits. The Court does so today. But the Court does not express any opinion on the wisdom of the Affordable Care Act. Under the Constitution, that judgment is reserved to the people.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit is affirmed in part and reversed in part.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\nIt is so ordered.\n\t\t\t

Notes

\n\n1\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Eleventh Circuit did not consider whether the Anti-Injunction Act bars challenges to the individual mandate. The District Court had determined that it did not, and neither side challenged that holding on appeal. The same was true in the Fourth Circuit, but that court examined the question sua\xc2\xa0sponte because it viewed the Anti-Injunction Act as a limit on its subject matter jurisdiction. See Liberty Univ., 671 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 400\xe2\x80\x93401. The Sixth Circuit and the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit considered the question but determined that the Anti-Injunction Act did not apply. See Thomas More, 651 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 539\xe2\x80\x93540 (CA6); Seven-Sky, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 5\xe2\x80\x9314 (CADC).\n\t\n
\n\n2\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0We appointed H. Bartow Farr III to brief and argue in support of the Eleventh Circuit\xe2\x80\x99s judgment with respect to severability, and Robert A. Long to brief and argue the proposition that the Anti-Injunction Act bars the current challenges to the individual mandate. 565 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___ (2011). Both amici have ably discharged their assigned responsibilities.\n\t\n
\n\n3\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The examples of other congressional mandates cited by Justice Ginsburg, post, at 35, n.\xc2\xa010 (opinion concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dissenting in part), are not to the contrary. Each of those mandates\xe2\x80\x94to report for jury duty, to register for the draft, to purchase firearms in anticipation of militia service, to exchange gold currency for paper currency, and to file a tax return\xe2\x80\x94are based on constitutional provisions other than the Commerce Clause. See Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa09 (to \xe2\x80\x9cconstitute Tribunals inferior to the supreme Court\xe2\x80\x9d); id., cl.\xc2\xa012 (to \xe2\x80\x9craise and support Armies\xe2\x80\x9d); id., cl.\xc2\xa016 (to \xe2\x80\x9cprovide for organizing, arming, and disciplining, the Militia\xe2\x80\x9d); id., cl.\xc2\xa05 (to \xe2\x80\x9ccoin Money\xe2\x80\x9d); id., cl.\xc2\xa01 (to \xe2\x80\x9clay and collect Taxes\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\n
\n\n4\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg suggests that \xe2\x80\x9cat the time the Constitution was framed, to \xe2\x80\x98regulate\xe2\x80\x99 meant, among other things, to require action.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 23 (citing Seven-Sky v. Holder, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d 1, 16 (CADC 2011); brackets and some internal quotation marks omitted). But to reach this conclusion, the case cited by Justice Ginsburg relied on a dictionary in which \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o order; to command\xe2\x80\x9d was the fifth-alternative definition of \xe2\x80\x9cto direct,\xe2\x80\x9d which was itself the second-alternative definition of \xe2\x80\x9cto regulate.\xe2\x80\x9d See Seven-Sky, supra, at 16 (citing S. Johnson, Dictionary of the English Language (4th ed. 1773) (reprinted 1978)). It is unlikely that the Framers had such an obscure meaning in mind when they used the word \xe2\x80\x9cregulate.\xe2\x80\x9d Far more commonly, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o regulate\xe2\x80\x9d meant \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o adjust by rule or method,\xe2\x80\x9d which presupposes something to adjust. 2 Johnson, supra, at 1619; see also Gibbons, 9 Wheat., at 196 (defining the commerce power as the power \xe2\x80\x9cto prescribe the rule by which commerce is to be governed\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\n
\n\n5\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg cites two eminent domain cases from the 1890s to support the proposition that our case law does not \xe2\x80\x9ctoe the activity versus inactivity line.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 24\xe2\x80\x9325 (citing Monongahela Nav. Co. v. United States, \n\xe2\x80\x93337 (1893), and Cherokee Nation v. Southern Kansas R. Co., \n\xe2\x80\x93659 (1890)). The fact that the \n requires the payment of just compensation when the Government exercises its power of eminent domain does not turn the taking into a commercial transaction between the landowner and the Government, let alone a government-compelled transaction between the landowner and a third party.\n\t\n
\n\n6\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0In an attempt to recast the individual mandate as a regulation of commercial activity, Justice Ginsburg suggests that \xe2\x80\x9c[a]n individual who opts not to purchase insurance from a private insurer can be seen as actively selecting another form of insurance: self-insurance.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 26. But \xe2\x80\x9cself-insurance\xe2\x80\x9d is, in this context, nothing more than a description of the failure to purchase insurance. Individuals are no more \xe2\x80\x9cactiv[e] in the self-insurance market\xe2\x80\x9d when they fail to purchase insurance, ibid., than they are active in the \xe2\x80\x9crest\xe2\x80\x9d market when doing nothing.\n\t\n
\n\n7\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Sotelo, in particular, would seem to refute the joint dissent\xe2\x80\x99s contention that we have \xe2\x80\x9cnever\xe2\x80\x9d treated an exaction as a tax if it was denominated a penalty. Post, at 20. We are not persuaded by the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to distinguish Sotelo as a statutory construction case from the bankruptcy context. Post, at 17, n.\xc2\xa05. The dissent itself treats the question here as one of statutory interpretation, and indeed also relies on a statutory interpretation case from the bankruptcy context. Post, at 23 (citing United States v. Reorganized CF&I Fabricators of Utah, Inc., \n).\n\t\n
\n\n8\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0In 2016, for example, individuals making $35,000 a year are expected to owe the IRS about $60 for any month in which they do not have health insurance. Someone with an annual income of $100,000 a year would likely owe about $200. The price of a qualifying insurance policy is projected to be around $400 per month. See D. Newman, CRS Report for Congress, Individual Mandate and Related Information Re-quirements Under PPACA 7, and n.\xc2\xa025 (2011).\n\t\n
\n\n9\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0We do not suggest that any exaction lacking a scienter requirement and enforced by the IRS is within the taxing power. See post, at 23\xe2\x80\x9324 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ., dissenting). Congress could not, for example, expand its authority to impose criminal fines by creating strict liability offenses enforced by the IRS rather than the FBI. But the fact the exaction here is paid like a tax, to the agency that collects taxes\xe2\x80\x94rather than, for example, exacted by Department of Labor inspectors after ferreting out willful malfeasance\xe2\x80\x94suggests that this exaction may be viewed as a tax.\n\t\n
\n\n10\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The joint dissent attempts to distinguish New York v. United States on the ground that the seemingly imperative language in that case was in an \xe2\x80\x9cintroductory provision\xe2\x80\x9d that had \xe2\x80\x9cno legal consequences.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 19. We did not rely on that reasoning in New York. See 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 169\xe2\x80\x93170. Nor could we have. While the Court quoted only the broad statement that \xe2\x80\x9c[e]ach State shall be responsible\xe2\x80\x9d for its waste, that language was implemented through operative provisions that also use the words on which the dissent relies. See \n (entitled \xe2\x80\x9cRequirements for non-sited compact regions and non-member States\xe2\x80\x9d and directing that those entities \xe2\x80\x9cshall comply with the following requirements\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa72021e(e)(2) (describing \xe2\x80\x9cPenalties for failure to comply\xe2\x80\x9d). The Court upheld those provisions not as lawful commands, but as \xe2\x80\x9cincentives.\xe2\x80\x9d See 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 152\xe2\x80\x93153, 171\xe2\x80\x93173.\n\t\n
\n\n11\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Of course, individuals do not have a lawful choice not to pay a tax due, and may sometimes face prosecution for failing to do so (although not for declining to make the shared responsibility payment, see \n). But that does not show that the tax restricts the lawful choice whether to undertake or forgo the activity on which the tax is predicated. Those subject to the individual mandate may lawfully forgo health insurance and pay higher taxes, or buy health insurance and pay lower taxes. The only thing they may not lawfully do is not buy health insurance and not pay the resulting tax.\n\t\n
\n\n12\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg observes that state Medicaid spending will increase by only 0.8 percent after the expansion. Post, at 43. That not only ignores increased state administrative expenses, but also assumes that the Federal Government will continue to fund the expansion at the current statutorily specified levels. It is not unheard of, however, for the Federal Government to increase requirements in such a manner as to impose unfunded mandates on the States. More importantly, the size of the new financial burden imposed on a State is irrelevant in analyzing whether the State has been coerced into accepting that burden. \xe2\x80\x9cYour money or your life\xe2\x80\x9d is a coercive proposition, whether you have a single dollar in your pocket or $500.\n\t\n
\n\n13\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Nor, of course, can the number of pages the amendment occu-pies, or the extent to which the change preserves and works withinthe existing program, be dispositive. Cf. post, at 49\xe2\x80\x9350 (opinion of Ginsburg, J.). Take, for example, the following hypothetical amendment: \xe2\x80\x9cAll of a State\xe2\x80\x99s citizens are now eligible for Medicaid.\xe2\x80\x9d That change would take up a single line and would not alter any \xe2\x80\x9coperational aspect[\xc2\xa0] of the program\xe2\x80\x9d beyond the eligibility requirements. Post, at 49. Yet it could hardly be argued that such an amendment was a permissible modification of Medicaid, rather than an attempt to foist an entirely new health care system upon the States.\n\t\n
\n\n14\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg suggests that the States can have no objection to the Medicaid expansion, because \xe2\x80\x9cCongress could have repealed Medicaid [and,] [t]hereafter, .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. could have enacted Medicaid II, a new program combining the pre-2010 coverage with the expanded coverage required by the ACA.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 51; see also post, at 38. But it would certainly not be that easy. Practical constraints would plainly inhibit, if not preclude, the Federal Government from repealing the existing program and putting every feature of Medicaid on the table for political reconsideration. Such a massive undertaking would hardly be \xe2\x80\x9critualistic.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. The same is true of Justice Ginsburg\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that Congress could establish Medicaid as an exclusively federal program. Post, at 44.\n\t\n

\n\t\t\t\t11\xe2\x80\x93400\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tv.\n\t\t\t

\n\t\t\t\tDEPARTMENT OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\ton writs of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the eleventh circuit\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t[June 28, 2012]\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Ginsburg, with whom Justice Sotomayor joins, and with whom Justice Breyer and Justice Kagan join as to Parts I, II, III, and IV, concurring in part, concurring in the judgment in part, and dissenting in part.\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tI agree with The Chief Justice that the Anti-Injunction Act does not bar the Court\xe2\x80\x99s consideration of this case, and that the minimum coverage provision is a proper exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 taxing power. I therefore join Parts I, II, and III\xe2\x80\x93C of The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s opinion. Unlike The Chief Justice, however, I would hold, alternatively, that the Commerce Clause authorizes Congress to enact the minimum coverage provision. I would also hold that the Spending Clause permits the Medicaid expansion exactly as Congress enacted it.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tI\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe provision of health care is today a concern of national dimension, just as the provision of old-age and survivors\xe2\x80\x99 benefits was in the 1930\xe2\x80\x99s. In the Social Secu- rity Act, Congress installed a federal system to provide monthly benefits to retired wage earners and, eventually, to their survivors. Beyond question, Congress could have adopted a similar scheme for health care. Congress chose, instead, to preserve a central role for private insurers and state governments. According to The Chief Justice, the Commerce Clause does not permit that preservation. This rigid reading of the Clause makes scant sense and is stunningly retrogressive. \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSince 1937, our precedent has recognized Congress\xe2\x80\x99 large authority to set the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s course in the economic and social welfare realm. See United States v. Darby, \n\n312 U.\xc2\xa0S. 100, \n115 (1941)\n (overruling Hammer v. Dagenhart, \n\n247 U.\xc2\xa0S. 251 (1918)\n, and recognizing that \xe2\x80\x9cregulations of commerce which do not infringe some constitutional prohibi- tion are within the plenary power conferred on Congress by the Commerce Clause\xe2\x80\x9d); NLRB v. Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp., \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n37 (1937)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[The commerce] power is plenary and may be exerted to protect interstate commerce no matter what the source of the dangers which threaten it.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s crabbed reading of the Commerce Clause harks back to the era in which the Court routinely thwarted Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts to regulate the national economy in the interest of those who labor to sustain it. See, e.g., Railroad Retirement Bd. v. Alton R. Co., \n\n295 U.\xc2\xa0S. 330, \n362, \n368 (1935)\n (invalidating compulsory retirement and pension plan for employees of carriers subject to the Interstate Commerce Act; Court found law related essentially \xe2\x80\x9cto the social welfare of the worker, and therefore remote from any regulation of commerce as such\xe2\x80\x9d). It is a reading that should not have staying power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n
