\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn enacting the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act (ACA), Congress comprehensively reformed the national market for health-care products and services. By any measure, that market is immense. Collectively, Americans spent $2.5 trillion on health care in 2009, accounting for 17.6% of our Nation\xe2\x80\x99s economy. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(B) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). Within the next decade, it is anticipated, spending on health care will nearly double. Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe health-care market\xe2\x80\x99s size is not its only distinctive feature. Unlike the market for almost any other product or service, the market for medical care is one in which all individuals inevitably participate. Virtually every person residing in the United States, sooner or later, will visit a doctor or other health-care professional. See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Center for Health Statistics, Summary Health Statistics for U.\xc2\xa0S. Adults: National Health Interview Survey 2009, Ser. 10, No. 249, p. 124, Table 37 (Dec. 2010) (Over 99.5% of adults above 65 have visited a health-care professional.). Most people will do so repeatedly. See id., at 115, Table 34 (In 2009 alone, 64% of adults made two or more visits to a doctor\xe2\x80\x99s office.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen individuals make those visits, they face another reality of the current market for medical care: its high cost. In 2010, on average, an individual in the United States incurred over $7,000 in health-care expenses. Dept. of Health and Human Services, Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, Historic National Health Expenditure Data, National Health Expenditures: Se- lected Calendar Years 1960\xe2\x80\x932010 (Table 1). Over a lifetime, costs mount to hundreds of thousands of dollars. See Alemayahu & Warner, The Lifetime Distribution of Health Care Costs, in 39 Health Service Research 627, 635 (June 2004). When a person requires nonroutine care, the cost will generally exceed what he or she can afford to pay. A single hospital stay, for instance, typically costs upwards of $10,000. See Dept. of Health and Human Services, Office of Health Policy, ASPE Research Brief: The Value of Health Insurance 5 (May 2011). Treatments for many serious, though not uncommon, conditions similarly cost a substantial sum. Brief for Economic Scholars as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p. 10 (citing a study indicating that, in 1998, the cost of treating a heart attack for the first 90 days exceeded $20,000, while the annual cost of treating certain cancers was more than $50,000). \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAlthough every U.\xc2\xa0S. domiciliary will incur significant medical expenses during his or her lifetime, the time when care will be needed is often unpredictable. An accident, a heart attack, or a cancer diagnosis commonly occurs without warning. Inescapably, we are all at peril of needing medical care without a moment\xe2\x80\x99s notice. See, e.g., Campbell, Down the Insurance Rabbit Hole, N.\xc2\xa0Y. Times, Apr. 5, 2012, p. A23 (telling of an uninsured 32-year-old woman who, healthy one day, became a quadriplegic the next due to an auto accident). \n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTo manage the risks associated with medical care\xe2\x80\x94 its high cost, its unpredictability, and its inevitability\xe2\x80\x94most people in the United States obtain health insurance. Many (approximately 170 million in 2009) are insured by private insurance companies. Others, including those over 65 and certain poor and disabled persons, rely on government-funded insurance programs, notably Medicare and Medicaid. Combined, private health insurers and State and Federal Governments finance almost 85% of the medical care administered to U.\xc2\xa0S. residents. See Congressional Budget Office, CBO\xe2\x80\x99s 2011 Long-Term Budget Outlook 37 (June 2011).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNot all U.\xc2\xa0S. residents, however, have health insurance. In 2009, approximately 50 million people were uninsured, either by choice or, more likely, because they could not afford private insurance and did not qualify for government aid. See Dept. of Commerce, Census Bureau, C. DeNavas-Walt, B. Proctor, & J. Smith, Income, Poverty, and Health Insurance Coverage in the United States: 2009, p.\xc2\xa023, Table 8 (Sept. 2010). As a group, uninsured individuals annually consume more than $100 billion in health- care services, nearly 5% of the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s total. Hidden Health Tax: Americans Pay a Premium 2 (2009), avail- able at http://www.familiesusa.org (all Internet mate- rial as visited June 25, 2012, and included in Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file). Over 60% of those without insurance visit a doctor\xe2\x80\x99s office or emergency room in a given year. See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Cen- ter for Health Statistics, Health\xe2\x80\x94United States\xe2\x80\x942010, p.\xc2\xa0282, Table 79 (Feb. 2011).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe large number of individuals without health insurance, Congress found, heavily burdens the national health-care market. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2). As just noted, the cost of emergency care or treatment for a serious illness generally exceeds what an individual can afford to pay on her own. Unlike markets for most products, however, the inability to pay for care does not mean that an uninsured individual will receive no care. Federal and state law, as well as professional obligations and embedded social norms, require hospitals and physicians to provide care when it is most needed, regardless of the patient\xe2\x80\x99s ability to pay. See, e.g., \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71395dd; Fla. Stat. \xc2\xa7395.1041(3)(f) (2010); Tex. Health & Safety Code Ann. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7311.022(a) and (b) (West 2010); American Medical Association, Council on Ethical and Judicial Affairs, Code of Medical Ethics, Current Opinions: Opinion 8.11\xe2\x80\x94Neglect of Patient, p.\xc2\xa070 (1998\xe2\x80\x931999 ed.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs a consequence, medical-care providers deliver sig- nificant amounts of care to the uninsured for which the providers receive no payment. In 2008, for example, hospi- tals, physicians, and other health-care professionals received no compensation for $43 billion worth of the $116 billion in care they administered to those without insurance. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(F) (2006 ed., Supp. IV).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tHealth-care providers do not absorb these bad debts. Instead, they raise their prices, passing along the cost of uncompensated care to those who do pay reliably: the government and private insurance companies. In response, private insurers increase their premiums, shifting the cost of the elevated bills from providers onto those who carry insurance. The net result: Those with health insurance subsidize the medical care of those without it. As economists would describe what happens, the uninsured \xe2\x80\x9cfree ride\xe2\x80\x9d on those who pay for health insurance.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe size of this subsidy is considerable. Congress found that the cost-shifting just described \xe2\x80\x9cincreases family [insurance] premiums by on average over $1,000 a year.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Higher premiums, in turn, render health insurance less affordable, forcing more people to go without insurance and leading to further cost-shifting.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAnd it is hardly just the currently sick or injured among the uninsured who prompt elevation of the price of health care and health insurance. Insurance companies and health-care providers know that some percentage of healthy, uninsured people will suffer sickness or injury each year and will receive medical care despite their inability to pay. In anticipation of this uncompensated care, health-care companies raise their prices, and insurers their premiums. In other words, because any uninsured person may need medical care at any moment and because health-care companies must account for that risk, every uninsured person impacts the market price of medical care and medical insurance.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe failure of individuals to acquire insurance has other deleterious effects on the health-care market. Because those without insurance generally lack access to preventative care, they do not receive treatment for conditions\xe2\x80\x94like hypertension and diabetes\xe2\x80\x94that can be successfully and affordably treated if diagnosed early on. See Institute of Medicine, National Academies, Insuring America\xe2\x80\x99s Health: Principles and Recommendations 43 (2004). When sickness finally drives the uninsured to seek care, once treatable conditions have escalated into grave health problems, requiring more costly and extensive intervention. Id., at 43\xe2\x80\x9344. The extra time and resources providers spend serving the uninsured lessens the providers\xe2\x80\x99 ability to care for those who do have insurance. See Kliff, High Uninsured Rates Can Kill You\xe2\x80\x94Even if You Have Coverage, Washington Post (May 7, 2012) (describing a study of California\xe2\x80\x99s health-care market which found that, when hospitals divert time and resources to provide uncompensated care, the quality of care the hospitals deliver to those with insurance drops significantly), availa- ble at http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/ezra-klein/post/ high-uninsured-rates-can-kill-you-even-if-you-have-coverage/2012/ 05/07/gIQALNHN8T_print.html.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tStates cannot resolve the problem of the uninsured on their own. Like Social Security benefits, a universal health-care system, if adopted by an individual State, would be \xe2\x80\x9cbait to the needy and dependent elsewhere, encouraging them to migrate and seek a haven of repose.\xe2\x80\x9d Helvering v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 619, \n644 (1937)\n. See also Brief for Commonwealth of Massachusetts as Amicus Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa015 (noting that, in 2009, Massachusetts\xe2\x80\x99 emergency rooms served thousands of uninsured, out-of-state residents). An influx of unhealthy individuals into a State with universal health care would result in increased spending on medical services. To cover the increased costs, a State would have to raise taxes, and private health-insurance companies would have to increase premiums. Higher taxes and increased insurance costs would, in turn, encourage businesses and healthy individuals to leave the State.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tStates that undertake health-care reforms on their own thus risk \xe2\x80\x9cplacing themselves in a position of economic disadvantage as compared with neighbors or competitors.\xe2\x80\x9d Davis, 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 644. See also Brief for Health Care for All, Inc., et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa04 (\xe2\x80\x9c[O]ut-of-state residents continue to seek and receive millions of dollars in uncompensated care in Massachusetts hospitals, limiting the State\xe2\x80\x99s efforts to improve its health care system through the elimination of uncompensated care.\xe2\x80\x9d). Facing that risk, individual States are unlikely to take the initiative in addressing the problem of the uninsured, even though solving that problem is in all States\xe2\x80\x99 best interests. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 intervention was needed to overcome this collective- action impasse.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tD\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAware that a national solution was required, Congress could have taken over the health-insurance market by establishing a tax-and-spend federal program like Social Security. Such a program, commonly referred to as a single-payer system (where the sole payer is the Federal Government), would have left little, if any, room for private enterprise or the States. Instead of going this route, Congress enacted the ACA, a solution that retains a robust role for private insurers and state governments. To make its chosen approach work, however, Congress had to use some new tools, including a requirement that most individuals obtain private health insurance coverage. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (the minimum coverage provision). As explained below, by employing these tools, Congress was able to achieve a practical, alto- gether reasonable, solution.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tA central aim of the ACA is to reduce the number of uninsured U.\xc2\xa0S. residents. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(C) and (I) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). The minimum coverage provision advances this objective by giving potential recipients of health care a financial incentive to acquire insurance. Per the minimum coverage provision, an individual must either obtain insurance or pay a toll constructed as a tax penalty. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A. \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe minimum coverage provision serves a further purpose vital to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 plan to reduce the number of uninsured. Congress knew that encouraging individuals to purchase insurance would not suffice to solve the problem, because most of the uninsured are not uninsured by choice.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Of particular concern to Congress were people who, though desperately in need of insurance, often cannot acquire it: persons who suffer from preexisting medical conditions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBefore the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment, private insurance companies took an applicant\xe2\x80\x99s medical history into account when setting insurance rates or deciding whether to insure an individual. Because individuals with preexisting med- ical conditions cost insurance companies significantly more than those without such conditions, insurers routinely re- fused to insure these individuals, charged them substantially higher premiums, or offered only limited coverage that did not include the preexisting illness. See Dept. of Health and Human Services, Coverage Denied: How the Current Health Insurance System Leaves Millions Behind 1 (2009) (Over the past three years, 12.6 million non- elderly adults were denied insurance coverage or charged higher premiums due to a preexisting condition.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTo ensure that individuals with medical histories have access to affordable insurance, Congress devised a three-part solution. First, Congress imposed a \xe2\x80\x9cguaranteed is- sue\xe2\x80\x9d requirement, which bars insurers from denying coverage to any person on account of that person\xe2\x80\x99s medical condition or history. See 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x931, 300gg\xe2\x80\x933, 300gg\xe2\x80\x934(a) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). Second, Congress required insurers to use \xe2\x80\x9ccommunity rating\xe2\x80\x9d to price their insurance policies. See \xc2\xa7300gg. Community rating, in effect, bars insurance companies from charging higher premiums to those with preexisting conditions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBut these two provisions, Congress comprehended, could not work effectively unless individuals were given a powerful incentive to obtain insurance. See Hearings before the House Ways and Means Committee, 111th Cong., 1st Sess., 10, 13 (2009) (statement of Uwe Reinhardt) (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]m- position of community-rated premiums and guaranteed issue on a market of competing private health insurers will inexorably drive that market into extinction, unless these two features are coupled with .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. a mandate on individual[s] to be insured.\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis in original)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the 1990\xe2\x80\x99s, several States\xe2\x80\x94including New York, New Jersey, Washington, Kentucky, Maine, New Hampshire, and Vermont\xe2\x80\x94enacted guaranteed-issue and community-rating laws without requiring universal acquisition of insurance coverage. The results were disastrous. \xe2\x80\x9cAll seven states suffered from skyrocketing insurance pre- mium costs, reductions in individuals with coverage, and reductions in insurance products and providers.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for American Association of People with Disabilities et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa09 (hereinafter AAPD Brief). See also Brief for Governor of Washington Christine Gregoire as Amicus Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, pp.\xc2\xa011\xe2\x80\x9314 (describing the \xe2\x80\x9cdeath spiral\xe2\x80\x9d in the insurance market Washington experienced when the State passed a law requiring coverage for preexisting conditions).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress comprehended that guaranteed-issue and community-rating laws alone will not work. When insurance companies are required to insure the sick at affordable prices, individuals can wait until they become ill to buy insurance. Pretty soon, those in need of immediate medical care\xe2\x80\x94i.e., those who cost insurers the most\xe2\x80\x94become the insurance companies\xe2\x80\x99 main customers. This \xe2\x80\x9cadverse selection\xe2\x80\x9d problem leaves insurers with two choices: They can either raise premiums dramatically to cover their ever-increasing costs or they can exit the market. In the seven States that tried guaranteed-issue and community-rating requirements without a minimum coverage provision, that is precisely what insurance companies did. See, e.g., AAPD Brief 10 (\xe2\x80\x9c[In Maine,] [m]any insurance providers doubled their premiums in just three years or less.\xe2\x80\x9d); id., at 12 (\xe2\x80\x9cLike New York, Vermont saw substantial increases in premiums after its .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. insurance reform measures took effect in 1993.\xe2\x80\x9d); Hall, An Evaluation of New York\xe2\x80\x99s Reform Law, 25 J. Health Pol. Pol\xe2\x80\x99y & L. 71, 91\xe2\x80\x9392 (2000) (Guaranteed-issue and community-rating laws resulted in a \xe2\x80\x9cdramatic exodus of indemnity insurers from New York\xe2\x80\x99s individual [insurance] market.\xe2\x80\x9d); Brief for Barry Friedman et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa017 (\xe2\x80\x9cIn Kentucky, all but two insurers (one State-run) abandoned the State.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMassachusetts, Congress was told, cracked the adverse selection problem. By requiring most residents to obtain insurance, see Mass. Gen. Laws, ch. 111M, \xc2\xa72 (West 2011), the Commonwealth ensured that insurers would not be left with only the sick as customers. As a result, federal lawmakers observed, Massachusetts succeeded where other States had failed. See Brief for Commonwealth of Massachusetts as Amicus Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa03 (noting that the Commonwealth\xe2\x80\x99s reforms reduced the number of uninsured residents to less than 2%, the lowest rate in the Nation, and cut the amount of uncompensated care by a third); \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(D) (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (noting the success of Massachusetts\xe2\x80\x99 reforms).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t In coupling the minimum coverage provision with guaranteed-issue and community-rating prescriptions, Congress followed Massachusetts\xe2\x80\x99 lead.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn sum, Congress passed the minimum coverage provision as a key component of the ACA to address an economic and social problem that has plagued the Nation for decades: the large number of U.\xc2\xa0S. residents who are unable or unwilling to obtain health insurance. Whatever one thinks of the policy decision Congress made, it was Congress\xe2\x80\x99 prerogative to make it. Reviewed with appropriate deference, the minimum coverage provision, allied to the guaranteed-issue and community-rating prescriptions, should survive measurement under the Commerce and Necessary and Proper Clauses.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Commerce Clause, it is widely acknowledged, \xe2\x80\x9cwas the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 response to the central problem that gave rise to the Constitution itself.\xe2\x80\x9d EEOC v. Wyoming, \n\n460 U.\xc2\xa0S. 226\n, n.\xc2\xa01 (1983) (Stevens, J., concurring) (citing sources). Under the Articles of Confederation, the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s precursor, the regulation of commerce was left to the States. This scheme proved unworkable, because the individual States, understandably focused on their own economic interests, often failed to take actions critical to the success of the Nation as a whole. See Vices of the Political System of the United States, in James Madison: Writings 69, 71, \xc2\xb65 (J. Rakove ed. 1999) (As a result of the \xe2\x80\x9cwant of concert in matters where common interest requires it,\xe2\x80\x9d the \xe2\x80\x9cnational dignity, interest, and reve- nue [have] suffered.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhat was needed was a \xe2\x80\x9cnational Government .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. armed with a positive & compleat authority in all cases where uniform measures are necessary.\xe2\x80\x9d See Letter from James Madison to Edmund Randolph (Apr. 8, 1787), in 9 Papers of James Madison 368, 370 (R. Rutland ed. 1975). See also Letter from George Washington to James Madison (Nov. 30, 1785), in 8 id., at 428, 429 (\xe2\x80\x9cWe are either a United people, or we are not. If the former, let us, in all matters of general concern act as a nation, which ha[s] national objects to promote, and a national character to support.\xe2\x80\x9d). The Framers\xe2\x80\x99 solution was the Commerce Clause, which, as they perceived it, granted Congress the authority to enact economic legislation \xe2\x80\x9cin all Cases for the general Interests of the Union, and also in those Cases to which the States are separately incompetent.\xe2\x80\x9d 2 Records of the Federal Convention of 1787, pp. 131\xe2\x80\x93132, \xc2\xb68 (M. Farrand rev. 1966). See also North American Co. v. SEC, \n\n327 U.\xc2\xa0S. 686, \n705 (1946)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[The commerce power] is an affirmative power commensurate with the national needs.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Framers understood that the \xe2\x80\x9cgeneral Interests of the Union\xe2\x80\x9d would change over time, in ways they could not anticipate. Accordingly, they recognized that the Constitution was of necessity a \xe2\x80\x9cgreat outlin[e],\xe2\x80\x9d not a detailed blueprint, see McCulloch v. Maryland, 4 Wheat. 316, 407 (1819), and that its provisions included broad concepts, to be \xe2\x80\x9cexplained by the context or by the facts of the case,\xe2\x80\x9d Letter from James Madison to N. P. Trist (Dec. 1831), in 9 Writings of James Madison 471, 475 (G. Hunt ed. 1910). \xe2\x80\x9cNothing .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. can be more fallacious,\xe2\x80\x9d Alexander Hamilton emphasized, \xe2\x80\x9cthan to infer the extent of any power, proper to be lodged in the national government, from .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. its immediate necessities. There ought to be a capacity to provide for future contingencies[,] as they may happen; and as these are illimitable in their nature, it is impossible safely to limit that capacity.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No. 34, pp.\xc2\xa0205, 206 (John Harvard Library ed. 2009). See also McCulloch, 4 Wheat., at 415 (The Necessary and Proper Clause is lodged \xe2\x80\x9cin a constitution[,] intended to endure for ages to come, and consequently, to be adapted to the various crises of human affairs.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tConsistent with the Framers\xe2\x80\x99 intent, we have repeatedly emphasized that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority under the Commerce Clause is dependent upon \xe2\x80\x9cpractical\xe2\x80\x9d considerations, including \xe2\x80\x9cactual experience.\xe2\x80\x9d Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp., 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 41\xe2\x80\x9342; see Wickard v. Filburn, \n\n317 U.\xc2\xa0S. 111, \n122 (1942)\n; United States v. Lopez, \n\n514 U.\xc2\xa0S. 549, \n573 (1995)\n (Kennedy, J., concurring) (emphasizing \xe2\x80\x9cthe Court\xe2\x80\x99s definitive commitment to the practical conception of the commerce power\xe2\x80\x9d). See also North American Co., 327 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 705 (\xe2\x80\x9cCommerce itself is an intensely practical matter. To deal with it effectively, Congress must be able to act in terms of economic and financial realities.\xe2\x80\x9d (citation omitted)). We afford Congress the leeway \xe2\x80\x9cto undertake to solve national problems directly and realistically.\xe2\x80\x9d American Power & Light Co. v. SEC, \n\n329 U.\xc2\xa0S. 90, \n103 (1946)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tUntil today, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s pragmatic approach to judging whether Congress validly exercised its commerce power was guided by two familiar principles. First, Congress has the power to regulate economic activities \xe2\x80\x9cthat substantially affect interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Gonzales v. Raich, \n\n545 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n17 (2005)\n. This capacious power extends even to local activities that, viewed in the aggregate, have a substantial impact on interstate commerce. See ibid. See also Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 125 (\xe2\x80\x9c[E]ven if appellee\xe2\x80\x99s activ- ity be local and though it may not be regarded as commerce, it may still, whatever its nature, be reached by Congress if it exerts a substantial economic effect on interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)); Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp., 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 37.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, we owe a large measure of respect to Congress when it frames and enacts economic and social legislation. See Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 17. See also Pension Benefit Guaranty Corporation v. R.\xc2\xa0A. Gray & Co., \n\n467 U.\xc2\xa0S. 717, \n729 (1984)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[S]trong deference [is] accorded legislation in the field of national economic policy.\xe2\x80\x9d); Hodel v. Indiana, \n\n452 U.\xc2\xa0S. 314, \n326 (1981)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cThis [C]ourt will certainly not substitute its judgment for that of Congress unless the relation of the subject to interstate commerce and its ef- fect upon it are clearly non-existent.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). When appraising such legislation, we ask only (1) whether Congress had a \xe2\x80\x9crational basis\xe2\x80\x9d for concluding that the regulated activity substantially affects interstate commerce, and (2) whether there is a \xe2\x80\x9creasonable connection between the regulatory means selected and the asserted ends.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 323\xe2\x80\x93324. See also Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 22; Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 557; Hodel v. Virginia Surface Mining & Reclamation Assn., Inc., \n\n452 U.\xc2\xa0S. 264, \n277 (1981)\n; Katzenbach v. McClung, \n\n379 U.\xc2\xa0S. 294, \n303 (1964)\n; Heart of Atlanta Motel, Inc. v. United States, \n\n379 U.\xc2\xa0S. 241, \n258 (1964)\n; United States v. Carolene Products Co., \n\n304 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144\n\xe2\x80\x93153 (1938). In answering these questions, we presume the statute under review is constitutional and may strike it down only on a \xe2\x80\x9cplain showing\xe2\x80\x9d that Congress acted irrationally. United States v. Morrison, \n\n529 U.\xc2\xa0S. 598, \n607 (2000)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tStraightforward application of these principles would require the Court to hold that the minimum coverage provision is proper Commerce Clause legislation. Beyond dispute, Congress had a rational basis for concluding that the uninsured, as a class, substantially affect interstate commerce. Those without insurance consume billions of dollars of health-care products and services each year. See supra, at 5. Those goods are produced, sold, and delivered largely by national and regional companies who routinely transact business across state lines. The uninsured also cross state lines to receive care. Some have medical emergencies while away from home. Others, when sick, go to a neighboring State that provides better care for those who have not prepaid for care. See supra, at 7\xe2\x80\x938.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNot only do those without insurance consume a large amount of health care each year; critically, as earlier explained, their inability to pay for a significant portion of that consumption drives up market prices, foists costs on other consumers, and reduces market efficiency and stability. See supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x937. Given these far-reaching effects on interstate commerce, the decision to forgo insurance is hardly inconsequential or equivalent to \xe2\x80\x9cdoing nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 20; it is, instead, an economic decision Congress has the authority to address under the Commerce Clause. See supra, at 14\xe2\x80\x9316. See also Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 128 (\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well established by decisions of this Court that the power to regulate commerce includes the power to regulate the prices at which commodities in that commerce are dealt in and practices affecting such prices.\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe minimum coverage provision, furthermore, bears a \xe2\x80\x9creasonable connection\xe2\x80\x9d to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 goal of protecting the health-care market from the disruption caused by individuals who fail to obtain insurance. By requiring those who do not carry insurance to pay a toll, the minimum coverage provision gives individuals a strong incentive to insure. This incentive, Congress had good reason to believe, would reduce the number of uninsured and, correspondingly, mitigate the adverse impact the uninsured have on the national health-care market.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress also acted reasonably in requiring uninsured individuals, whether sick or healthy, either to obtain insurance or to pay the specified penalty. As earlier observed, because every person is at risk of needing care at any moment, all those who lack insurance, regardless of their current health status, adversely affect the price of health care and health insurance. See supra, at 6\xe2\x80\x937. Moreover, an insurance-purchase requirement limited to those in need of immediate care simply could not work. Insurance companies would either charge these individuals prohibitively expensive premiums, or, if community-rating regulations were in place, close up shop. See supra, at 9\xe2\x80\x9311. See also Brief for State of Maryland and 10 Other States et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, p.\xc2\xa028 (hereinafter Maryland Brief) (\xe2\x80\x9cNo insurance regime can survive if people can opt out when the risk insured against is only a risk, but opt in when the risk materializes.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\xe2\x80\x9c[W]here we find that the legislators .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. have a rational basis for finding a chosen regulatory scheme necessary to the protection of commerce, our investigation is at an end.\xe2\x80\x9d Katzenbach, 379 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 303\xe2\x80\x93304. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 enactment of the minimum coverage provision, which addresses a specific interstate problem in a practical, experience-informed manner, easily meets this criterion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tD\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tRather than evaluating the constitutionality of the minimum coverage provision in the manner established by our precedents, The Chief Justice relies on a newly minted constitutional doctrine. The commerce power does not, The Chief Justice announces, permit Congress to \xe2\x80\x9ccompe[l] individuals to become active in commerce by purchasing a product.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 20 (emphasis deleted).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\ta\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s novel constraint on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 commerce power gains no force from our precedent and for that reason alone warrants disapprobation. See infra, at 23\xe2\x80\x9327. But even assuming, for the moment, that Congress lacks authority under the Commerce Clause to \xe2\x80\x9ccompel individuals not engaged in commerce to purchase an unwanted product,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 18, such a limitation would be inapplicable here. Everyone will, at some point, consume health-care products and services. See supra, at 3. Thus, if The Chief Justice is correct that an insurance-purchase requirement can be applied only to those who \xe2\x80\x9cactively\xe2\x80\x9d consume health care, the minimum coverage provision fits the bill.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice does not dispute that all U.\xc2\xa0S. residents participate in the market for health services over the course of their lives. See ante, at 16 (\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone will eventually need health care at a time and to an extent they cannot predict.\xe2\x80\x9d). But, The Chief Justice insists, the uninsured cannot be considered active in the market for health care, because \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he proximity and degree of connection between the [uninsured today] and [their] subsequent commercial activity is too lacking.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 27.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis argument has multiple flaws. First, more than 60% of those without insurance visit a hospital or doctor\xe2\x80\x99s office each year. See supra, at 5. Nearly 90% will within five years.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t An uninsured\xe2\x80\x99s consumption of health care is thus quite proximate: It is virtually certain to occur in the next five years and more likely than not to occur this year.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tEqually evident, Congress has no way of separating those uninsured individuals who will need emergency medi- cal care today (surely their consumption of medical care is sufficiently imminent) from those who will not need medical services for years to come. No one knows when an emergency will occur, yet emergencies involving the uninsured arise daily. To capture individuals who unexpect- edly will obtain medical care in the very near future, then, Congress needed to include individuals who will not go to a doctor anytime soon. Congress, our decisions instruct, has authority to cast its net that wide. See Perez v. United States, \n\n402 U.\xc2\xa0S. 146, \n154 (1971)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[W]hen it is necessary in order to prevent an evil to make the law embrace more than the precise thing to be prevented it may do so.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, it is Congress\xe2\x80\x99 role, not the Court\xe2\x80\x99s, to delineate the boundaries of the market the Legislature seeks to regulate. The Chief Justice defines the health-care mar- ket as including only those transactions that will occur either in the next instant or within some (unspecified) proximity to the next instant. But Congress could reasonably have viewed the market from a long-term perspective, encompassing all transactions virtually certain to occur over the next decade, see supra, at 19, not just those occurring here and now.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThird, contrary to The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s contention, our precedent does indeed support \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he proposition that Congress may dictate the conduct of an individual today because of prophesied future activity.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 26. In Wickard, the Court upheld a penalty the Federal Government imposed on a farmer who grew more wheat than he was permitted to grow under the Agricultural Adjustment Act of 1938 (AAA). 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 114\xe2\x80\x93115. He could not be penalized, the farmer argued, as he was growing the wheat for home consumption, not for sale on the open market. Id., at 119. The Court rejected this argument. Id., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129. Wheat intended for home consumption, the Court noted, \xe2\x80\x9coverhangs the market, and if induced by rising prices, tends to flow into the market and check price increases [intended by the AAA].\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 128.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSimilar reasoning supported the Court\xe2\x80\x99s judgment in Raich, which upheld Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to regulate marijuana grown for personal use. 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 19. Homegrown marijuana substantially affects the interstate mar- ket for marijuana, we observed, for \xe2\x80\x9cthe high demand in the interstate market will [likely] draw such marijuana into that market.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur decisions thus acknowledge Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority, under the Commerce Clause, to direct the conduct of an individual today (the farmer in Wickard, stopped from growing excess wheat; the plaintiff in Raich, ordered to cease cultivating marijuana) because of a prophesied future transaction (the eventual sale of that wheat or marijuana in the interstate market). Congress\xe2\x80\x99 actions are even more rational in this case, where the future activity (the consumption of medical care) is certain to occur, the sole uncertainty being the time the activity will take place.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMaintaining that the uninsured are not active in the health-care market, The Chief Justice draws an analogy to the car market. An individual \xe2\x80\x9cis not \xe2\x80\x98active in the car market,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d The Chief Justice observes, simply because he or she may someday buy a car. Ante, at 25. The analogy is inapt. The inevitable yet unpredictable need for medical care and the guarantee that emergency care will be provided when required are conditions nonexistent in other markets. That is so of the market for cars, and of the market for broccoli as well. Although an individual might buy a car or a crown of broccoli one day, there is no certainty she will ever do so. And if she eventually wants a car or has a craving for broccoli, she will be obliged to pay at the counter before receiving the vehicle or nourishment. She will get no free ride or food, at the expense of another consumer forced to pay an inflated price. See Thomas More Law Center v. Obama, 651 F.\xc2\xa03d 529, 565 (CA6 2011) (Sutton, J., concurring in part) (\xe2\x80\x9cRegulating how citizens pay for what they already receive (health care), never quite know when they will need, and in the case of severe illnesses or emergencies generally will not be able to afford, has few (if any) parallels in modern life.\xe2\x80\x9d). Upholding the minimum coverage provision on the ground that all are participants or will be participants in the health-care market would therefore carry no implication that Congress may justify under the Commerce Clause a mandate to buy other products and services.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNor is it accurate to say that the minimum coverage provision \xe2\x80\x9ccompel[s] individuals .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. to purchase an unwanted product,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 18, or \xe2\x80\x9csuite of products,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 11, n.\xc2\xa02 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.). If unwanted today, medical service secured by insurance may be desperately needed tomorrow. Virtually everyone, I reiterate, consumes health care at some point in his or her life. See supra, at 3. Health insurance is a means of paying for this care, nothing more. In requiring individuals to obtain insurance, Congress is therefore not mandating the purchase of a discrete, unwanted product. Rather, Congress is merely defining the terms on which individuals pay for an interstate good they consume: Persons subject to the mandate must now pay for medical care in advance (instead of at the point of service) and through insurance (instead of out of pocket). Establishing payment terms for goods in or affecting interstate commerce is quintessential economic regulation well within Congress\xe2\x80\x99 domain. See, e.g., United States v. Wrightwood Dairy Co., \n\n315 U.\xc2\xa0S. 110, \n118 (1942)\n. Cf. post, at 13 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.) (recognizing that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Federal Government can prescribe [a commodity\xe2\x80\x99s] quality .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. and even [its price]\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice also calls the minimum coverage provision an illegitimate effort to make young, healthy individuals subsidize insurance premiums paid by the less hale and hardy. See ante, at 17, 25\xe2\x80\x9326. This complaint, too, is spurious. Under the current health-care system, healthy persons who lack insurance receive a benefit for which they do not pay: They are assured that, if they need it, emergency medical care will be available, although they cannot afford it. See supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x936. Those who have insurance bear the cost of this guarantee. See ibid. By requiring the healthy uninsured to obtain insurance or pay a penalty structured as a tax, the minimum coverage provision ends the free ride these individuals currently enjoy.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the fullness of time, moreover, today\xe2\x80\x99s young and healthy will become society\xe2\x80\x99s old and infirm. Viewed over a lifespan, the costs and benefits even out: The young who pay more than their fair share currently will pay less than their fair share when they become senior citizens. And even if, as undoubtedly will be the case, some individuals, over their lifespans, will pay more for health insurance than they receive in health services, they have little to complain about, for that is how insurance works. Every insured person receives protection against a catastrophic loss, even though only a subset of the covered class will ultimately need that protection.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tb\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn any event, The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s limitation of the commerce power to the regulation of those actively engaged in commerce finds no home in the text of the Constitution or our decisions. Article I, \xc2\xa78, of the Constitution grants Congress the power \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o regulate Commerce .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. among the several States.\xe2\x80\x9d Nothing in this language im- plies that Congress\xe2\x80\x99 commerce power is limited to regu- lating those actively engaged in commercial transactions. Indeed, as the D.\xc2\xa0C. Circuit observed, \xe2\x80\x9c[a]t the time the Constitution was [framed], to \xe2\x80\x98regulate\xe2\x80\x99 meant,\xe2\x80\x9d among other things, \xe2\x80\x9cto require action.\xe2\x80\x9d See Seven-Sky v. Holder, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d 1, 16 (2011).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tArguing to the contrary, The Chief Justice notes that \xe2\x80\x9cthe Constitution gives Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x98coin Money,\xe2\x80\x99 in addition to the power to \xe2\x80\x98regulate the Value thereof,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and similarly \xe2\x80\x9cgives Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x98raise and support Armies\xe2\x80\x99 and to \xe2\x80\x98provide and maintain a Navy,\xe2\x80\x99 in addition to the power to \xe2\x80\x98make Rules for the Government and Regulation of the land and naval Forces.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 18\xe2\x80\x9319 (citing Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cls. 5, 12\xe2\x80\x9314). In separating the power to regulate from the power to bring the subject of the regulation into existence, The Chief Justice asserts, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he language of the Constitution reflects the natural understanding that the power to regulate assumes there is already something to be regulated.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 19.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis argument is difficult to fathom. Requiring individuals to obtain insurance unquestionably regulates the inter- state health-insurance and health-care markets, both of them in existence well before the enactment of the ACA. See Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 128 (\xe2\x80\x9cThe stimulation of commerce is a use of the regulatory function quite as definitely as prohibitions or restrictions thereon.\xe2\x80\x9d). Thus, the \xe2\x80\x9csomething to be regulated\xe2\x80\x9d was surely there when Congress created the minimum coverage provision.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNor does our case law toe the activity versus inactiv- ity line. In Wickard, for example, we upheld the penalty imposed on a farmer who grew too much wheat, even though the regulation had the effect of compelling farmers to purchase wheat in the open market. Id., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129. \xe2\x80\x9c[F]orcing some farmers into the market to buy what they could provide for themselves\xe2\x80\x9d was, the Court held, a valid means of regulating commerce. Id., at 128\xe2\x80\x93129. In an- other context, this Court similarly upheld Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority under the commerce power to compel an \xe2\x80\x9cinactive\xe2\x80\x9d land- holder to submit to an unwanted sale. See Monongahela Nav. Co. v. United States, \n\n148 U.\xc2\xa0S. 312\n\xe2\x80\x93337 (1893) (\xe2\x80\x9c[U]pon the [great] power to regulate commerce[,]\xe2\x80\x9d Congress has the authority to mandate the sale of real prop- erty to the Government, where the sale is essential to the improvement of a navigable waterway (emphasis added)); Cherokee Nation v. Southern Kansas R. Co., \n\n135 U.\xc2\xa0S. 641\n\xe2\x80\x93659 (1890) (similar reliance on the commerce power regarding mandated sale of private property for railroad construction).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn concluding that the Commerce Clause does not permit Congress to regulate commercial \xe2\x80\x9cinactivity,\xe2\x80\x9d and there- fore does not allow Congress to adopt the practical solution it devised for the health-care problem, The Chief Justice views the Clause as a \xe2\x80\x9ctechnical legal conception,\xe2\x80\x9d precisely what our case law tells us not to do. Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 122 (internal quotation marks omitted). See also supra, at 14\xe2\x80\x9316. This Court\xe2\x80\x99s former endeavors to impose categorical limits on the commerce power have not fared well. In several pre-New Deal cases, the Court attempted to cabin Congress\xe2\x80\x99 Commerce Clause authority by distinguishing \xe2\x80\x9ccommerce\xe2\x80\x9d from activity once conceived to be noncommercial, notably, \xe2\x80\x9cproduction,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cmining,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cmanufacturing.\xe2\x80\x9d See, e.g., United States v. E. C. Knight Co., \n\n156 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n12 (1895)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cCommerce succeeds to manufacture, and is not a part of it.\xe2\x80\x9d); Carter v. Carter Coal Co., \n\n298 U.\xc2\xa0S. 238, \n304 (1936)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cMining brings the subject matter of commerce into existence. Commerce disposes of it.\xe2\x80\x9d). The Court also sought to distinguish activities having a \xe2\x80\x9cdirect\xe2\x80\x9d effect on interstate commerce, and for that reason, subject to federal regulation, from those having only an \xe2\x80\x9cindirect\xe2\x80\x9d effect, and therefore not amenable to federal control. See, e.g., A. L. A. Schechter Poultry Corp. v. United States, \n\n295 U.\xc2\xa0S. 495, \n548 (1935)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he dis- tinction between direct and indirect effects of intrastate transactions upon interstate commerce must be recognized as a fundamental one.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThese line-drawing exercises were untenable, and the Court long ago abandoned them. \xe2\x80\x9c[Q]uestions of the power of Congress [under the Commerce Clause],\xe2\x80\x9d we held in Wickard, \xe2\x80\x9care not to be decided by reference to any for- mula which would give controlling force to nomenclature such as \xe2\x80\x98production\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98indirect\xe2\x80\x99 and foreclose consideration of the actual effects of the activity in question upon interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 120. See also Morrison, 529 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 641\xe2\x80\x93644 (Souter, J., dissenting) (recounting the Court\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cnearly disastrous experiment\xe2\x80\x9d with formalistic limits on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 commerce power). Failing to learn from this history, The Chief Justice plows ahead with his formalistic distinction between those who are \xe2\x80\x9cactive in commerce,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 20, and those who are not.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIt is not hard to show the difficulty courts (and Congress) would encounter in distinguishing statutes that reg- ulate \xe2\x80\x9cactivity\xe2\x80\x9d from those that regulate \xe2\x80\x9cinactivity.\xe2\x80\x9d As Judge Easterbrook noted, \xe2\x80\x9cit is possible to restate most actions as corresponding inactions with the same effect.\xe2\x80\x9d Archie v. Racine, 847 F.\xc2\xa02d 1211, 1213 (CA7 1988) (en banc). Take this case as an example. An individual who opts not to purchase insurance from a private insurer can be seen as actively selecting another form of insurance: self-insurance. See Thomas More Law Center, 651 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 561 (Sutton, J., concurring in part) (\xe2\x80\x9cNo one is in- active when deciding how to pay for health care, as self-insurance and private insurance are two forms of action for addressing the same risk.\xe2\x80\x9d). The minimum coverage provision could therefore be described as regulating activists in the self-insurance market.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Wickard is another example. Did the statute there at issue target activity (the growing of too much wheat) or inactivity (the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s failure to purchase wheat in the marketplace)? If anything, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s analysis suggested the latter. See 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAt bottom, The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s and the joint dissenters\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cview that an individual cannot be subject to Commerce Clause regulation absent voluntary, affirmative acts that enter him or her into, or affect, the interstate mar- ket expresses a concern for individual liberty that [is] more redolent of Due Process Clause arguments.\xe2\x80\x9d Seven-Sky, 661 F.\xc2\xa03d, at 19. See also Troxel v. Granville, \n\n530 U.\xc2\xa0S. 57, \n65 (2000)\n (plurality opinion) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe [Due Process] Clause also includes a substantive component that provides heightened protection against government interference with certain fundamental rights and liberty interests.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). Plaintiffs have abandoned any argument pinned to substantive due process, however, see 648 F.\xc2\xa03d 1235, 1291, n. 93 (CA11 2011), and now concede that the provisions here at issue do not offend the Due Process Clause.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tUnderlying The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s view that the Commerce Clause must be confined to the regulation of active participants in a commercial market is a fear that the commerce power would otherwise know no limits. See, e.g., ante, at 23 (Allowing Congress to compel an individ- ual not engaged in commerce to purchase a product would \xe2\x80\x9cpermi[t] Congress to reach beyond the natural extent of its authority, everywhere extending the sphere of its activity, and drawing all power into its impetuous vortex.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). The joint dissenters express a similar apprehension. See post, at 8 (If the minimum coverage provision is upheld under the commerce power then \xe2\x80\x9cthe Commerce Clause becomes a font of unlimited power, .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. the hideous monster whose devouring jaws .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. spare neither sex nor age, nor high nor low, nor sacred nor profane.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). This concern is unfounded.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFirst, The Chief Justice could certainly uphold the individual mandate without giving Congress carte blanche to enact any and all purchase mandates. As several times noted, the unique attributes of the health-care market render everyone active in that market and give rise to a significant free-riding problem that does not occur in other markets. See supra, at 3\xe2\x80\x937, 16\xe2\x80\x9318, 21.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNor would the commerce power be unbridled, absent The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cactivity\xe2\x80\x9d limitation. Congress would remain unable to regulate noneconomic conduct that has only an attenuated effect on interstate commerce and is traditionally left to state law. See Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 567; Morrison, 529 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 617\xe2\x80\x93619. In Lopez, for example, the Court held that the Federal Government lacked power, under the Commerce Clause, to criminalize the possession of a gun in a local school zone. Possessing a gun near a school, the Court reasoned, \xe2\x80\x9cis in no sense an economic activity that might, through repetition elsewhere, substantially affect any sort of interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 567; ibid. (noting that the Court would have \xe2\x80\x9cto pile inference upon inference\xe2\x80\x9d to conclude that gun possession has a substantial effect on commerce). Relying on similar logic, the Court concluded in Morrison that Congress could not regulate gender-motivated violence, which the Court deemed to have too \xe2\x80\x9cattenuated [an] effect upon interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d 529 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 615.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAn individual\xe2\x80\x99s decision to self-insure, I have explained, is an economic act with the requisite connection to interstate commerce. See supra, at 16\xe2\x80\x9317. Other choices individuals make are unlikely to fit the same or similar description. As an example of the type of regulation he fears, The Chief Justice cites a Government mandate to purchase green vegetables. Ante, at 22\xe2\x80\x9323. One could call this concern \xe2\x80\x9cthe broccoli horrible.\xe2\x80\x9d Congress, The Chief Justice posits, might adopt such a mandate, reasoning that an individual\xe2\x80\x99s failure to eat a healthy diet, like the failure to purchase health insurance, imposes costs on others. See ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tConsider the chain of inferences the Court would have to accept to conclude that a vegetable-purchase mandate was likely to have a substantial effect on the health-care costs borne by lithe Americans. The Court would have to believe that individuals forced to buy vegetables would then eat them (instead of throwing or giving them away), would prepare the vegetables in a healthy way (steamed or raw, not deep-fried), would cut back on unhealthy foods, and would not allow other factors (such as lack of exercise or little sleep) to trump the improved diet.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Such \xe2\x80\x9cpil[ing of] inference upon inference\xe2\x80\x9d is just what the Court refused to do in Lopez and Morrison.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOther provisions of the Constitution also check congressional overreaching. A mandate to purchase a particu- lar product would be unconstitutional if, for example, the edict impermissibly abridged the freedom of speech, interfered with the free exercise of religion, or infringed on a liberty interest protected by the Due Process Clause.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSupplementing these legal restraints is a formidable check on congressional power: the democratic process. See Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 33; Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 120 (repeating Chief Justice Marshall\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cwarning that effective restraints on [the commerce power\xe2\x80\x99s] exercise must proceed from political rather than judicial processes\xe2\x80\x9d (citing Gibbons v. Ogden, 9 Wheat. 1, 197 (1824)). As the controversy surrounding the passage of the Affordable Care Act attests, purchase mandates are likely to engender political resistance. This prospect is borne out by the behavior of state legislators. Despite their possession of unquestioned authority to impose mandates, state governments have rarely done so. See Hall, Commerce Clause Challenges to Health Care Reform, 159 U.\xc2\xa0Pa. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1825, 1838 (2011).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen contemplated in its extreme, almost any power looks dangerous. The commerce power, hypothetically, would enable Congress to prohibit the purchase and home production of all meat, fish, and dairy goods, effectively compelling Americans to eat only vegetables. Cf. Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 9; Wickard, 317 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 127\xe2\x80\x93129. Yet no one would offer the \xe2\x80\x9chypothetical and unreal possibilit[y],\xe2\x80\x9d Pullman Co. v. Knott, \n\n235 U.\xc2\xa0S. 23, \n26 (1914)\n, of a vegetarian state as a credible reason to deny Congress the authority ever to ban the possession and sale of goods. The Chief Justice accepts just such specious logic when he cites the broccoli horrible as a reason to deny Congress the power to pass the individual mandate. Cf. R. Bork, The Tempting of America 169 (1990) (\xe2\x80\x9cJudges and lawyers live on the slippery slope of analogies; they are not supposed to ski it to the bottom.\xe2\x80\x9d). But see, e.g., post, at 3 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.) (asserting, outlandishly, that if the minimum coverage provision is sustained, then Congress could make \xe2\x80\x9cbreathing in and out the basis for federal prescription\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTo bolster his argument that the minimum coverage provision is not valid Commerce Clause legislation, The Chief Justice emphasizes the provision\xe2\x80\x99s novelty. See ante, at 18 (asserting that \xe2\x80\x9csometimes the most telling indication of [a] severe constitutional problem .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. is the lack of historical precedent for Congress\xe2\x80\x99s action\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)). While an insurance-purchase mandate may be novel, The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s argument certainly is not. \xe2\x80\x9c[I]n almost every instance of the exer- cise of the [commerce] power differences are asserted from previous exercises of it and made a ground of attack.\xe2\x80\x9d Hoke v. United States, \n\n227 U.\xc2\xa0S. 308, \n320 (1913)\n. See, e.g., Brief for Petitioner in Perez v. United States, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1970, No. 600, p.\xc2\xa05 (\xe2\x80\x9cunprecedented exercise of power\xe2\x80\x9d); Sup- plemental Brief for Appellees in Katzenbach v. McClung, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1964, No. 543, p.\xc2\xa040 (\xe2\x80\x9cnovel assertion of federal power\xe2\x80\x9d); Brief for Appellee in Wickard v. Filburn, O.\xc2\xa0T. 1941, No. 59, p.\xc2\xa06 (\xe2\x80\x9ccomplete departure\xe2\x80\x9d). For decades, the Court has declined to override legislation because of its novelty, and for good reason. As our national economy grows and changes, we have recognized, Congress must adapt to the changing \xe2\x80\x9ceconomic and financial realities.\xe2\x80\x9d See supra, at 14\xe2\x80\x9315. Hindering Congress\xe2\x80\x99 ability to do so is shortsighted; if history is any guide, today\xe2\x80\x99s constriction of the Commerce Clause will not endure. See supra, at 25\xe2\x80\x9326.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor the reasons explained above, the minimum coverage provision is valid Commerce Clause legislation. See supra, Part II. When viewed as a component of the entire ACA, the provision\xe2\x80\x99s constitutionality becomes even plainer.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Necessary and Proper Clause \xe2\x80\x9cempowers Congress to enact laws in effectuation of its [commerce] powe[r] that are not within its authority to enact in isolation.\xe2\x80\x9d Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 39 (Scalia, J., concurring in judgment). Hence, \xe2\x80\x9c[a] complex regulatory program .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. can survive a Commerce Clause challenge without a showing that every single facet of the program is independently and directly related to a valid congressional goal.\xe2\x80\x9d Indiana, 452 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 329, n.\xc2\xa017. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is enough that the challenged provisions are an integral part of the regulatory program and that the regulatory scheme when considered as a whole satisfies this test.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (collecting cases). See also Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 24\xe2\x80\x9325 (A challenged statutory provision fits within Congress\xe2\x80\x99 commerce authority if it is an \xe2\x80\x9cessential par[t] of a larger regulation of economic activity,\xe2\x80\x9d such that, in the absence of the provision, \xe2\x80\x9cthe regulatory scheme could be undercut.\xe2\x80\x9d (quoting Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 561)); Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 37 (Scalia, J., concurring in judgment) (\xe2\x80\x9cCongress may regulate even noneconomic local activity if that regulation is a necessary part of a more general regulation of interstate commerce. The relevant question is simply whether the means chosen are \xe2\x80\x98reasonably adapted\xe2\x80\x99 to the attainment of a legitimate end under the commerce power.\xe2\x80\x9d (citation omitted)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tRecall that one of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 goals in enacting the Affordable Care Act was to eliminate the insurance industry\xe2\x80\x99s practice of charging higher prices or denying coverage to individuals with preexisting medical conditions. See supra, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310. The commerce power allows Congress to ban this practice, a point no one disputes. See United States v. South-Eastern Underwriters Assn., \n\n322 U.\xc2\xa0S. 533\n\xe2\x80\x93553 (1944) (Congress may regulate \xe2\x80\x9cthe methods by which interstate insurance companies do business.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress knew, however, that simply barring insurance companies from relying on an applicant\xe2\x80\x99s medical history would not work in practice. Without the individual mandate, Congress learned, guaranteed-issue and community-rating requirements would trigger an adverse-selection death-spiral in the health-insurance market: Insurance premiums would skyrocket, the number of uninsured would increase, and insurance companies would exit the market. See supra, at 10\xe2\x80\x9311. When complemented by an insurance mandate, on the other hand, guaranteed issue and community rating would work as intended, increasing access to insurance and reducing uncompensated care. See supra, at 11\xe2\x80\x9312. The minimum coverage provision is thus an \xe2\x80\x9cessential par[t] of a larger regulation of economic activity\xe2\x80\x9d; without the provision, \xe2\x80\x9cthe regulatory scheme [w]ould be undercut.\xe2\x80\x9d Raich, 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 24\xe2\x80\x9325 (inter- nal quotation marks omitted). Put differently, the minimum coverage provision, together with the guaranteed-issue and community-rating requirements, is \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98reasonably adapted\xe2\x80\x99 to the attainment of a legitimate end under the commerce power\xe2\x80\x9d: the elimination of pricing and sales practices that take an applicant\xe2\x80\x99s medical history into account. See id., at 37 (Scalia, J., concurring in judgment).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAsserting that the Necessary and Proper Clause does not authorize the minimum coverage provision, The Chief Justice focuses on the word \xe2\x80\x9cproper.\xe2\x80\x9d A mandate to purchase health insurance is not \xe2\x80\x9cproper\xe2\x80\x9d legislation, The Chief Justice urges, because the command \xe2\x80\x9cundermine[s] the structure of government established by the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 28. If long on rhetoric, The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s argument is short on substance.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice cites only two cases in which this Court concluded that a federal statute impermissibly transgressed the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s boundary between state and federal authority: Printz v. United States, \n\n521 U.\xc2\xa0S. 898 (1997)\n, and New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144 (1992)\n. See ante, at 29. The statutes at issue in both cases, however, compelled state officials to act on the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s behalf. 521 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 925\xe2\x80\x93933 (holding unconstitutional a statute obligating state law enforcement officers to implement a federal gun-control law); New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 176\xe2\x80\x93177 (striking down a statute requiring state legislators to pass regulations pursuant to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 instructions). \xe2\x80\x9c[Federal] laws conscripting state officers,\xe2\x80\x9d the Court reasoned, \xe2\x80\x9cviolate state sovereignty and are thus not in accord with the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Printz, 521 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 925, 935; New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 176.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe minimum coverage provision, in contrast, acts \xe2\x80\x9cdirectly upon individuals, without employing the States as intermediaries.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 164. The provision is thus entirely consistent with the Consti- tution\xe2\x80\x99s design. See Printz, 521 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 920 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Framers explicitly chose a Constitution that confers upon Congress the power to regulate individuals, not States.\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks omitted)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tLacking case law support for his holding, The Chief Justice nevertheless declares the minimum coverage provision not \xe2\x80\x9cproper\xe2\x80\x9d because it is less \xe2\x80\x9cnarrow in scope\xe2\x80\x9d than other laws this Court has upheld under the Necessary and Proper Clause. Ante, at 29 (citing United States v. Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___ (2010); Sabri v. United States, \n\n541 U.\xc2\xa0S. 600 (2004)\n; Jinks v. Richland County, \n\n538 U.\xc2\xa0S. 456 (2003)\n). The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on cases in which this Court has affirmed Congress\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cbroad authority to enact federal legislation\xe2\x80\x9d under the Necessary and Proper Clause, Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S., at ___ (slip op., at 5), is underwhelming.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNor does The Chief Justice pause to explain why the power to direct either the purchase of health insurance or, alternatively, the payment of a penalty collectible as a tax is more far-reaching than other implied powers this Court has found meet under the Necessary and Proper Clause. These powers include the power to enact criminal laws, see, e.g., United States v. Fox, \n\n95 U.\xc2\xa0S. 670, \n672 (1878)\n; the power to imprison, including civil imprisonment, see, e.g., Comstock, 560 U.\xc2\xa0S., at ___ (slip op., at 1); and the power to create a national bank, see McCulloch, 4 Wheat., at 425. See also Jinks, 538 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 463 (affirming Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to alter the way a state law is applied in state court, where the alteration \xe2\x80\x9cpromotes fair and efficient operation of the federal courts\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn failing to explain why the individual mandate threatens our constitutional order, The Chief Justice disserves future courts. How is a judge to decide, when ruling on the constitutionality of a federal statute, whether Congress employed an \xe2\x80\x9cindependent power,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 28, or merely a \xe2\x80\x9cderivative\xe2\x80\x9d one, ante, at 29. Whether the power used is \xe2\x80\x9csubstantive,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 30, or just \xe2\x80\x9cincidental,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 29? The instruction The Chief Justice, in effect, provides lower courts: You will know it when you see it.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIt is more than exaggeration to suggest that the minimum coverage provision improperly intrudes on \xe2\x80\x9cessential attributes of state sovereignty.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (internal quotation marks omitted). First, the Affordable Care Act does not operate \xe2\x80\x9cin [an] are[a] such as criminal law enforcement or education where States historically have been sovereign.\xe2\x80\x9d Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 564. As evidenced by Medicare, Medicaid, the Employee Retirement Income Security Act of 1974 (ERISA), and the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act of 1996 (HIPAA), the Federal Government plays a lead role in the health-care sector, both as a direct payer and as a regulator.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, and perhaps most important, the minimum coverage provision, along with other provisions of the ACA, addresses the very sort of interstate problem that made the commerce power essential in our federal system. See supra, at 12\xe2\x80\x9314. The crisis created by the large number of U.\xc2\xa0S. residents who lack health insurance is one of national dimension that States are \xe2\x80\x9cseparately incompetent\xe2\x80\x9d to handle. See supra, at 7\xe2\x80\x938, 13. See also Maryland Brief 15\xe2\x80\x9326 (describing \xe2\x80\x9cthe impediments to effective state policymaking that flow from the interconnectedness of each state\xe2\x80\x99s healthcare economy\xe2\x80\x9d and emphasizing that \xe2\x80\x9cstate-level reforms cannot fully address the problems associated with uncompensated care\xe2\x80\x9d). Far from trampling on States\xe2\x80\x99 sovereignty, the ACA attempts a federal solution for the very reason that the States, acting separately, cannot meet the need. Notably, the ACA serves the general welfare of the people of the United States while retaining a prominent role for the States. See id., at 31\xe2\x80\x9336 (explaining and illustrating how the ACA affords States wide latitude in implementing key elements of the Act\xe2\x80\x99s reforms).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIV\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the early 20th century, this Court regularly struck down economic regulation enacted by the peoples\xe2\x80\x99 representatives in both the States and the Federal Government. See, e.g., Carter Coal Co., 298 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 303\xe2\x80\x93304, 309\xe2\x80\x93310; Dagenhart, 247 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 276\xe2\x80\x93277; Lochner v. New York, \n\n198 U.\xc2\xa0S. 45, \n64 (1905)\n. The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s Commerce Clause opinion, and even more so the joint dissenters\xe2\x80\x99 reasoning, see post, at 4\xe2\x80\x9316, bear a disquieting resemblance to those long-overruled decisions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tUltimately, the Court upholds the individual mandate as a proper exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to tax and spend \xe2\x80\x9cfor the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01; ante, at 43\xe2\x80\x9344. I concur in that determination, which makes The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s Commerce Clause essay all the more puzzling. Why should The Chief Justice strive so mightily to hem in Congress\xe2\x80\x99 capacity to meet the new problems arising constantly in our ever-developing modern economy? I find no satisfying response to that question in his opinion.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tV\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThrough Medicaid, Congress has offered the States an opportunity to furnish health care to the poor with the aid of federal financing. To receive federal Medicaid funds, States must provide health benefits to specified categories of needy persons, including pregnant women, children, parents, and adults with disabilities. Guaranteed eligibility varies by category: for some it is tied to the federal poverty level (incomes up to 100% or 133%); for others it depends on criteria such as eligibility for designated state or federal assistance programs. The ACA enlarges the population of needy people States must cover to include adults under age 65 with incomes up to 133% of the fed- eral poverty level. The spending power conferred by the Constitution, the Court has never doubted, permits Congress to define the contours of programs financed with federal funds. See, e.g., Pennhurst State School and Hospital v. Halderman, \n\n451 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n17 (1981)\n. And to expand coverage, Congress could have recalled the existing legislation, and replaced it with a new law making Medicaid as embracive of the poor as Congress chose. \n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe question posed by the 2010 Medicaid expansion, then, is essentially this: To cover a notably larger population, must Congress take the repeal/reenact route, or may it achieve the same result by amending existing law? The answer should be that Congress may expand by amendment the classes of needy persons entitled to Medicaid benefits. A ritualistic requirement that Congress repeal and reenact spending legislation in order to enlarge the population served by a federally funded program would advance no constitutional principle and would scarcely serve the interests of federalism. To the contrary, such a requirement would rigidify Congress\xe2\x80\x99 efforts to empower States by partnering with them in the implementation of federal programs.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMedicaid is a prototypical example of federal-state cooperation in serving the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s general welfare. Rather than authorizing a federal agency to administer a uni- form national health-care system for the poor, Con- gress offered States the opportunity to tailor Medicaid grants to their particular needs, so long as they remain within bounds set by federal law. In shaping Medicaid, Congress did not endeavor to fix permanently the terms participating states must meet; instead, Congress reserved the \xe2\x80\x9cright to alter, amend, or repeal\xe2\x80\x9d any provision of the Medicaid Act. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71304. States, for their part, agreed to amend their own Medicaid plans consistent with changes from time to time made in the federal law. See 42 CFR \xc2\xa7430.12(c)(i) (2011). And from 1965 to the present, States have regularly conformed to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 alterations of the Medicaid Act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice acknowledges that Congress may \xe2\x80\x9ccondition the receipt of [federal] funds on the States\xe2\x80\x99 complying with restrictions on the use of those funds,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 50, but nevertheless concludes that the 2010 expansion is unduly coercive. His conclusion rests on three premises, each of them essential to his theory. First, the Medicaid expansion is, in The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s view, a new grant program, not an addition to the Medicaid program existing before the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment. Congress, The Chief Justice maintains, has threatened States with the loss of funds from an old program in an effort to get them to adopt a new one. Second, the expansion was unforeseeable by the States when they first signed on to Medicaid. Third, the threatened loss of funding is so large that the States have no real choice but to participate in the Medicaid expansion. The Chief Justice therefore\xe2\x80\x94for the first time ever\xe2\x80\x94finds an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 spending power unconstitutionally coercive.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMedicaid, as amended by the ACA, however, is not two spending programs; it is a single program with a constant aim\xe2\x80\x94to enable poor persons to receive basic health care when they need it. Given past expansions, plus express statutory warning that Congress may change the requirements participating States must meet, there can be no tenable claim that the ACA fails for lack of notice. Moreover, States have no entitlement to receive any Medicaid funds; they enjoy only the opportunity to accept funds on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 terms.
