Future Congresses are not bound by their predecessors\xe2\x80\x99 dispositions; they have authority to spend federal revenue as they see fit. The Federal Government, therefore, is not, as The Chief Justice charges, threatening States with the loss of \xe2\x80\x9cexisting\xe2\x80\x9d funds from one spending program in order to induce them to opt into another program. Congress is simply requiring States to do what States have long been required to do to receive Medicaid funding: comply with the conditions Congress prescribes for participation.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tA majority of the Court, however, buys the argument that prospective withholding of funds formerly available exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99 spending power. Given that holding, I entirely agree with The Chief Justice as to the appropriate remedy. It is to bar the withholding found impermissible\xe2\x80\x94not, as the joint dissenters would have it, to scrap the expansion altogether, see post, at 46\xe2\x80\x9348. The dissenters\xe2\x80\x99 view that the ACA must fall in its entirety is a radical departure from the Court\xe2\x80\x99s normal course. When a constitutional infirmity mars a statute, the Court ordinarily removes the infirmity. It undertakes a salvage operation; it does not demolish the legislation. See, e.g., Brockett v. Spokane Arcades, Inc., \n\n472 U.\xc2\xa0S. 491, \n504 (1985)\n (Court\xe2\x80\x99s normal course is to declare a statute invalid \xe2\x80\x9cto the extent that it reaches too far, but otherwise [to leave the statute] intact\xe2\x80\x9d). That course is plainly in order where, as in this case, Congress has expressly instructed courts to leave untouched every provision not found invalid. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71303. Because The Chief Justice finds the withholding\xe2\x80\x94not the granting\xe2\x80\x94of federal funds incom- patible with the Spending Clause, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 extension of Medicaid remains available to any State that affirms its willingness to participate.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tExpansion has been characteristic of the Medicaid program. Akin to the ACA in 2010, the Medicaid Act as passed in 1965 augmented existing federal grant programs jointly administered with the States.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t13\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t States were not required to participate in Medicaid. But if they did, the Federal Government paid at least half the costs. To qual- ify for these grants, States had to offer a minimum level of health coverage to beneficiaries of four federally funded, state-administered welfare programs: Aid to Families with Dependent Children; Old Age Assistance; Aid to the Blind; and Aid to the Permanently and Totally Disabled. See Social Security Amendments of 1965, \xc2\xa7121(a), \n\n79Stat.\n343; Schweiker v. Gray Panthers, \n\n453 U.\xc2\xa0S. 34, \n37 (1981)\n. At their option, States could enroll additional \xe2\x80\x9cmedically needy\xe2\x80\x9d individuals; these costs, too, were partially borne by the Federal Government at the same, at least 50%, rate. Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSince 1965, Congress has amended the Medicaid program on more than 50 occasions, sometimes quite sizably. Most relevant here, between 1988 and 1990, Congress required participating States to include among their beneficiaries pregnant women with family incomes up to 133% of the federal poverty level, children up to age 6 at the same income levels, and children ages 6 to 18 with family incomes up to 100% of the poverty level. See 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(i), 1396a(l); Medicare Catastrophic Cov- erage Act of 1988, \xc2\xa7302, \n\n102Stat.\n750; Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1989, \xc2\xa76401, \n\n103Stat.\n2258; Om- nibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1990, \xc2\xa74601, \n\n104Stat.\n1388\xe2\x80\x93166. These amendments added millions to the Medicaid-eligible population. Dubay & Kenney, Lessons from the Medicaid Expansions for Children and Pregnant Women 5 (Apr. 1997). \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBetween 1966 and 1990, annual federal Medicaid spending grew from $631.6 million to $42.6 billion; state spending rose to $31 billion over the same period. See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Health Expenditures by Type of Service and Source of Funds: Calendar Years 1960 to 2010 (table).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t14\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t And between 1990 and 2010, federal spending increased to $269.5 billion. Ibid. Enlargement of the population and services covered by Medicaid, in short, has been the trend.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCompared to past alterations, the ACA is notable for the extent to which the Federal Government will pick up the tab. Medicaid\xe2\x80\x99s 2010 expansion is financed largely by federal outlays. In 2014, federal funds will cover 100% of the costs for newly eligible beneficiaries; that rate will gradually decrease before settling at 90% in 2020. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396d(y) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). By comparison, federal contributions toward the care of beneficiaries eligible pre-ACA range from 50% to 83%, and averaged 57% between 2005 and 2008. \xc2\xa71396d(b) (2006 ed., Supp. IV); Dept. of Health and Human Services, Centers for Medicare and Medicaid Services, C. Truffer et\xc2\xa0al., 2010 Actuarial Report on the Financial Outlook for Medicaid, p.\xc2\xa020.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNor will the expansion exorbitantly increase state Medicaid spending. The Congressional Budget Office (CBO) projects that States will spend 0.8% more than they would have, absent the ACA. See CBO, Spending & Enrollment Detail for CBO\xe2\x80\x99s March 2009 Baseline. But see ante, at 44\xe2\x80\x9345 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Act dramatically increases state obligations under Medicaid.\xe2\x80\x9d); post, at 45 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.) (\xe2\x80\x9c[A]cceptance of the [ACA expansion] will impose very substantial costs on participating States.\xe2\x80\x9d). Whatever the increase in state obligations after the ACA, it will pale in comparison to the increase in federal funding.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t15\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFinally, any fair appraisal of Medicaid would require acknowledgment of the considerable autonomy States enjoy under the Act. Far from \xe2\x80\x9cconscript[ing] state agencies into the national bureaucratic army,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 55 (citing FERC v. Mississippi, \n\n456 U.\xc2\xa0S. 742, \n775 (1982)\n (O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part) (brackets in original and internal quotation marks omitted)), Medicaid \xe2\x80\x9cis designed to advance cooperative federalism.\xe2\x80\x9d Wisconsin Dept. of Health and Family Servs. v. Blumer, \n\n534 U.\xc2\xa0S. 473, \n495 (2002)\n (citing Harris v. McRae, \n\n448 U.\xc2\xa0S. 297, \n308 (1980)\n). Subject to its basic requirements, the Medicaid Act empowers States to \xe2\x80\x9cselect dramatically different levels of funding and coverage, alter and experiment with different financing and delivery modes, and opt to cover (or not to cover) a range of parti- cular procedures and therapies. States have leveraged this policy discretion to generate a myriad of dramatically different Medicaid programs over the past several decades.\xe2\x80\x9d Ruger, Of Icebergs and Glaciers, 75 Law & Contemp. Probs. 215, 233\xc2\xa0(2012) (footnote omitted). The ACA does not jettison this approach. States, as first-line administrators, will continue to guide the distribution of substantial resources among their needy populations.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe alternative to conditional federal spending, it bears emphasis, is not state autonomy but state marginalization.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t16\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t In 1965, Congress elected to nationalize health coverage for seniors through Medicare. It could similarly have established Medicaid as an exclusively federal program. Instead, Congress gave the States the opportunity to partner in the program\xe2\x80\x99s administration and development. Absent from the nationalized model, of course, is the state-level policy discretion and experimentation that is Medicaid\xe2\x80\x99s hallmark; undoubtedly the interests of federalism are better served when States retain a meaning- ful role in the implementation of a program of such importance. See Caminker, State Sovereignty and Sub- ordinacy, 95 Colum. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1001, 1002\xe2\x80\x931003 (1995) (coopera- tive federalism can preserve \xe2\x80\x9ca significant role for state discretion in achieving specified federal goals, where the alternative is complete federal preemption of any state regulatory role\xe2\x80\x9d); Rose-Ackerman, Cooperative Federalism and Co-optation, 92 Yale L. J. 1344, 1346 (1983) (\xe2\x80\x9cIf the federal government begins to take full responsibility for social welfare spending and preempts the states, the result is likely to be weaker .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. state governments.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t17\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAlthough Congress \xe2\x80\x9chas no obligation to use its Spending Clause power to disburse funds to the States,\xe2\x80\x9d College Savings Bank v. Florida Prepaid Postsecondary Ed. Expense Bd., \n\n527 U.\xc2\xa0S. 666, \n686 (1999)\n, it has provided Medicaid grants notable for their generosity and flexibility. \xe2\x80\x9c[S]uch funds,\xe2\x80\x9d we once observed, \xe2\x80\x9care gifts,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 686\xe2\x80\x93687, and so they have remained through decades of expansion in their size and scope.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Spending Clause authorizes Congress \xe2\x80\x9cto pay the Debts and provide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare of the United States.\xe2\x80\x9d Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01. To ensure that federal funds granted to the States are spent \xe2\x80\x9cto \xe2\x80\x98provide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare\xe2\x80\x99 in the manner Congress intended,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 46, Congress must of course have authority to impose limitations on the States\xe2\x80\x99 use of the federal dollars. This Court, time and again, has respected Congress\xe2\x80\x99 prescription of spending conditions, and has required States to abide by them. See, e.g., Pennhurst, 451 U. S., at 17 (\xe2\x80\x9c[O]ur cases have long recognized that Congress may fix the terms on which it shall disburse federal money to the States.\xe2\x80\x9d). In particular, we have recognized Congress\xe2\x80\x99 prerogative to condition a State\xe2\x80\x99s receipt of Medicaid funding on compliance with the terms Congress set for participation in the program. See, e.g., Harris, 448 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 301 (\xe2\x80\x9c[O]nce a State elects to participate [in Medicaid], it must comply with the requirements of [the Medicaid Act].\xe2\x80\x9d); Arkansas Dept. of Health and Human Servs. v. Ahlborn, \n\n547 U.\xc2\xa0S. 268, \n275 (2006)\n; Frew v. Hawkins, \n\n540 U.\xc2\xa0S. 431, \n433 (2004)\n; Atkins v. Rivera, \n\n477 U.\xc2\xa0S. 154\n\xe2\x80\x93157 (1986).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to condition the use of federal funds is not confined to spending programs as first launched. The legislature may, and often does, amend the law, imposing new conditions grant recipients henceforth must meet in order to continue receiving funds. See infra, at 54 (describing Bennett v. Kentucky Dept. of Ed., \n\n470 U.\xc2\xa0S. 656\n\xe2\x80\x93660 (1985) (enforcing restriction added five years after adoption of educational program)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tYes, there are federalism-based limits on the use of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 conditional spending power. In the leading decision in this area, South Dakota v. Dole, \n\n483 U.\xc2\xa0S. 203 (1987)\n, the Court identified four criteria. The conditions placed on federal grants to States must (a) promote the \xe2\x80\x9cgeneral welfare,\xe2\x80\x9d (b) \xe2\x80\x9cunambiguously\xe2\x80\x9d inform States what is demanded of them, (c) be germane \xe2\x80\x9cto the federal interest in particular national projects or programs,\xe2\x80\x9d and (d) not \xe2\x80\x9cinduce the States to engage in activities that would themselves be unconstitutional.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 207\xe2\x80\x93208, 210 (internal quotation marks omitted).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t18\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court in Dole mentioned, but did not adopt, a further limitation, one hypothetically raised a half-century earlier: In \xe2\x80\x9csome circumstances,\xe2\x80\x9d Congress might be prohibited from offering a \xe2\x80\x9cfinancial inducement .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. so coercive as to pass the point at which \xe2\x80\x98pressure turns into compulsion.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 211 (quoting Steward Machine Co. v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 548, \n590 (1937)\n). Prior to today\xe2\x80\x99s decision, however, the Court has never ruled that the terms of any grant crossed the indistinct line between temptation and coercion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tDole involved the National Minimum Drinking Age Act, \n\n23 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7158, enacted in 1984. That Act directed the Secretary of Transportation to withhold 5% of the federal highway funds otherwise payable to a State if the State permitted purchase of alcoholic beverages by persons less than 21 years old. Drinking age was not within the authority of Congress to regulate, South Dakota argued, because the \n \nTwenty-First Amendment gave the States exclusive power to control the manufacture, transportation, and consumption of alcoholic beverages. The small percentage of highway-construction funds South Dakota stood to lose by adhering to 19 as the age of eligibility to purchase 3.2% beer, however, was not enough to qualify as coercion, the Court concluded.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis case does not present the concerns that led the Court in Dole even to consider the prospect of coercion. In Dole, the condition\xe2\x80\x94set 21 as the minimum drinking age\xe2\x80\x94 did not tell the States how to use funds Congress pro- vided for highway construction. Further, in view of the \n \nTwenty-First Amendment, it was an open question whether Congress could directly impose a national minimum drinking age.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe ACA, in contrast, relates solely to the federally funded Medicaid program; if States choose not to comply, Congress has not threatened to withhold funds earmarked for any other program. Nor does the ACA use Medicaid funding to induce States to take action Congress itself could not undertake. The Federal Government undoubtedly could operate its own health-care program for poor persons, just as it operates Medicare for seniors\xe2\x80\x99 health care. See supra, at 44.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat is what makes this such a simple case, and the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decision so unsettling. Congress, aiming to assist the needy, has appropriated federal money to subsidize state health-insurance programs that meet federal standards. The principal standard the ACA sets is that the state program cover adults earning no more than 133% of the federal poverty line. Enforcing that prescription ensures that federal funds will be spent on health care for the poor in furtherance of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 present perception of the general welfare.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice asserts that the Medicaid expan- sion creates a \xe2\x80\x9cnew health care program.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 54. Moreover, States could \xe2\x80\x9chardly anticipate\xe2\x80\x9d that Congress would \xe2\x80\x9ctransform [the program] so dramatically.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 55. Therefore, The Chief Justice maintains, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 threat to withhold \xe2\x80\x9cold\xe2\x80\x9d Medicaid funds based on a State\xe2\x80\x99s refusal to participate in the \xe2\x80\x9cnew\xe2\x80\x9d program is a \xe2\x80\x9cthrea[t] to terminate [an]other .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. independent gran[t].\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 50, 52\xe2\x80\x9353. And because the threat to withhold a large amount of funds from one program \xe2\x80\x9cleaves the States with no real option but to acquiesce [in a newly created program],\xe2\x80\x9d The Chief Justice concludes, the Medicaid expansion is unconstitutionally coercive. Ante, at 52.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe starting premise on which The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s coercion analysis rests is that the ACA did not really \xe2\x80\x9cextend\xe2\x80\x9d Medicaid; instead, Congress created an entirely new program to co-exist with the old. The Chief Justice calls the ACA new, but in truth, it simply reaches more of America\xe2\x80\x99s poor than Congress originally covered.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMedicaid was created to enable States to provide medical assistance to \xe2\x80\x9cneedy persons.\xe2\x80\x9d See S. Rep. No. 404, 89th Cong., 1st Sess., pt. 1, p.\xc2\xa09 (1965). See also \xc2\xa7121(a), \n\n79Stat.\n343 (The purpose of Medicaid is to enable States \xe2\x80\x9cto furnish .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. medical assistance on behalf of [certain persons] whose income and resources are insufficient to meet the costs of necessary medical services.\xe2\x80\x9d). By bringing health care within the reach of a larger population of Americans unable to afford it, the Medicaid expansion is an extension of that basic aim.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Medicaid Act contains hundreds of provisions governing operation of the program, setting conditions ranging from \xe2\x80\x9cLimitation on payments to States for expend- itures attributable to taxes,\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(t) (2006 ed.), to \xe2\x80\x9cMedical assistance to aliens not lawfully admitted for permanent residence,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa71396b(v) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV). The Medicaid expansion leaves unchanged the vast majority of these provisions; it adds beneficiaries to the existing program and specifies the rate at which States will be reimbursed for services provided to the added bene- ficiaries. See ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa72001(a)(1), (3), \n\n124Stat.\n271\xe2\x80\x93272. The ACA does not describe operational aspects of the program for these newly eligible persons; for that information, one must read the existing Medicaid Act. See 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396\xe2\x80\x931396v(b) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress styled and clearly viewed the Medicaid expansion as an amendment to the Medicaid Act, not as a \xe2\x80\x9cnew\xe2\x80\x9d health-care program. To the four categories of beneficiaries for whom coverage became mandatory in 1965, and the three mandatory classes added in the late 1980\xe2\x80\x99s, see supra, at 41\xe2\x80\x9342, the ACA adds an eighth: individuals under 65 with incomes not exceeding 133% of the federal poverty level. The expansion is effectuated by \xc2\xa72001 of the ACA, aptly titled: \xe2\x80\x9cMedicaid Coverage for the Lowest Income Populations.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n124Stat.\n271. That section amends Title 42, Chapter 7, Subchapter XIX: Grants to States for Medical Assistance Programs. Commonly known as the Medicaid Act, Subchapter XIX filled some 278 pages in 2006. Section 2001 of the ACA would add approximately three pages.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t19\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress has broad authority to construct or adjust spending programs to meet its contemporary understanding of \xe2\x80\x9cthe general Welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d Helvering v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 619\n\xe2\x80\x93641 (1937). Courts owe a large measure of respect to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 characterization of the grant programs it establishes. See Steward Machine, 301 U. S., at 594. Even if courts were inclined to second-guess Congress\xe2\x80\x99 conception of the character of its legislation, how would reviewing judges divine whether an Act of Congress, purporting to amend a law, is in reality not an amendment, but a new creation? At what point does an extension become so large that it \xe2\x80\x9ctransforms\xe2\x80\x9d the basic law?\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tEndeavoring to show that Congress created a new program, The Chief Justice cites three aspects of the expansion. First, he asserts that, in covering those earning no more than 133% of the federal poverty line, the Medicaid expansion, unlike pre-ACA Medicaid, does not \xe2\x80\x9ccare for the neediest among us.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 53. What makes that so? Single adults earning no more than $14,856 per year\xe2\x80\x94133% of the current federal poverty level\xe2\x80\x94surely rank among the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s poor.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, according to The Chief Justice, \xe2\x80\x9cCongress mandated that newly eligible persons receive a level of coverage that is less comprehensive than the traditional Medicaid benefit package.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. That less comprehensive benefit package, however, is not an innovation introduced by the ACA; since 2006, States have been free to use it for many of their Medicaid beneficiaries.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t20\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t The level of benefits offered therefore does not set apart post-ACA Medicaid recipients from all those entitled to benefits pre-ACA. \n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThird, The Chief Justice correctly notes that the reimbursement rate for participating States is differ- ent regarding individuals who became Medicaid-eligible through the ACA. Ibid. But the rate differs only in its generosity to participating States. Under pre-ACA Medicaid, the Federal Government pays up to 83% of the costs of coverage for current enrollees, \xc2\xa71396d(b) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV); under the ACA, the federal contribution starts at 100% and will eventually settle at 90%, \xc2\xa71396d(y). Even if one agreed that a change of as little as 7 percentage points carries constitutional significance, is it not passing strange to suggest that the purported incursion on state sovereignty might have been averted, or at least mitigated, had Congress offered States less money to carry out the same obligations?\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tConsider also that Congress could have repealed Medicaid. See supra, at 38\xe2\x80\x9339 (citing \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71304); Brief for Petitioners in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, p.\xc2\xa041. Thereafter, Congress could have enacted Medicaid II, a new program combin- ing the pre-2010 coverage with the expanded coverage required by the ACA. By what right does a court stop Congress from building up without first tearing down?\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice finds the Medicaid expansion vulnerable because it took participating States by surprise. Ante, at 54. \xe2\x80\x9cA State could hardly anticipate that Congres[s]\xe2\x80\x9d would endeavor to \xe2\x80\x9ctransform [the Medicaid program] so dramatically,\xe2\x80\x9d he states. Ante, at 54\xe2\x80\x9355. For the notion that States must be able to foresee, when they sign up, alterations Congress might make later on, The Chief Justice cites only one case: Pennhurst State School and Hospital v. Halderman, \n\n451 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn Pennhurst, residents of a state-run, federally funded institution for the mentally disabled complained of abusive treatment and inhumane conditions in alleged violation of the Developmentally Disabled Assistance and Bill of Rights Act. 451 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 5\xe2\x80\x936. We held that the State was not answerable in damages for violating conditions it did not \xe2\x80\x9cvoluntarily and knowingly accep[t].\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 17, 27. Inspecting the statutory language and legislative his- tory, we found that the Act did not \xe2\x80\x9cunambiguously\xe2\x80\x9d impose the requirement on which the plaintiffs relied: that they receive appropriate treatment in the least restrictive environment. Id., at 17\xe2\x80\x9318. Satisfied that Congress had not clearly conditioned the States\xe2\x80\x99 receipt of federal funds on the States\xe2\x80\x99 provision of such treatment, we declined to read such a requirement into the Act. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 spending power, we concluded, \xe2\x80\x9cdoes not include surprising participating States with postacceptance or \xe2\x80\x98retroactive\xe2\x80\x99 conditions.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 24\xe2\x80\x9325.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tPennhurst thus instructs that \xe2\x80\x9cif Congress intends to impose a condition on the grant of federal moneys, it must do so unambiguously.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 53 (quoting Pennhurst, 451 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 17). That requirement is met in this case. Section 2001 does not take effect until 2014. The ACA makes perfectly clear what will be required of States that accept Medicaid funding after that date: They must extend eligibility to adults with incomes no more than 133% of the federal poverty line. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A) (i)(VIII) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice appears to find in Pennhurst a requirement that, when spending legislation is first passed, or when States first enlist in the federal program, Congress must provide clear notice of conditions it might later impose. If I understand his point correctly, it was incumbent on Congress, in 1965, to warn the States clearly of the size and shape potential changes to Medicaid might take. And absent such notice, sizable changes could not be made mandatory. Our decisions do not support such a requirement.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t21\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn Bennett v. New Jersey, \n\n470 U.\xc2\xa0S. 632 (1985)\n, the Secretary of Education sought to recoup Title I funds\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t22\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t based on the State\xe2\x80\x99s noncompliance, from 1970 to 1972, with a 1978 amendment to Title I. Relying on Pennhurst, we rejected the Secretary\xe2\x80\x99s attempt to recover funds based on the States\xe2\x80\x99 alleged violation of a rule that did not exist when the State accepted and spent the funds. See 470 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 640 (\xe2\x80\x9cNew Jersey[,] when it applied for and received Title I funds for the years 1970\xe2\x80\x931972[,] had no basis to believe that the propriety of the expenditures would be judged by any standards other than the ones in effect at the time.\xe2\x80\x9d (citing Pennhurst, 451 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 17, 24\xe2\x80\x9325; emphasis added)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen amendment of an existing grant program has no such retroactive effect, however, we have upheld Congress\xe2\x80\x99 instruction. In Bennett v. Kentucky Dept. of Ed., \n\n470 U.\xc2\xa0S. 656 (1985)\n, the Secretary sued to recapture Title I funds based on the Commonwealth\xe2\x80\x99s 1974 violation of a spending condition Congress added to Title I in 1970. Rejecting Kentucky\xe2\x80\x99s argument pinned to Pennhurst, we held that the Commonwealth suffered no surprise after accepting the federal funds. Kentucky was therefore obliged to re- turn the money. 470 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 665\xe2\x80\x93666, 673\xe2\x80\x93674. The conditions imposed were to be assessed as of 1974, in light of \xe2\x80\x9cthe legal requirements in place when the grants were made,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 670, not as of 1965, when Title I was originally enacted.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs these decisions show, Pennhurst\xe2\x80\x99s rule demands that conditions on federal funds be unambiguously clear at the time a State receives and uses the money\xe2\x80\x94not at the time, perhaps years earlier, when Congress passed the law establishing the program. See also Dole, 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 208 (finding Pennhurst satisfied based on the clarity of the Federal Aid Highway Act as amended in 1984, without looking back to 1956, the year of the Act\xe2\x80\x99s adoption).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn any event, from the start, the Medicaid Act put States on notice that the program could be changed: \xe2\x80\x9cThe right to alter, amend, or repeal any provision of [Medicaid],\xe2\x80\x9d the statute has read since 1965, \xe2\x80\x9cis hereby reserved to the Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71304. The \xe2\x80\x9ceffect of these few simple words\xe2\x80\x9d has long been settled. See National Railroad Passenger Corporation v. Atchison, T. & S. F. R. Co., \n\n470 U.\xc2\xa0S. 451\n\xe2\x80\x93468, n. 22 (1985) (citing Sinking Fund Cases, \n\n99 U. S. 700, \n720 (1879)\n). By reserving the right to \xe2\x80\x9calter, amend, [or] repeal\xe2\x80\x9d a spending program, Congress \xe2\x80\x9chas given special notice of its intention to retain .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. full and complete power to make such alterations and amendments .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. as come within the just scope of legislative power.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 720.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur decision in Bowen v. Public Agencies Opposed to Social Security Entrapment, \n\n477 U. S. 41\n\xe2\x80\x9352 (1986), is guiding here. As enacted in 1935, the Social Security Act did not cover state employees. Id., at 44. In response to pressure from States that wanted coverage for their employees, Congress, in 1950, amended the Act to allow States to opt into the program. Id., at 45. The statutory provision giving States this option expressly permitted them to withdraw from the program. Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBeginning in the late 1970\xe2\x80\x99s, States increasingly exercised the option to withdraw. Id., at 46. Concerned that withdrawals were threatening the integrity of Social Security, Congress repealed the termination provision. Congress thereby changed Social Security from a program voluntary for the States to one from which they could not escape. Id., at 48. California objected, arguing that the change impermissibly deprived it of a right to withdraw from Social Security. Id., at 49\xe2\x80\x9350. We unanimously rejected California\xe2\x80\x99s argument. Id., at 51\xe2\x80\x9353. By including in the Act \xe2\x80\x9ca clause expressly reserving to it \xe2\x80\x98[t]he right to alter, amend, or repeal any provision\xe2\x80\x99 of the Act,\xe2\x80\x9d we held, Congress put States on notice that the Act \xe2\x80\x9ccreated no contractual rights.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 51\xe2\x80\x9352. The States therefore had no law-based ground on which to complain about the amendment, despite the significant character of the change.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice nevertheless would rewrite \xc2\xa71304 to countenance only the \xe2\x80\x9cright to alter somewhat,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9camend, but not too much.\xe2\x80\x9d Congress, however, did not so qualify \xc2\xa71304. Indeed, Congress retained discretion to \xe2\x80\x9crepeal\xe2\x80\x9d Medicaid, wiping it out entirely. Cf. Delta Air Lines, Inc. v. August, \n\n450 U.\xc2\xa0S. 346, \n368 (1981)\n (Rehnquist, J., dissenting) (invoking \xe2\x80\x9cthe common-sense maxim that the greater includes the lesser\xe2\x80\x9d). As Bowen indicates, no State could reasonably have read \xc2\xa71304 as reserving to Congress authority to make adjustments only if modestly sized.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn fact, no State proceeded on that understanding. In com- pliance with Medicaid regulations, each State expressly undertook to abide by future Medicaid changes. See 42 CFR \xc2\xa7430.12(c)(1) (2011) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe [state Medicaid] plan must provide that it will be amended whenever necessary to reflect .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. [c]hanges in Federal law, regulations, policy interpretations, or court decisions.\xe2\x80\x9d). Whenever a State notifies the Federal Government of a change in its own Medicaid program, the State certifies both that it knows the federally set terms of participation may change, and that it will abide by those changes as a condition of continued participation. See, e.g., Florida Agency for Health Care Admin., State Plan Under Title XIX of the Social Security Act Medical Assistance Program \xc2\xa77.1, p. 86 (Oct. 6, 1992).\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice insists that the most recent expansion, in contrast to its predecessors, \xe2\x80\x9caccomplishes a shift in kind, not merely degree.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 53. But why was Medicaid altered only in degree, not in kind, when Congress required States to cover millions of children and pregnant women? See supra, at 41\xe2\x80\x9342. Congress did not \xe2\x80\x9cmerely alte[r] and expan[d] the boundaries of\xe2\x80\x9d the Aid to Families with Dependent Children program. But see ante, at 53\xe2\x80\x9355. Rather, Congress required participating States to provide coverage tied to the federal poverty level (as it later did in the ACA), rather than to the AFDC program. See Brief for National Health Law Program et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 16\xe2\x80\x9318. In short, given \xc2\xa71304, this Court\xe2\x80\x99s construction of \xc2\xa71304\xe2\x80\x99s language in Bowen, and the enlargement of Medicaid in the years since 1965,\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t23\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t a State would be hard put to complain that it lacked fair notice when, in 2010, Congress altered Medicaid to embrace a larger portion of the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s poor.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice ultimately asks whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe financial inducement offered by Congress .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. pass[ed] the point at which pressure turns into compulsion.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 50 (internal quotation marks omitted). The financial inducement Congress employed here, he concludes, crosses that threshold: The threatened withholding of \xe2\x80\x9cexisting Medicaid funds\xe2\x80\x9d is \xe2\x80\x9ca gun to the head\xe2\x80\x9d that forces States to acquiesce. Ante, at 50\xe2\x80\x9351 (citing \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396c).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t24\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Chief Justice sees no need to \xe2\x80\x9cfix the outermost line,\xe2\x80\x9d Steward Machine, 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 591, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere persuasion gives way to coercion,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 55. Neither do the joint dissenters. See post, at 36, 38.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t25\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Notably, the decision on which they rely, Steward Machine, found the statute at issue inside the line, \xe2\x80\x9cwherever the line may be.\xe2\x80\x9d 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 591.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen future Spending Clause challenges arrive, as they likely will in the wake of today\xe2\x80\x99s decision, how will litigants and judges assess whether \xe2\x80\x9ca State has a legitimate choice whether to accept the federal conditions in exchange for federal funds\xe2\x80\x9d? Ante, at 48. Are courts to measure the number of dollars the Federal Government might withhold for noncompliance? The portion of the State\xe2\x80\x99s budget at stake? And which State\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94or States\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94budget is determinative: the lead plaintiff, all challenging States (26 in this case, many with quite different fiscal situations), or some national median?\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tDoes it matter that Florida, unlike most States, imposes no state income tax, and therefore might be able to replace foregone federal funds with new state revenue?\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t26\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Or that the coercion state officials in fact fear is punishment at the ballot box for turning down a politically popular federal grant?\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe coercion inquiry, therefore, appears to involve polit- ical judgments that defy judicial calculation. See Baker v. Carr, \n\n369 U.\xc2\xa0S. 186, \n217 (1962)\n. Even commentators sympathetic to robust enforcement of Dole\xe2\x80\x99s limitations, see supra, at 46, have concluded that conceptions of \xe2\x80\x9cimpermissible coercion\xe2\x80\x9d premised on States\xe2\x80\x99 perceived inability to decline federal funds \xe2\x80\x9care just too amorphous to be judicially administrable.\xe2\x80\x9d Baker & Berman, Getting off the Dole, 78 Ind. L.\xc2\xa0J. 459, 521, 522, n. 307 (2003) (citing, e.g., Scalia, The Rule of Law as a Law of Rules, 56 U. Chi. L.\xc2\xa0Rev. 1175 (1989)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAt bottom, my colleagues\xe2\x80\x99 position is that the States\xe2\x80\x99 reliance on federal funds limits Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to alter its spending programs. This gets things backwards: Congress, not the States, is tasked with spending federal money in service of the general welfare. And each successive Congress is empowered to appropriate funds as it sees fit. When the 110th Congress reached a conclusion about Medicaid funds that differed from its predecessors\xe2\x80\x99 view, it abridged no State\xe2\x80\x99s right to \xe2\x80\x9cexisting,\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cpre-existing,\xe2\x80\x9d funds. But see ante, at 51\xe2\x80\x9352; post, at 47\xe2\x80\x9348 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.). For, in fact, there are no such funds. There is only money States anticipate receiving from future Congresses.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tD\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress has delegated to the Secretary of Health and Human Services the authority to withhold, in whole or in part, federal Medicaid funds from States that fail to comply with the Medicaid Act as originally composed and as subsequently amended. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396c.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t27\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t The Chief Justice, however, holds that the Constitution precludes the Secretary from withholding \xe2\x80\x9cexisting\xe2\x80\x9d Medicaid funds based on States\xe2\x80\x99 refusal to comply with the expanded Medi- caid program. Ante, at 55. For the foregoing reasons, I disagree that any such withholding would violate the Spending Clause. Accordingly, I would affirm the decision of the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit in this regard.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tBut in view of The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s disposition, I agree with him that the Medicaid Act\xe2\x80\x99s severability clause determines the appropriate remedy. That clause provides that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]f any provision of [the Medicaid Act], or the application thereof to any person or circumstance, is held in- valid, the remainder of the chapter, and the application of such provision to other persons or circumstances shall not be affected thereby.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71303.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court does not strike down any provision of the ACA. It prohibits only the \xe2\x80\x9capplication\xe2\x80\x9d of the Secretary\xe2\x80\x99s authority to withhold Medicaid funds from States that decline to conform their Medicaid plans to the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s requirements. Thus the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s authorization of funds to finance the expansion remains intact, and the Secretary\xe2\x80\x99s authority to withhold funds for reasons other than noncompliance with the expansion remains unaffected.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tEven absent \xc2\xa71303\xe2\x80\x99s command, we would have no warrant to invalidate the Medicaid expansion, contra post, at 46\xe2\x80\x9348 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.), not to mention the entire ACA, post, at 49\xe2\x80\x9364 (same). For when a court confronts an unconstitutional statute, its endeavor must be to conserve, not destroy, the legislature\xe2\x80\x99s dominant objective. See, e.g., Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., \n\n546 U.\xc2\xa0S. 320\n\xe2\x80\x93330 (2006). In this case, that objective was to increase access to health care for the poor by increasing the States\xe2\x80\x99 access to federal funds. The Chief Justice is undoubtedly right to conclude that Congress may offer States funds \xe2\x80\x9cto expand the availability of health care, and requir[e] that States accepting such funds comply with the conditions on their use.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 55. I therefore concur in the judgment with respect to Part IV\xe2\x80\x93B of The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s opinion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor the reasons stated, I agree with The Chief Justice that, as to the validity of the minimum coverage provi- sion, the judgment of the Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit should be reversed. In my view, the provision en- counters no constitutional obstruction. Further, I would uphold the Eleventh Circuit\xe2\x80\x99s decision that the Medicaid expansion is within Congress\xe2\x80\x99 spending power.\n\t\t\t

See ibid., n.\xc2\xa02.\n\t\n

\n\n3\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Alexander Hamilton described the problem this way: \xe2\x80\x9c[Often] it would be beneficial to all the states to encourage, or suppress[,] a particular branch of trade, while it would be detrimental .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. to attempt it without the concurrence of the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d The Continentalist No. V, in 3 Papers of Alexander Hamilton 75, 78 (H. Syrett ed. 1962). Because the concurrence of all States was exceedingly difficult to obtain, Hamilton observed, \xe2\x80\x9cthe experiment would probably be left untried.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.\n\t\n
\n\n4\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0See Dept. of Health and Human Services, National Center for Health Statistics, Summary Health Statistics for U.\xc2\xa0S. Adults: National Health Interview Survey 2009, Ser. 10, No. 249, p. 124, Table 37 (Dec.\xc2\xa02010).\n\t\n
\n\n5\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Echoing The Chief Justice, the joint dissenters urge that the minimum coverage provision impermissibly regulates young people who \xe2\x80\x9chave no intention of purchasing [medical care]\xe2\x80\x9d and are too far \xe2\x80\x9cremoved from the [health-care] market.\xe2\x80\x9d See post, at 8, 11. This criticism ignores the reality that a healthy young person may be a day away from needing health care. See supra, at 4. A victim of an accident or unforeseen illness will consume extensive medical care immediately, though scarcely expecting to do so.\n\t\n
\n\n6\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s reliance on the quoted passages of the Constitution, see ante, at 18\xe2\x80\x9319, is also dubious on other grounds. The power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate the Value\xe2\x80\x9d of the national currency presumably includes the power to increase the currency\xe2\x80\x99s worth\xe2\x80\x94i.e., to create value where none previously existed. And if the power to \xe2\x80\x9c[r]egulat[e] .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. the land and naval Forces\xe2\x80\x9d presupposes \xe2\x80\x9cthere is already [in existence] something to be regulated,\xe2\x80\x9d i.e., an Army and a Navy, does Congress lack authority to create an Air Force?\n\t\n
\n\n7\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s characterization of individuals who choose not to purchase private insurance as \xe2\x80\x9cdoing nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 20, is simi-larly questionable. A person who self-insures opts against prepayment for a product the person will in time consume. When aggregated, exercise of that option has a substantial impact on the health-care market. See supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x937, 16\xe2\x80\x9317.\n\t\n
\n\n8\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Some adherents to the joint dissent have questioned the existence of substantive due process rights. See McDonald v. Chicago, 561 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___, ___ (2010) (Thomas, J., concurring) (slip op., at 7) (The notion that the Due Process Clause \xe2\x80\x9ccould define the substance of th[e] righ[t to liberty] strains credulity.\xe2\x80\x9d); Albright v. Oliver, \n (Scalia, J., concurring) (\xe2\x80\x9cI reject the proposition that the Due Process Clause guarantees certain (unspecified) liberties[.]\xe2\x80\x9d). Given these Justices\xe2\x80\x99 reluctance to interpret the Due Process Clause as guaranteeing liberty interests, their willingness to plant such protections in the Commerce Clause is striking.\n\t\n
\n\n9\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The failure to purchase vegetables in The Chief Justice\xe2\x80\x99s hypothetical, then, is not what leads to higher health-care costs for others; rather, it is the failure of individuals to maintain a healthy diet, and the resulting obesity, that creates the cost-shifting problem. See ante, at 22\xe2\x80\x9323. Requiring individuals to purchase vegetables is thusseveral steps removed from solving the problem. The failure to obtain health insurance, by contrast, is the immediate cause of the cost-shifting Congress sought to address through the ACA. See supra, at 5\xe2\x80\x937. Requiring individuals to obtain insurance attacks the source of the problem directly, in a single step.\n\t\n
\n\n10\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Indeed, Congress regularly and uncontroversially requires individuals who are \xe2\x80\x9cdoing nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d see ante, at 20, to take action. Exam-ples include federal requirements to report for jury duty, \n (2006 ed., Supp. IV); to register for selective service, 50 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. App. \xc2\xa7453; to purchase firearms and gear in anticipation of service in the Militia, \n (Uniform Militia Act of 1792); to turn gold currency over to the Federal Government in exchange for paper currency, see Nortz v. United States, \n; and to file a tax return, \n (2006 ed., Supp. IV).\n\t\n
\n\n11\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0In a separate argument, the joint dissenters contend that the minimum coverage provision is not necessary and proper because it was not the \xe2\x80\x9conly .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. way\xe2\x80\x9d Congress could have made the guaranteed-issue and community-rating reforms work. Post, at 9\xe2\x80\x9310. Congress could also have avoided an insurance-market death spiral, the dissenters maintain, by imposing a surcharge on those who did not previously purchase insurance when those individuals eventually enter the health-insurance system. Post, at 10. Or Congress could \xe2\x80\x9cden[y] a full income tax credit\xe2\x80\x9d to those who do not purchase insurance. Ibid.\nNeither a surcharge on those who purchase insurance nor the denial of a tax credit to those who do not would solve the problem created by guaranteed-issue and community-rating requirements. Neither would prompt the purchase of insurance before sickness or injury occurred.\nBut even assuming there were \xe2\x80\x9cpracticable\xe2\x80\x9d alternatives to the minimum coverage provision, \xe2\x80\x9cwe long ago rejected the view that the Necessary and Proper Clause demands that an Act of Congress be \xe2\x80\x98absolutely necessary\xe2\x80\x99 to the exercise of an enumerated power.\xe2\x80\x9d Jinksv. Richland County, \n (quoting McCullochv. Maryland, 4 Wheat. 316, 414\xe2\x80\x93415 (1819)). Rather, the statutory provision at issue need only be \xe2\x80\x9cconducive\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c[reasonably] adapted\xe2\x80\x9d to the goal Congress seeks to achieve. Jinks, 538 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 462 (internal quotation marks omitted). The minimum coverage provision meets this requirement. See supra, at 31\xe2\x80\x9333.\n\t\n
\n\n12\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice states that he must evaluate the constitution-ality of the minimum coverage provision under the Commerce Clause because the provision \xe2\x80\x9creads more naturally as a command to buy insurance than as a tax.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 44. The Chief Justice ultimately concludes, however, that interpreting the provision as a tax is a \xe2\x80\x9cfairly possible\xe2\x80\x9d construction. Ante, at 32 (internal quotation marks omitted). That being so, I see no reason to undertake a Commerce Clause analysis that is not outcome determinative.\n\t\n
\n\n13\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Medicaid was \xe2\x80\x9cplainly an extension of the existing Kerr-Mills\xe2\x80\x9d grant program. Huberfeld, Federalizing Medicaid, 14 U.\xc2\xa0Pa. J. Const. L. 431, 444\xe2\x80\x93445 (2011). Indeed, the \xe2\x80\x9csection of the Senate report dealing with Title XIX\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the title establishing Medicaid\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwas entitled, \xe2\x80\x98Improvement and Extension of Kerr-Mills Medical Assistance Program.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Stevens & Stevens, Welfare Medicine in America 51 (1974) (quoting S.\xc2\xa0Rep. No. 404, 89th Cong., 1st Sess., pt. 1, p.\xc2\xa09 (1965)). Setting the pattern for Medicaid, Kerr-Mills reimbursed States for a portion of the cost of health care provided to welfare recipients ifStates met conditions specified in the federal law, e.g., participating States were obliged to offer minimum coverage for hospitalization and physician services. See Huberfeld, supra, at 443\xe2\x80\x93444.\n\t\n
\n\n14\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Available online at http://www.cms.gov/Research-Statistics-Data-and-Systems/Statistics-Trends-and-Reports/NationalHealthExpendData/NationalHealthAccountsHistorical.html.\n\t\n
\n\n15\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Even the study on which the plaintiffs rely, see Brief for Petitioners 10, concludes that \xe2\x80\x9c[w]hile most states will experience some increase in spending, this is quite small relative to the federal matching payments and low relative to the costs of uncompensated care that [the states] would bear if the[re] were no health reform.\xe2\x80\x9d See Kaiser Commission on Medicaid & the Uninsured, Medicaid Coverage & Spending in Health Reform 16 (May 2010). Thus there can be no objection to the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s expansion of Medicaid as an \xe2\x80\x9cunfunded mandate.\xe2\x80\x9d Quite the contrary, the program is impressively well funded.\n\t\n
\n\n16\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0In 1972, for example, Congress ended the federal cash-assistance program for the aged, blind, and disabled. That program previously had been operated jointly by the Federal and State Governments, asis the case with Medicaid today. Congress replaced the cooperative federal program with the nationalized Supplemental Security In-come (SSI) program. See Schweiker v. Gray Panthers, \n.\n\t\n
\n\n17\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice and the joint dissenters perceive in cooperative federalism a \xe2\x80\x9cthrea[t]\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cpolitical accountability.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 48; see post, at 34\xe2\x80\x9335. By that, they mean voter confusion: Citizens upset by unpopular government action, they posit, may ascribe to state officials blame more appropriately laid at Congress\xe2\x80\x99 door. But no such confusion is apparent in this case: Medicaid\xe2\x80\x99s status as a federally funded, state-administered program is hardly hidden from view.\n\t\n
\n\n18\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Although the plaintiffs, in the proceedings below, did not contest the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s satisfaction of these criteria, see 648 F.\xc2\xa03d 1235, 1263 (CA11 2011), The Chief Justice appears to rely heavily on the second crite-rion. Compare ante, at 52, 54, with infra, at 52\xe2\x80\x9354.\n\t\n
\n\n19\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Compare Subchapter XIX, 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396\xe2\x80\x931396v(b) (2006 ed. and Supp. IV) with \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396a(a) (10)(A)(i)(VIII) (2006 ed. and Supp.IV); 1396a(a) (10)(A)(ii)(XX), 1396a(a)(75), 1396a(k), 1396a(gg) to (hh), 1396d(y), 1396r\xe2\x80\x931(e), 1396u\xe2\x80\x937(b)(5) to (6).\n\t\n
\n\n20\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Deficit Reduction Act of 2005 authorized States to provide \xe2\x80\x9cbenchmark coverage\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cbenchmark equivalent coverage\xe2\x80\x9d to certain Medicaid populations. See \xc2\xa76044, \n, \n\xe2\x80\x937 (2006 ed. and Supp. IV). States may offer the same level of coverage to persons newly eligible under the ACA. See \xc2\xa71396a(k).\n\t\n
\n\n21\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The Chief Justice observes that \xe2\x80\x9cSpending Clause legislation[i]s much in the nature of a contract.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 46 (internal quotation marks omitted). See also post, at 33 (joint opinion of Scalia, Kennedy, Thomas, and Alito, JJ.) (same). But the Court previously has rec-ognized that \xe2\x80\x9c[u]nlike normal contractual undertakings, federal grant programs originate in and remain governed by statutory provisions expressing the judgment of Congress concerning desirable public policy.\xe2\x80\x9d Bennett v. Kentucky Dept. of Ed., \n.\n\t\n
\n\n22\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Title I of the Elementary and Secondary Education Act of 1965 provided federal grants to finance supplemental educational programs in school districts with high concentrations of children from low-income families. See Bennett v. New Jersey, \n\xe2\x80\x93635 (1985) (citing Pub. L. No. 89\xe2\x80\x9310, \n).\n\t\n
\n\n23\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Note, in this regard, the extension of Social Security, which began in 1935 as an old-age pension program, then expanded to include sur-vivor benefits in 1939 and disability benefits in 1956. See Social Security Act, ch. 531, \n\xe2\x80\x93625; Social Security Act Amendments of 1939, \n\xe2\x80\x931365; Social Security Amendments of 1956, ch. 836, \xc2\xa7103, \n\xe2\x80\x93816.\n\t\n
\n\n24\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The joint dissenters, for their part, would make this the entire inquiry. \xe2\x80\x9c[I]f States really have no choice other than to accept the package,\xe2\x80\x9d they assert, \xe2\x80\x9cthe offer is coercive.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 35. The Chief Justice recognizes Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority to construct a single federal program and \xe2\x80\x9ccondition the receipt of funds on the States\xe2\x80\x99 complying with restrictions on the use of those funds.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 50. For the joint dissenters, however, all that matters, it appears, is whether States can resist the temptation of a given federal grant. Post, at 35. On this logic, any federal spending program, sufficiently large and well-funded, would be unconstitutional. The joint dissenters point to smaller programs States might have the will to refuse. See post, at 40\xe2\x80\x9341 (elementary and secondary education). But how is a court to judge whether \xe2\x80\x9conly 6.6% of all state expenditures,\xe2\x80\x9d post, at 41, is an amount States could or would do without?\nSpeculations of this genre are characteristic of the joint dissent. See, e.g., post, at 35 (\xe2\x80\x9cit may be state officials who will bear the brunt of public disapproval\xe2\x80\x9d for joint federal-state endeavors); ibid., (\xe2\x80\x9cfederal officials .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. may remain insulated from the electoral ramifications of their decision\xe2\x80\x9d); post, at 37 (\xe2\x80\x9ca heavy federal tax .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. levied to support a federal program that offers large grants to the States .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. may, as a practical matter, [leave States] unable to refuse to participate\xe2\x80\x9d); ibid. (withdrawal from a federal program \xe2\x80\x9cwould likely force the State to impose a huge tax increase\xe2\x80\x9d); post, at 46 (state share of ACA expansion costs \xe2\x80\x9cmay increase in the future\xe2\x80\x9d) (all emphasis added; some internal quotation marks omitted). The joint dissenters are long on conjecture and short on real-world examples.\n\t\n
\n\n25\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The joint dissenters also rely heavily on Congress\xe2\x80\x99 perceived intent to coerce the States. Post, at 42\xe2\x80\x9346; see, e.g., post, at 42 (\xe2\x80\x9cIn crafting the ACA, Congress clearly expressed its informed view that no State could possibly refuse the offer that the ACA extends.\xe2\x80\x9d). We should not lightly ascribe to Congress an intent to violate the Constitution (at least as my colleagues read it). This is particularly true when the ACA could just as well be comprehended as demonstrating Congress\xe2\x80\x99 mere expectation, in light of the uniformity of past participation and the generosity of the federal contribution, that States would not withdraw. Cf. South Dakota v. Dole, \n (\xe2\x80\x9cWe cannot conclude .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. that a con-ditional grant of federal money .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. is unconstitutional simply byreason of its success in achieving the congressional objective.\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\n
\n\n26\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Federal taxation of a State\xe2\x80\x99s citizens, according to the joint dissenters, may diminish a State\xe2\x80\x99s ability to raise new revenue. This, in turn, could limit a State\xe2\x80\x99s capacity to replace a federal program with an \xe2\x80\x9cequivalent\xe2\x80\x9d state-funded analog. Post, at 40. But it cannot be true that \xe2\x80\x9cthe amount of the federal taxes extracted from the taxpayers of a State to pay for the program in question is relevant in determining whether there is impermissible coercion.\xe2\x80\x9d Post, at 37. When the United States Government taxes United States citizens, it taxes them \xe2\x80\x9cin their individual capacities\xe2\x80\x9d as \xe2\x80\x9cthe people of America\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94not as residents of a particular State. See U.\xc2\xa0S. Term Limits, Inc. v. Thornton, \n (Kennedy, J., concurring). That is because the \xe2\x80\x9cFramers split the atom of sovereignty[,] .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. establishing two orders of government\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cone state and one federal\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ceach with its own direct relationship\xe2\x80\x9d to the people. Id., at 838.\nA State therefore has no claim on the money its residents pay in federal taxes, and federal \xe2\x80\x9cspending programs need not help people in all states in the same measure.\xe2\x80\x9d See Brief for David Satcher et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae 19. In 2004, for example, New Jersey received 55 centsin federal spending for every dollar its residents paid to the Federal Government in taxes, while Mississippi received $1.77 per tax dollar paid. C. Dubay, Tax Foundation, Federal Tax Burdens and Expenditures by State: Which States Gain Most from Federal Fiscal Operations? 2 (Mar. 2006). Thus no constitutional problem was created when Arizona declined for 16 years to participate in Medicaid, even though its residents\xe2\x80\x99 tax dollars financed Medicaid programs in every other State.\n\t\n
\n\n27\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0As The Chief Justice observes, the Secretary is authorized to withhold all of a State\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid funding. See ante, at 51. But total withdrawal is what the Secretary may, not must, do. She has discretion to withhold only a portion of the Medicaid funds otherwise due a noncompliant State. See \xc2\xa71396c; cf. 45 CFR \xc2\xa780.10(f) (2011) (Secretary may enforce Title VI\xe2\x80\x99s nondiscrimination requirement through \xe2\x80\x9crefusal to grant or continue Federal financial assistance, in whole or in part.\xe2\x80\x9d (emphasis added)). The Secretary, it is worth noting, may herself experience political pressures, which would make her all the more reluctant to cut off funds Congress has appropriated for a State\xe2\x80\x99s needy citizens.\n\t\n

\n\t\t\t\t\nFLORIDA, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS\n\t\t\t

\n\t\t\t\t11\xe2\x80\x93400\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tv.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tDEPARTMENT OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\ton writs of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the eleventh circuit\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t[June 28, 2012]\n\t\t\t

\n

\n

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Thomas, dissenting.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tI dissent for the reasons stated in our joint opinion, but I write separately to say a word about the Commerce Clause. The joint dissent and The Chief Justice cor- rectly apply our precedents to conclude that the Individual Mandate is beyond the power granted to Congress un- der the Commerce Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause. Under those precedents, Congress may regulate \xe2\x80\x9ceconomic activity [that] substantially affects interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Lopez, \n\n514 U.\xc2\xa0S. 549, \n560 (1995)\n. I adhere to my view that \xe2\x80\x9cthe very notion of a \xe2\x80\x98substantial effects\xe2\x80\x99 test under the Commerce Clause is inconsistent with the original understanding of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 powers and with this Court\xe2\x80\x99s early Commerce Clause cases.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Morrison, \n\n529 U.\xc2\xa0S. 598, \n627 (2000)\n (Thomas, J., concurring); see also Lopez, supra, at 584\xe2\x80\x93602 (Thomas, J., concurring); Gonzales v. Raich, \n\n545 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n\xe2\x80\x9369 (2005) (Thomas, J., dissenting). As I have explained, the Court\xe2\x80\x99s continued use of that test \xe2\x80\x9chas encouraged the Federal Government to persist in its view that the Commerce Clause has virtually no limits.\xe2\x80\x9d Morrison, supra, at 627. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s unprecedented claim in this suit that it may regulate not only economic activity but also inactivity that substantially affects interstate commerce is a case in point.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tSUPREME COURT OF THE UNITED STATES\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tNATIONAL FEDERATION OF INDEPENDENT BUSINESS, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t11\xe2\x80\x93393\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tv.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tKATHLEEN SEBELIUS, SECRETARY OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES, et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\nDEPARTMENT OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t11\xe2\x80\x93398\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tv.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tFLORIDA et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\nFLORIDA, et\xc2\xa0al., PETITIONERS\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t11\xe2\x80\x93400\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tv.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tDEPARTMENT OF HEALTH AND HUMAN SERVICES et\xc2\xa0al.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\ton writs of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the eleventh circuit\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t[June 28, 2012]\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tJustice Scalia, Justice Kennedy, Justice Thomas, and Justice Alito, dissenting.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress has set out to remedy the problem that the best health care is beyond the reach of many Americans who cannot afford it. It can assuredly do that, by exercising the powers accorded to it under the Constitution. The question in this case, however, is whether the complex structures and provisions of the Patient Protection and Affordable Care Act (Affordable Care Act or ACA) go be- yond those powers. We conclude that they do.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis case is in one respect difficult: it presents two questions of first impression. The first of those is whether failure to engage in economic activity (the purchase of health insurance) is subject to regulation under the Commerce Clause. Failure to act does result in an effect on commerce, and hence might be said to come under this Court\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9caffecting commerce\xe2\x80\x9d criterion of Commerce Clause jurisprudence. But in none of its decisions has this Court extended the Clause that far. The second question is whether the congressional power to tax and spend, U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa78, cl.\xc2\xa01, permits the conditioning of a State\xe2\x80\x99s continued receipt of all funds under a massive state-administered federal welfare program upon its acceptance of an expansion to that program. Several of our opinions have suggested that the power to tax and spend cannot be used to coerce state administration of a federal program, but we have never found a law enacted under the spending power to be coercive. Those questions are difficult.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe case is easy and straightforward, however, in another respect. What is absolutely clear, affirmed by the text of the 1789 Constitution, by the \n \nTenth Amendment ratified in 1791, and by innumerable cases of ours in the 220 years since, is that there are structural limits upon federal power\xe2\x80\x94upon what it can prescribe with respect to private conduct, and upon what it can impose upon the sovereign States. Whatever may be the conceptual limits upon the Commerce Clause and upon the power to tax and spend, they cannot be such as will enable the Federal Government to regulate all private conduct and to com- pel the States to function as administrators of federal programs.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat clear principle carries the day here. The striking case of Wickard v. Filburn, \n\n317 U.\xc2\xa0S. 111 (1942)\n, which held that the economic activity of growing wheat, even for one\xe2\x80\x99s own consumption, affected commerce sufficiently that it could be regulated, always has been regarded as the ne plus ultra of expansive Commerce Clause jurisprudence. To go beyond that, and to say the failure to grow wheat (which is not an economic activity, or any activity at all) nonetheless affects commerce and therefore can be federally regulated, is to make mere breathing in and out the basis for federal prescription and to extend federal power to virtually all human activity.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs for the constitutional power to tax and spend for the general welfare: The Court has long since expanded that beyond (what Madison thought it meant) taxing and spending for those aspects of the general welfare that were within the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated powers, see United States v. Butler, \n\n297 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n\xe2\x80\x9366 (1936). Thus, we now have sizable federal Departments devoted to subjects not mentioned among Congress\xe2\x80\x99 enumerated powers, and only marginally related to commerce: the De- partment of Education, the Department of Health and Human Services, the Department of Housing and Urban Development. The principal practical obstacle that prevents Congress from using the tax-and-spend power to assume all the general-welfare responsibilities traditionally exercised by the States is the sheer impossibility of managing a Federal Government large enough to administer such a system. That obstacle can be overcome by granting funds to the States, allowing them to administer the program. That is fair and constitutional enough when the States freely agree to have their powers employed and their employees enlisted in the federal scheme. But it is a blatant violation of the constitutional structure when the States have no choice.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Act before us here exceeds federal power both in mandating the purchase of health insurance and in denying nonconsenting States all Medicaid funding. These parts of the Act are central to its design and operation, and all the Act\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions would not have been enacted without them. In our view it must follow that the entire statute is inoperative.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tI\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Individual Mandate\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tArticle I, \xc2\xa78, of the Constitution gives Congress the power to \xe2\x80\x9cregulate Commerce .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. among the several States.\xe2\x80\x9d The Individual Mandate in the Act commands that every \xe2\x80\x9capplicable individual shall for each month beginning after 2013 ensure that the individual, and any dependent of the individual who is an applicable individ- ual, is covered under minimum essential coverage.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(a) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). If this provision \xe2\x80\x9cregulates\xe2\x80\x9d anything, it is the failure to maintain minimum essential coverage. One might argue that it regulates that failure by requiring it to be accompanied by payment of a penalty. But that failure\xe2\x80\x94that abstention from commerce\xe2\x80\x94is not \xe2\x80\x9cCommerce.\xe2\x80\x9d To be sure, purchasing insurance is \xe2\x80\x9dCommerce\xe2\x80\x9d; but one does not regulate commerce that does not exist by compelling its existence.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn Gibbons v. Ogden, 9 Wheat. 1, 196 (1824), Chief Justice Marshall wrote that the power to regulate commerce is the power \xe2\x80\x9cto prescribe the rule by which commerce is to be governed.\xe2\x80\x9d That understanding is con- sistent with the original meaning of \xe2\x80\x9cregulate\xe2\x80\x9d at the time of the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s ratification, when \xe2\x80\x9cto regulate\xe2\x80\x9d meant \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o adjust by rule, method or established mode,\xe2\x80\x9d 2 N. Webster, An American Dictionary of the English Language (1828); \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o adjust by rule or method,\xe2\x80\x9d 2 S. Johnson, A Dictionary of the English Language (7th ed. 1785); \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o adjust, to direct according to rule,\xe2\x80\x9d 2 J. Ash, New and Complete Dictionary of the English Language (1775); \xe2\x80\x9cto put in order, set to rights, govern or keep in order,\xe2\x80\x9d T. Dyche & W. Pardon, A New General English Dictionary \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t(16th ed. 1777).\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t It can mean to direct the manner of something but not to direct that something come into being. There is no instance in which this Court or Congress (or anyone else, to our knowledge) has used \xe2\x80\x9cregulate\xe2\x80\x9d in that peculiar fashion. If the word bore that meaning, Congress\xe2\x80\x99 authority \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o make Rules for the Govern- ment and Regulation of the land and naval Forces,\xe2\x80\x9d U.\xc2\xa0S. Const., Art. I, \xc2\xa78, cl. 14, would have made superfluous the later provision for authority \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o raise and support Armies,\xe2\x80\x9d id., \xc2\xa78, cl. 12, and \xe2\x80\x9c[t]o provide and maintain a Navy,\xe2\x80\x9d id., \xc2\xa78, cl. 13.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe do not doubt that the buying and selling of health insurance contracts is commerce generally subject to federal regulation. But when Congress provides that (nearly) all citizens must buy an insurance contract, it goes beyond \xe2\x80\x9cadjust[ing] by rule or method,\xe2\x80\x9d Johnson, supra, or \xe2\x80\x9cdirect[ing] according to rule,\xe2\x80\x9d Ash, supra; it directs the creation of commerce.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn response, the Government offers two theories as to why the Individual Mandate is nevertheless constitutional. Neither theory suffices to sustain its validity.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFirst, the Government submits that \xc2\xa75000A is \xe2\x80\x9cintegral to the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s insurance reforms\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cnecessary to make effective the Act\xe2\x80\x99s core reforms.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Petitioners in No.\xc2\xa011\xe2\x80\x93398 (Minimum Coverage Provision) 24 (hereinafter Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief). Congress included a \xe2\x80\x9cfinding\xe2\x80\x9d to similar effect in the Act \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\titself. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(H).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs discussed in more detail in Part V, infra, the Act contains numerous health insurance reforms, but most notable for present purposes are the \xe2\x80\x9cguaranteed issue\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ccommunity rating\xe2\x80\x9d provisions, \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg to 300gg\xe2\x80\x934. The former provides that, with a few exceptions, \xe2\x80\x9ceach health insurance issuer that offers health insurance coverage in the individual or group market in a State must accept every employer and individual in the State that applies for such coverage.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x931(a). That is, an insurer may not deny coverage on the basis of, among other things, any pre-existing medical condition that the applicant may have, and the resulting insurance must cover that condition. See \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x933.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tUnder ordinary circumstances, of course, insurers would respond by charging high premiums to individuals with pre-existing conditions. The Act seeks to prevent this through the community-rating provision. Simply put, the community-rating provision requires insurers to calculate an individual\xe2\x80\x99s insurance premium based on only four factors: (i) whether the individual\xe2\x80\x99s plan covers just the individual or his family also, (ii) the \xe2\x80\x9crating area\xe2\x80\x9d in which the individual lives, (iii) the individual\xe2\x80\x99s age, and (iv) whether the individual uses tobacco. \xc2\xa7300gg(a)(1)(A). Aside from the rough proxies of age and tobacco use (and possibly rating area), the Act does not allow an insurer to factor the individual\xe2\x80\x99s health characteristics into the price of his insurance premium. This creates a new incentive for young and healthy individuals without pre-existing conditions. The insurance premiums for those in this group will not reflect their own low actuarial risks but will subsidize insurance for others in the pool. Many of them may decide that purchasing health insurance is not an eco- nomically sound decision\xe2\x80\x94especially since the guaranteed-issue provision will enable them to purchase it at the same cost in later years and even if they have developed a pre-existing condition. But without the contribution of above-risk premiums from the young and healthy, the community-rating provision will not enable insurers to take on high-risk individuals without a massive increase in premiums.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government presents the Individual Mandate as a unique feature of a complicated regulatory scheme governing many parties with countervailing incentives that must be carefully balanced. Congress has imposed an extensive set of regulations on the health insurance industry, and compliance with those regulations will likely cost the in- dustry a great deal. If the industry does not respond by increasing premiums, it is not likely to survive. And if the industry does increase premiums, then there is a seri- ous risk that its products\xe2\x80\x94insurance plans\xe2\x80\x94will become economically undesirable for many and prohibitively ex- pensive for the rest.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis is not a dilemma unique to regulation of the health-insurance industry. Government regulation typically imposes costs on the regulated industry\xe2\x80\x94especially regulation that prohibits economic behavior in which most market participants are already engaging, such as \xe2\x80\x9cpiecing out\xe2\x80\x9d the market by selling the product to different classes of people at different prices (in the present context, providing much lower insurance rates to young and healthy buyers). And many industries so regulated face the reality that, without an artificial increase in demand, they cannot continue on. When Congress is regulating these industries directly, it enjoys the broad power to enact \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98all appropriate legislation\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98protec[t]\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98advanc[e]\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d commerce, NLRB v. Jones & Laughlin Steel Corp., \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1\n\xe2\x80\x9337 (1937) (quoting The Daniel Ball, 10 Wall. 557, 564 (1871)). Thus, Congress might protect the imperiled industry by prohibiting low-cost competition, or by according it preferential tax treatment, or even by granting it a direct subsidy.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tHere, however, Congress has impressed into service third parties, healthy individuals who could be but are not customers of the relevant industry, to offset the undesirable consequences of the regulation. Congress\xe2\x80\x99 desire to force these individuals to purchase insurance is motivated by the fact that they are further removed from the market than unhealthy individuals with pre-existing conditions, because they are less likely to need extensive care in the near future. If Congress can reach out and command even those furthest removed from an interstate market to participate in the market, then the Commerce Clause becomes a font of unlimited power, or in Hamilton\xe2\x80\x99s words, \xe2\x80\x9cthe hideous monster whose devouring jaws .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. spare neither sex nor age, nor high nor low, nor sacred nor profane.\xe2\x80\x9d The Federalist No. 33, p.\xc2\xa0202 (C. Rossiter ed. 1961).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAt the outer edge of the commerce power, this Court has insisted on careful scrutiny of regulations that do not act directly on an interstate market or its participants. In New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144 (1992)\n, we held that Congress could not, in an effort to regulate the disposal of radioactive waste produced in several different industries, order the States to take title to that waste. Id., at 174\xe2\x80\x93177. In Printz v.
