United States, \n\n521 U.\xc2\xa0S. 898 (1997)\n, we held that Congress could not, in an effort to regulate the distribution of firearms in the interstate mar- ket, compel state law-enforcement officials to perform background checks. Id., at 933\xe2\x80\x93935. In United States v. Lopez, \n\n514 U.\xc2\xa0S. 549 (1995)\n, we held that Congress could not, as a means of fostering an educated interstate labor market through the protection of schools, ban the possession of a firearm within a school zone. Id., at 559\xe2\x80\x93563. And in United States v. Morrison, \n\n529 U.\xc2\xa0S. 598 (2000)\n, we held that Congress could not, in an effort to ensure the full participation of women in the interstate economy, subject private individuals and companies to suit for gender-motivated violent torts. Id., at 609\xe2\x80\x93619. The lesson of these cases is that the Commerce Clause, even when sup- plemented by the Necessary and Proper Clause, is not carte blanche for doing whatever will help achieve the ends Congress seeks by the regulation of commerce. And the last two of these cases show that the scope of the Necessary and Proper Clause is exceeded not only when the congressional action directly violates the sovereignty of the States but also when it violates the background principle of enumerated (and hence limited) federal power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe case upon which the Government principally relies to sustain the Individual Mandate under the Necessary and Proper Clause is Gonzales v. Raich, \n\n545 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1 (2005)\n. That case held that Congress could, in an effort to restrain the interstate market in marijuana, ban the local cultivation and possession of that drug. Id., at 15\xe2\x80\x9322. Raich is no precedent for what Congress has done here. That case\xe2\x80\x99s prohibition of growing (cf. Wickard, \n\n317 U.\xc2\xa0S. 111\n), and of possession (cf. innumerable federal statutes) did not represent the expansion of the federal power to direct into a broad new field. The mandating of economic activity does, and since it is a field so limitless that it converts the Commerce Clause into a general authority to direct the economy, that mandating is not \xe2\x80\x9cconsist[ent] with the letter and spirit of the constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d McCulloch v. Maryland, 4 Wheat. 316, 421 (1819).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMoreover, Raich is far different from the Individual Mandate in another respect. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s opinion in Raich pointed out that the growing and possession prohibitions were the only practicable way of enabling the prohibition of interstate traffic in marijuana to be effectively enforced. 545 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 22. See also Shreveport Rate Cases, \n\n234 U.\xc2\xa0S. 342 (1914)\n (Necessary and Proper Clause allows regulations of intrastate transactions if necessary to the regulation of an interstate market). Intrastate marijuana could no more be distinguished from interstate marijuana than, for example, endangered-species trophies obtained before the species was federally protected can be distinguished from trophies obtained afterwards\xe2\x80\x94which made it necessary and proper to prohibit the sale of all such trophies, see Andrus v. Allard, \n\n444 U.\xc2\xa0S. 51 (1979)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWith the present statute, by contrast, there are many ways other than this unprecedented Individual Mandate by which the regulatory scheme\xe2\x80\x99s goals of reducing insurance premiums and ensuring the profitability of insurers could be achieved. For instance, those who did not purchase insurance could be subjected to a surcharge when they do enter the health insurance system. Or they could be denied a full income tax credit given to those who do purchase the insurance.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government was invited, at oral argument, to suggest what federal controls over private conduct (other than those explicitly prohibited by the Bill of Rights or other constitutional controls) could not be justified as necessary and proper for the carrying out of a general regulatory scheme. See Tr. of Oral Arg. 27\xe2\x80\x9330, 43\xe2\x80\x9345 (Mar. 27, 2012). It was unable to name any. As we said at the outset, whereas the precise scope of the Commerce Clause and the Necessary and Proper Clause is uncertain, the proposition that the Federal Government cannot do everything is a fundamental precept. See Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 564 (\xe2\x80\x9c[I]f we were to accept the Government\xe2\x80\x99s arguments, we are hard pressed to posit any activity by an in- dividual that Congress is without power to regulate\xe2\x80\x9d). Section 5000A is defeated by that proposition.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government\xe2\x80\x99s second theory in support of the In- dividual Mandate is that \xc2\xa75000A is valid because it is actually a \xe2\x80\x9cregulat[ion of] activities having a substantial relation to interstate commerce, .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. i.e., .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. activities that substantially affect interstate commerce.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 558\xe2\x80\x93559. See also Shreveport Rate Cases, supra. This argument takes a few different forms, but the basic idea is that \xc2\xa75000A regulates \xe2\x80\x9cthe way in which individuals finance their participation in the health-care market.\xe2\x80\x9d Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 33 (emphasis added). That is, the provision directs the manner in which individuals purchase health care services and related goods (directing that they be purchased through insurance) and is therefore a straightforward exercise of the commerce power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe primary problem with this argument is that \xc2\xa75000A does not apply only to persons who purchase all, or most, or even any, of the health care services or goods that the mandated insurance covers. Indeed, the main objection many have to the Mandate is that they have no intention of purchasing most or even any of such goods or services and thus no need to buy insurance for those purchases. The Government responds that the health-care market involves \xe2\x80\x9cessentially universal participation,\xe2\x80\x9d id., at 35. The principal difficulty with this response is that it is, in the only relevant sense, not true. It is true enough that everyone consumes \xe2\x80\x9chealth care,\xe2\x80\x9d if the term is taken to include the purchase of a bottle of aspirin. But the health care \xe2\x80\x9cmarket\xe2\x80\x9d that is the object of the Individual Mandate not only includes but principally consists of goods and services that the young people primarily affected by the Mandate do not purchase. They are quite simply not participants in that market, and cannot be made so (and thereby subjected to regulation) by the simple device of defining participants to include all those who will, later in their lifetime, probably purchase the goods or services covered by the mandated insurance.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Such a definition of market participants is unprecedented, and were it to be a premise for the exercise of national power, it would have no principled limits.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn a variation on this attempted exercise of federal power, the Government points out that Congress in this Act has purported to regulate \xe2\x80\x9ceconomic and financial decision[s] to forego [sic] health insurance coverage and [to] attempt to self-insure,\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(A), since those decisions have \xe2\x80\x9ca substantial and deleterious effect on interstate commerce,\xe2\x80\x9d Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 34. But as the discussion above makes clear, the decision to forgo participation in an interstate market is not itself commercial activity (or indeed any activity at all) within Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to regulate. It is true that, at the end of the day, it is inevitable that each American will affect commerce and become a part of it, even if not by choice. But if every person comes within the Commerce Clause power of Congress to regulate by the simple reason that he will one day engage in commerce, the idea of a limited Government power is at an end.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWickard v. Filburn has been regarded as the most expansive assertion of the commerce power in our history. A close second is Perez v. United States, \n\n402 U.\xc2\xa0S. 146 (1971)\n, which upheld a statute criminalizing the eminently local activity of loan-sharking. Both of those cases, however, \n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tinvolved commercial activity. To go beyond that, and to say that the failure to grow wheat or the refusal to make loans affects commerce, so that growing and lending can be federally compelled, is to extend federal power to virtually everything. All of us consume food, and when we do so the Federal Government can prescribe what its quality must be and even how much we must pay. But the mere fact that we all consume food and are thus, sooner or later, participants in the \xe2\x80\x9cmarket\xe2\x80\x9d for food, does not empower the Government to say when and what we will buy. That is essentially what this Act seeks to do with respect to the purchase of health care. It exceeds federal power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tA few respectful responses to Justice Ginsburg\xe2\x80\x99s dissent on the issue of the Mandate are in order. That dissent duly recites the test of Commerce Clause power that our opinions have applied, but disregards the premise the test contains. It is true enough that Congress needs only a \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98rational basis\xe2\x80\x99 for concluding that the regulated activity substantially affects interstate commerce,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 15 (em- phasis added). But it must be activity affecting com- merce that is regulated, and not merely the failure to engage in commerce. And one is not now purchasing the health care covered by the insurance mandate simply because one is likely to be purchasing it in the future. Our test\xe2\x80\x99s premise of regulated activity is not invented out of whole cloth, but rests upon the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s requirement that it be commerce which is regulated. If all inactivity affecting commerce is commerce, commerce is everything. Ultimately the dissent is driven to saying that there is really no difference between action and inaction, ante, at 26, a proposition that has never recommended itself, neither to the law nor to common sense. To say, for example, that the inaction here consists of activity in \xe2\x80\x9cthe self-insurance market,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., seems to us wordplay. By parity of reasoning the failure to buy a car can be called participation in the non-private-car-transportation market. Com- merce becomes everything.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe dissent claims that we \xe2\x80\x9cfai[l] to explain why the individual mandate threatens our constitutional order.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 35. But we have done so. It threatens that order because it gives such an expansive meaning to the Commerce Clause that all private conduct (including failure to act) becomes subject to federal control, effectively destroying the Constitution\xe2\x80\x99s division of governmental powers. Thus the dissent, on the theories proposed for the validity of the Mandate, would alter the accepted constitutional relation between the individual and the National Government. The dissent protests that the Necessary and Proper Clause has been held to include \xe2\x80\x9cthe power to enact criminal laws, .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. the power to imprison, .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. and the power to create a national bank,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 34\xe2\x80\x9335. Is not the power to compel purchase of health insurance much lesser? No, not if (unlike those other dispositions) its application rests upon a theory that everything is within federal control simply because it exists.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe dissent\xe2\x80\x99s exposition of the wonderful things the Fed- eral Government has achieved through exercise of its assigned powers, such as \xe2\x80\x9cthe provision of old-age and survivors\xe2\x80\x99 benefits\xe2\x80\x9d in the Social Security Act, ante, at 2, is quite beside the point. The issue here is whether the federal government can impose the Individual Mandate through the Commerce Clause. And the relevant history is not that Congress has achieved wide and wonderful results through the proper exercise of its assigned powers in the past, but that it has never before used the Commerce Clause to compel entry into commerce.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t The dissent treats the Constitution as though it is an enumeration of those problems that the Federal Government can address\xe2\x80\x94among which, it finds, is \xe2\x80\x9cthe Nation\xe2\x80\x99s course in the economic and social welfare realm,\xe2\x80\x9d ibid., and more specifically \xe2\x80\x9cthe problem of the uninsured,\xe2\x80\x9d ante, at 7. The Constitution is not that. It enumerates not federally soluble problems, but federally available powers. The Federal Government can address whatever problems it wants but can bring to their solution only those powers that the Constitution confers, among which is the power to regulate commerce. None of our cases say anything else. Article I contains no whatever-it-takes-to-solve-a-national-problem power.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe dissent dismisses the conclusion that the power to compel entry into the health-insurance market would include the power to compel entry into the new-car or broccoli markets. The latter purchasers, it says, \xe2\x80\x9cwill be obliged to pay at the counter before receiving the vehicle or nourishment,\xe2\x80\x9d whereas those refusing to purchase health-insurance will ultimately get treated anyway, at others\xe2\x80\x99 expense. Ante, at 21. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he unique attributes of the health-care market .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. give rise to a significant free-riding problem that does not occur in other markets.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 28. And \xe2\x80\x9ca vegetable-purchase mandate\xe2\x80\x9d (or a car-purchase mandate) is not \xe2\x80\x9clikely to have a substantial effect on the health-care costs\xe2\x80\x9d borne by other Americans. Ante, at 29. Those differences make a very good argument by the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s own lights, since they show that the failure to purchase health insurance, unlike the failure to purchase cars or broccoli, creates a national, social-welfare problem that is (in the dissent\xe2\x80\x99s view) included among the unenumerated \xe2\x80\x9cproblems\xe2\x80\x9d that the Constitution authorizes the Federal Government to solve. But those differences do not show that the failure to enter the health-insurance market, unlike the failure to buy cars and broccoli, is an activity that Congress can \xe2\x80\x9cregulate.\xe2\x80\x9d (Of course one day the failure of some of the public to purchase Amer- ican cars may endanger the existence of domestic automobile manufacturers; or the failure of some to eat broccoli may be found to deprive them of a newly discovered cancer- fighting chemical which only that food contains, producing health-care costs that are a burden on the rest of us\xe2\x80\x94in which case, under the theory of Justice Ginsburg\xe2\x80\x99s dissent, moving against those inactivities will also come within the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s unenumerated problem-solving powers.)\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Taxing Power\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAs far as \xc2\xa75000A is concerned, we would stop there. Congress has attempted to regulate beyond the scope of its Commerce Clause authority,\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t4\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t and \xc2\xa75000A is therefore invalid. The Government contends, however, as expressed in the caption to Part II of its brief, that \xe2\x80\x9cthe minimum coverage provision is independently authorized by congress\xe2\x80\x99s taxing power.\xe2\x80\x9d Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 52. The phrase \xe2\x80\x9cindependently authorized\xe2\x80\x9d suggests the existence of a creature never hitherto seen in the United States Reports: A penalty for constitutional purposes that is also a tax for constitutional purposes. In all our cases the two are mutually exclusive. The provision challenged under the Constitution is either a penalty or else a tax. Of course in many cases what was a regu- latory mandate enforced by a penalty could have been imposed as a tax upon permissible action; or what was im- posed as a tax upon permissible action could have been a regulatory mandate enforced by a penalty. But we know of no case, and the Government cites none, in which the imposition was, for constitutional purposes, both.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t5\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t The two are mutually exclusive. Thus, what the Government\xe2\x80\x99s caption should have read was \xe2\x80\x9calternatively, the minimum coverage provision is not a mandate-with-penalty but a tax.\xe2\x80\x9d It is important to bear this in mind in evaluating the tax argument of the Government and of those who support it: The issue is not whether Congress had the power to frame the minimum-coverage provision as a tax, but whether it did so.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn answering that question we must, if \xe2\x80\x9cfairly possible,\xe2\x80\x9d Crowell v. Benson, \n\n285 U.\xc2\xa0S. 22, \n62 (1932)\n, construe the provision to be a tax rather than a mandate-with-penalty, since that would render it constitutional rather than un- constitutional (ut res magis valeat quam pereat). But we cannot rewrite the statute to be what it is not. \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9c[A]l- though this Court will often strain to construe legis- lation so as to save it against constitutional attack, it must not and will not carry this to the point of perverting the purpose of a statute .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d or judicially rewriting it.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Commodity Futures Trading Comm\xe2\x80\x99n v. Schor, \n\n478 U.\xc2\xa0S. 833, \n841 (1986)\n (quoting Aptheker v. Secretary of State, \n\n378 U.\xc2\xa0S. 500, \n515 (1964)\n, in turn quoting Scales v. United States, \n\n367 U.\xc2\xa0S. 203, \n211 (1961)\n). In this case, there is simply no way, \xe2\x80\x9cwithout doing violence to the fair meaning of the words used,\xe2\x80\x9d Grenada County Supervisors v. Brogden, \n\n112 U.\xc2\xa0S. 261, \n269 (1884)\n, to escape what Congress enacted: a mandate that individuals maintain minimum essential coverage, enforced by a penalty.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOur cases establish a clear line between a tax and a penalty: \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98[A] tax is an enforced contribution to provide for the support of government; a penalty .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. is an exaction imposed by statute as punishment for an unlawful act.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Reorganized CF&I Fabricators of Utah, Inc., \n\n518 U.\xc2\xa0S. 213, \n224 (1996)\n (quoting United States v. La Franca, \n\n282 U.\xc2\xa0S. 568, \n572 (1931)\n). In a few cases, this Court has held that a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d imposed upon private conduct was so onerous as to be in effect a penalty. But we have never held\xe2\x80\x94never\xe2\x80\x94that a penalty imposed for violation of the law was so trivial as to be in effect a tax. We have never held that any exaction imposed for violation of the law is an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 taxing power\xe2\x80\x94even when the statute calls it a tax, much less when (as here) the statute repeatedly calls it a penalty. When an act \xe2\x80\x9cadopt[s] the criteria of wrongdoing\xe2\x80\x9d and then imposes a monetary penalty as the \xe2\x80\x9cprincipal consequence on those who transgress its standard,\xe2\x80\x9d it creates a regulatory penalty, not a tax. Child Labor Tax Case, \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 20, \n38 (1922)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSo the question is, quite simply, whether the exaction here is imposed for violation of the law. It unquestion- ably is. The minimum-coverage provision is found in \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A, entitled \xe2\x80\x9cRequirement to maintain minimum essential coverage.\xe2\x80\x9d (Emphasis added.) It commands that every \xe2\x80\x9capplicable individual shall .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. ensure that the individual .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. is covered under minimum essential coverage.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (emphasis added). And the immediately following provision states that, \xe2\x80\x9c[i]f .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. an applicable individual .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. fails to meet the requirement of subsection (a) .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. there is hereby imposed .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. a penalty.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa75000A(b) (emphasis added). And several of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 legislative \xe2\x80\x9cfindings\xe2\x80\x9d with regard to \xc2\xa75000A confirm that it sets forth a legal requirement and constitutes the assertion of regulatory power, not mere taxing power. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(A) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe requirement regulates activity .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(C) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe requirement .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. will add millions of new consumers to the health insurance market .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(D) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe requirement achieves near-universal coverage\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(H) (\xe2\x80\x9cThe requirement is an essential part of this larger regulation of economic activity, and the absence of the requirement would undercut Federal regulation of the health insurance market\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(3) (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Supreme Court of the United States ruled that insurance is interstate commerce subject to Federal regulation\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government and those who support its view on the tax point rely on New York v. United States, \n\n505 U.\xc2\xa0S. 144\n, to justify reading \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d to mean \xe2\x80\x9cmay.\xe2\x80\x9d The \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d in that case was contained in an introductory provision\xe2\x80\x94a recital that provided for no legal consequences\xe2\x80\x94which said that \xe2\x80\x9c[e]ach State shall be responsible for providing .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. for the disposal of .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. low-level radioactive waste.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa72021c(a)(1)(A). The Court did not hold that \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d could be construed to mean \xe2\x80\x9cmay,\xe2\x80\x9d but rather that this preliminary provision could not impose upon the oper- ative provisions of the Act a mandate that they did not contain: \xe2\x80\x9cWe .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. decline petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 invitation to con- strue \xc2\xa72021c(a)(1)(A), alone and in isolation, as a command to the States independent of the remainder of the Act.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 170. Our opinion then proceeded to \xe2\x80\x9cconsider each [of the three operative provisions] in turn.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Here the mandate\xe2\x80\x94the \xe2\x80\x9cshall\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94is contained not in an inoperative preliminary recital, but in the dispositive operative provision itself. New York provides no support for reading it to be permissive.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tQuite separately, the fact that Congress (in its own words) \xe2\x80\x9cimposed .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. a penalty,\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(b)(1), for failure to buy insurance is alone sufficient to render that failure unlawful. It is one of the canons of interpretation that a statute that penalizes an act makes it unlawful: \xe2\x80\x9c[W]here the statute inflicts a penalty for doing an act, although the act itself is not expressly prohibited, yet to do the act is unlawful, because it cannot be supposed that the Legislature intended that a penalty should be inflicted for a lawful act.\xe2\x80\x9d Powhatan Steamboat Co. v. Appomattox R. Co., 24 How. 247, 252 (1861). Or in the words of Chancellor Kent: \xe2\x80\x9cIf a statute inflicts a penalty for doing an act, the penalty implies a prohibition, and the thing is unlawful, though there be no prohibitory words in the statute.\xe2\x80\x9d 1 J. Kent, Commentaries on American Law 436 (1826).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe never have classified as a tax an exaction imposed for violation of the law, and so too, we never have classified as a tax an exaction described in the legislation itself as a penalty. To be sure, we have sometimes treated as a tax a statutory exaction (imposed for something other than a violation of law) which bore an agnostic label that does not entail the significant constitutional consequences of a penalty\xe2\x80\x94such as \xe2\x80\x9clicense\xe2\x80\x9d (License Tax Cases, 5 Wall. 462 (1867)) or \xe2\x80\x9csurcharge\xe2\x80\x9d (New York v. United States, supra.). But we have never\xe2\x80\x94never\xe2\x80\x94treated as a tax an exaction which faces up to the critical difference between a tax and a penalty, and explicitly denominates the exaction a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty.\xe2\x80\x9d Eighteen times in \xc2\xa75000A itself and else- where throughout the Act, Congress called the exaction in \xc2\xa75000A(b) a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat \xc2\xa75000A imposes not a simple tax but a mandate to which a penalty is attached is demonstrated by the fact that some are exempt from the tax who are not ex- empt from the mandate\xe2\x80\x94a distinction that would make no sense if the mandate were not a mandate. Section 5000A(d) exempts three classes of people from the definition of \xe2\x80\x9capplicable individual\xe2\x80\x9d subject to the minimum coverage requirement: Those with religious objections or who participate in a \xe2\x80\x9chealth care sharing ministry,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa75000A(d)(2); those who are \xe2\x80\x9cnot lawfully present\xe2\x80\x9d in the United States, \xc2\xa75000A(d)(3); and those who are incarcerated, \xc2\xa75000A(d)(4). Section 5000A(e) then creates a separate set of exemptions, excusing from liability for the penalty certain individuals who are subject to the minimum coverage requirement: Those who cannot afford coverage, \xc2\xa75000A(e)(1); who earn too little income to require filing a tax return, \xc2\xa75000A(e)(2); who are members of an Indian tribe, \xc2\xa75000A(e)(3); who experience only short gaps in coverage, \xc2\xa75000A(e)(4); and who, in the judgment of the Secretary of Health and Human Services, \xe2\x80\x9chave suffered a hardship with respect to the capability to obtain coverage,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa75000A(e)(5). If \xc2\xa75000A were a tax, these two classes of exemption would make no sense; there being no requirement, all the exemptions would attach to the penalty (renamed tax) alone.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the face of all these indications of a regulatory requirement accompanied by a penalty, the Solicitor General assures us that \xe2\x80\x9cneither the Treasury Department nor the Department of Health and Human Services interprets Section 5000A as imposing a legal obligation,\xe2\x80\x9d Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 61, and that \xe2\x80\x9c[i]f [those subject to the Act] pay the tax penalty, they\xe2\x80\x99re in compliance with the law,\xe2\x80\x9d Tr. of Oral Arg. 50 (Mar. 26, 2012). These self-serving litigating positions are entitled to no weight. What counts is what the statute says, and that is entirely clear. It is worth noting, moreover, that these assurances contradict the Government\xe2\x80\x99s position in related litigation. Shortly before the Affordable Care Act was passed, the Commonwealth of Virginia enacted Va. Code Ann. \xc2\xa738.2\xe2\x80\x933430.1:1 (Lexis Supp. 2011), which states, \xe2\x80\x9cNo resident of [the] Commonwealth .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. shall be required to obtain or maintain a policy of individual insurance coverage except as required by a court or the Department of Social Services .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0.\xe2\x80\x9d In opposing Virginia\xe2\x80\x99s assertion of standing to challenge \xc2\xa75000A based on this statute, the Government said that \xe2\x80\x9cif the minimum coverage provision is unconstitutional, the [Virginia] statute is unnecessary, and if the minimum coverage provision is upheld, the state statute is void under the Supremacy Clause.\xe2\x80\x9d Brief for Appellant in No. 11\xe2\x80\x931057 etc. (CA4), p.\xc2\xa029. But it would be void under the Supremacy Clause only if it was contradicted by a federal \xe2\x80\x9crequire[ment] to obtain or maintain a policy of individual insurance coverage.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAgainst the mountain of evidence that the minimum coverage requirement is what the statute calls it\xe2\x80\x94a requirement\xe2\x80\x94and that the penalty for its violation is what the statute calls it\xe2\x80\x94a penalty\xe2\x80\x94the Government brings forward the flimsiest of indications to the contrary. It notes that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he minimum coverage provision amends the Internal Revenue Code to provide that a non-exempted individual . . . will owe a monetary penalty, in addition to the income tax itself,\xe2\x80\x9d and that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he [Internal Revenue Service (IRS)] will assess and collect the penalty in the same manner as assessable penalties under the Internal Revenue Code.\xe2\x80\x9d Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 53. The manner of collection could perhaps suggest a tax if IRS penalty-collection were unheard-of or rare. It is not. See, e.g., \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7527(j) (2006 ed.) (IRS-collectible pen- alty for failure to make campaign-finance disclosures); \xc2\xa75761(c) (IRS-collectible penalty for domestic sales of to- bacco products labeled for export); \xc2\xa79707 (IRS-collectible penalty for failure to make required health-insurance premium payments on behalf of mining employees). In Reorganized CF&I Fabricators of Utah, Inc., \n\n518 U.\xc2\xa0S. 213\n, we held that an exaction not only enforced by the Commissioner of Internal Revenue but even called a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d was in fact a penalty. \xe2\x80\x9c[I]f the concept of penalty means anything,\xe2\x80\x9d we said, \xe2\x80\x9cit means punishment for an unlawful act or omission.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 224. See also Lipke v. Lederer, \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 557 (1922)\n (same). Moreover, while the penalty is assessed and collected by the IRS, \xc2\xa75000A is administered both by that agency and by the Department of Health and Human Services (and also the Secretary of Veteran Affairs), see \xc2\xa75000A(e)(1)(D), (e)(5), (f)(1)(A)(v), (f)(1)(E) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), which is responsible for defining its substantive scope\xe2\x80\x94a feature that would be quite extraordinary for taxes.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government points out that \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he amount of the penalty will be calculated as a percentage of household income for federal income tax purposes, subject to a floor and [a] ca[p],\xe2\x80\x9d and that individuals who earn so little money that they \xe2\x80\x9care not required to file income tax returns for the taxable year are not subject to the penalty\xe2\x80\x9d (though they are, as we discussed earlier, subject to the mandate). Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 12, 53. But varying a penalty according to ability to pay is an utterly familiar practice. See, e.g., \n\n33 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71319(d) (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (\xe2\x80\x9cIn determining the amount of a civil penalty the court shall consider .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. the economic impact of the penalty on the violator\xe2\x80\x9d); see also \n\n6 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7488e(c); 7 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa77734(b)(2), 8313(b)(2); 12 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71701q\xe2\x80\x931(d)(3), 1723i(c)(3), 1735f\xe2\x80\x9314(c)(3), 1735f\xe2\x80\x9315(d)(3), 4585(c)(2); 15 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa745(m)(1)(C), 77h\xe2\x80\x931(g)(3), 78u\xe2\x80\x932(d), 80a\xe2\x80\x939(d)(4), 80b\xe2\x80\x933(i)(4), 1681s(a)(2)(B), 1717a(b)(3), 1825(b)(1), 2615(a) (2)(B), 5408(b)(2); \n\n33 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa72716a(a).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe last of the feeble arguments in favor of petition- ers that we will address is the contention that what this statute repeatedly calls a penalty is in fact a tax because it contains no scienter requirement. The presence of such a requirement suggests a penalty\xe2\x80\x94though one can imagine a tax imposed only on willful action; but the absence of such a requirement does not suggest a tax. Penalties for absolute-liability offenses are commonplace. And where a statute is silent as to scienter, we traditionally presume a mens rea requirement if the statute imposes a \xe2\x80\x9csevere penalty.\xe2\x80\x9d Staples v. United States, \n\n511 U.\xc2\xa0S. 600, \n618 (1994)\n. Since we have an entire jurisprudence addressing when it is that a scienter requirement should be inferred from a penalty, it is quite illogical to suggest that a penalty is not a penalty for want of an express scienter requirement.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAnd the nail in the coffin is that the mandate and penalty are located in Title I of the Act, its operative core, rather than where a tax would be found\xe2\x80\x94in Title IX, containing the Act\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cRevenue Provisions.\xe2\x80\x9d In sum, \xe2\x80\x9cthe terms of [the] act rende[r] it unavoidable,\xe2\x80\x9d Parsons v. Bedford, 3 Pet. 433, 448 (1830), that Congress imposed a regulatory penalty, not a tax.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor all these reasons, to say that the Individual Mandate merely imposes a tax is not to interpret the statute but to rewrite it. Judicial tax-writing is particularly troubl- ing. Taxes have never been popular, see, e.g., Stamp Act of 1765, and in part for that reason, the Constitution requires tax increases to originate in the House of Representatives. See Art. I, \xc2\xa77, cl.\xc2\xa01. That is to say, they must originate in the legislative body most accountable to the people, where legislators must weigh the need for the tax against the terrible price they might pay at their next election, which is never more than two years off. The Federalist No. 58 \xe2\x80\x9cdefend[ed] the decision to give the origination power to the House on the ground that the Chamber that is more accountable to the people should have the primary role in raising revenue.\xe2\x80\x9d United States v. Munoz-Flores, \n\n495 U.\xc2\xa0S. 385, \n395 (1990)\n. We have no doubt that Congress knew precisely what it was doing when it rejected an earlier version of this legislation that imposed a tax instead of a requirement-with-penalty. See Affordable Health Care for America Act, H.\xc2\xa0R. 3962, 111th Cong., 1st Sess., \xc2\xa7501 (2009); America\xe2\x80\x99s Healthy Future Act of 2009, S. 1796, 111th Cong., 1st Sess., \xc2\xa71301. Imposing a tax through judicial legislation inverts the constitutional scheme, and places the power to tax in the branch of government least accountable to the citizenry.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFinally, we must observe that rewriting \xc2\xa75000A as a tax in order to sustain its constitutionality would force us to confront a difficult constitutional question: whether this is a direct tax that must be apportioned among the States according to their population. Art.\xc2\xa0I, \xc2\xa79, cl. 4. Perhaps it is not (we have no need to address the point); but the meaning of the Direct Tax Clause is famously unclear, and its application here is a question of first impression that deserves more thoughtful consideration than the lick-and-a-promise accorded by the Government and its supporters. The Government\xe2\x80\x99s opening brief did not even address the question\xe2\x80\x94perhaps because, until today, no federal court has accepted the implausible argument that \xc2\xa75000A is an exercise of the tax power. And once respondents raised the issue, the Government devoted a mere 21 lines of its reply brief to the issue. Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Reply Brief 25. At oral argument, the most prolonged statement about the issue was just over 50 words. Tr. of Oral Arg. 79 (Mar. 27, 2012). One would expect this Court to demand more than fly-by-night briefing and argument before deciding a difficult constitutional question of first impression.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIII\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Anti-Injunction Act\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThere is another point related to the Individual Mandate that we must discuss\xe2\x80\x94a point that logically should have been discussed first: Whether jurisdiction over the challenges to the minimum-coverage provision is precluded by the Anti-Injunction Act, which provides that \xe2\x80\x9cno suit for the purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax shall be maintained in any court by any person,\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa77421(a) (2006 ed.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe have left the question to this point because it seemed to us that the dispositive question whether the minimum-coverage provision is a tax is more appropriately addressed in the significant constitutional context of whether it is an exercise of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 taxing power. Having found that it is not, we have no difficulty in deciding that these suits do not have \xe2\x80\x9cthe purpose of restraining the assessment or collection of any tax.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t6\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government and those who support its position on this point make the remarkable argument that \xc2\xa75000A is not a tax for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act, see Brief for Petitioners in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398 (Anti-Injunction Act), but is a tax for constitutional purposes, see Petitioners\xe2\x80\x99 Minimum Coverage Brief 52\xe2\x80\x9362. The rhetorical device that tries to cloak this argument in superficial plausibility is the same device employed in arguing that for constitutional purposes the minimum-coverage provision is a tax: confusing the question of what Congress did with the question of what Congress could have done. What qualifies as a tax for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act, unlike what qualifies as a tax for purposes of the Constitution, is entirely within the control of Congress. Compare Bailey v. George, \n\n259 U.\xc2\xa0S. 16, \n20 (1922)\n (Anti-Injunction Act barred suit to restrain collections under the Child Labor Tax Law), with Child Labor Tax Case, 259 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 36\xe2\x80\x9341 (holding the same law unconstitutional as exceeding Congress\xe2\x80\x99 taxing power). Congress could have defined \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d for purposes of that statute in such fashion as to exclude some exactions that in fact are \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes.\xe2\x80\x9d It might have prescribed, for example, that a particular exercise of the taxing power \xe2\x80\x9cshall not be regarded as a tax for purposes of the Anti-Injunction Act.\xe2\x80\x9d But there is no such prescription here. What the Government would have us believe in these cases is that the very same textual indications that show this is not a tax under the Anti-Injunction Act show that it is a tax under the Constitution. That carries ver- bal wizardry too far, deep into the forbidden land of the sophists.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tIV\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Medicaid Expansion\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe now consider respondents\xe2\x80\x99 second challenge to the constitutionality of the ACA, namely, that the Act\xe2\x80\x99s dramatic expansion of the Medicaid program exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to attach conditions to federal grants to the States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe ACA does not legally compel the States to participate in the expanded Medicaid program, but the Act authorizes a severe sanction for any State that refuses to go along: termination of all the State\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid funding. For the average State, the annual federal Medicaid subsidy is equal to more than one-fifth of the State\xe2\x80\x99s expenditures.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t7\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t A State forced out of the program would not only lose this huge sum but would almost certainly find it necessary to increase its own health-care expenditures substantially, requiring either a drastic reduction in funding for other programs or a large increase in state taxes. And these new taxes would come on top of the federal taxes already paid by the State\xe2\x80\x99s citizens to fund the Medicaid program in other States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe States challenging the constitutionality of the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid Expansion contend that, for these practical reasons, the Act really does not give them any choice at all. As proof of this, they point to the goal and the struc- ture of the ACA. The goal of the Act is to provide near-universal medical coverage, \n\n42 U. S. C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(D), and without 100% State participation in the Medicaid program, attainment of this goal would be thwarted. Even if States could elect to remain in the old Medicaid program, while declining to participate in the Expansion, there would be a gaping hole in coverage. And if a substantial number of States were entirely expelled from the program, the number of persons without coverage would be even higher.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn light of the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s goal of near-universal coverage, petitioners argue, if Congress had thought that anything less than 100% state participation was a realistic possibility, Congress would have provided a backup scheme. But no such scheme is to be found anywhere in the more than 900 pages of the Act. This shows, they maintain, that Congress was certain that the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid offer was one that no State could refuse.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn response to this argument, the Government contends that any congressional assumption about uniform state participation was based on the simple fact that the offer of federal funds associated with the expanded coverage is such a generous gift that no State would want to turn it down.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tTo evaluate these arguments, we consider the extent of the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s power to spend money and to attach conditions to money granted to the States.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tNo one has ever doubted that the Constitution authorizes the Federal Government to spend money, but for many years the scope of this power was unsettled. The Constitution grants Congress the power to collect taxes \xe2\x80\x9cto .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. provide for the .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. general Welfare of the United States,\xe2\x80\x9d Art. I, \xc2\xa78, cl. 1, and from \xe2\x80\x9cthe foundation of the Nation sharp differences of opinion have persisted as to the true interpretation of the phrase\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cthe general welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d Butler, 297 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 65. Madison, it has been said, thought that the phrase \xe2\x80\x9camounted to no more than a reference to the other powers enumerated in the subsequent clauses of the same section,\xe2\x80\x9d while Hamilton \xe2\x80\x9cmaintained the clause confers a power separate and distinct from those later enumerated [and] is not restricted in meaning by the grant of them.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court resolved this dispute in Butler. Writing for the Court, Justice Roberts opined that the Madisonian view would make Article I\xe2\x80\x99s grant of the spending power a \xe2\x80\x9cmere tautology.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. To avoid that, he adopted Hamilton\xe2\x80\x99s approach and found that \xe2\x80\x9cthe power of Congress to authorize expenditure of public moneys for public purposes is not limited by the direct grants of legislative power found in the Constitution.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 66. Instead, he wrote, the spending power\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cconfines are set in the clause which confers it, and not in those of section 8 which bestow and define the legislative powers of the Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid.; see also Steward Machine Co. v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 548\n\xe2\x80\x93587 (1937); Helvering v. Davis, \n\n301 U.\xc2\xa0S. 619, \n640 (1937)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe power to make any expenditure that furthers \xe2\x80\x9cthe general welfare\xe2\x80\x9d is obviously very broad, and shortly after Butler was decided the Court gave Congress wide leeway to decide whether an expenditure qualifies. See Helvering, 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 640\xe2\x80\x93641. \xe2\x80\x9cThe discretion belongs to Congress,\xe2\x80\x9d the Court wrote, \xe2\x80\x9cunless the choice is clearly wrong, a display of arbitrary power, not an exercise of judgment.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 640. Since that time, the Court has never held that a federal expenditure was not for \xe2\x80\x9cthe general welfare.\xe2\x80\x9d\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOne way in which Congress may spend to promote the general welfare is by making grants to the States. Monetary grants, so-called grants-in-aid, became more frequent during the 1930\xe2\x80\x99s, G. Stephens & N. Wikstrom, Ameri- can Intergovernmental Relations\xe2\x80\x94A Fragmented Federal Polity 83 (2007), and by 1950 they had reached $20 billion\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t8\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t or 11.6% of state and local government expenditures from their own sources.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t9\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t By 1970 this number had grown to $123.7 billion\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t10\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t or 29.1% of state and local government expenditures from their own sources.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t11\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t As of 2010, fed- eral outlays to state and local governments came to over $608 billion or 37.5% of state and local government expenditures.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t12\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen Congress makes grants to the States, it customarily attaches conditions, and this Court has long held that the Constitution generally permits Congress to do this. See Pennhurst State School and Hospital v. Halderman, \n\n451 U.\xc2\xa0S. 1, \n17 (1981)\n; South Dakota v. Dole, \n\n483 U.\xc2\xa0S. 203, \n206 (1987)\n; Fullilove v. Klutznick, \n\n448 U. S. 448, \n474 (1980)\n (opinion of Burger, C.\xc2\xa0J.); Steward Machine, supra, at 593.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis practice of attaching conditions to federal funds greatly increases federal power. \xe2\x80\x9c[O]bjectives not thought to be within Article I\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated legislative fields, may nevertheless be attained through the use of the spending power and the conditional grant of federal funds.\xe2\x80\x9d Dole, supra, at 207 (internal quotation marks and citation omitted); see also College Savings Bank v. Florida Prepaid Postsecondary Ed. Expense Bd., \n\n527 U. S. 666, \n686 (1999)\n (by attaching conditions to federal funds, Congress may induce the States to \xe2\x80\x9ctak[e] certain actions that Congress could not require them to take\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis formidable power, if not checked in any way, would present a grave threat to the system of federalism created by our Constitution. If Congress\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x9cSpending Clause power to pursue objectives outside of Article I\xe2\x80\x99s enumerated legislative fields,\xe2\x80\x9d Davis v. Monroe County Bd. of Ed., \n\n526 U.\xc2\xa0S. 629, \n654 (1999)\n (Kennedy, J., dissenting) (internal quotation marks omitted), is \xe2\x80\x9climited only by Congress\xe2\x80\x99 notion of the general welfare, the reality, given the vast financial resources of the Federal Government, is that the Spending Clause gives \xe2\x80\x98power to the Congress to tear down the barriers, to invade the states\xe2\x80\x99 jurisdiction, and to become a parliament of the whole people, subject to no restrictions save such as are self-imposed,\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Dole, supra, at 217 (O\xe2\x80\x99Connor, J., dissenting) (quoting Butler, 297 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 78). \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Spending Clause power, if wielded without concern for the federal balance, has the potential to obliterate distinctions between national and local spheres of interest and power by permitting the Federal Government to set policy in the most sensitive areas of traditional state concern, areas which otherwise would lie outside its reach.\xe2\x80\x9d Davis, supra, at 654\xe2\x80\x93655 (Kennedy, J., dissenting).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tRecognizing this potential for abuse, our cases have long held that the power to attach conditions to grants to the States has limits. See, e.g., Dole, supra, at 207\xe2\x80\x93208; id., at 207 (spending power is \xe2\x80\x9csubject to several general re- strictions articulated in our cases\xe2\x80\x9d). For one thing, any such conditions must be unambiguous so that a State at least knows what it is getting into. See Pennhurst, supra, at 17. Conditions must also be related \xe2\x80\x9cto the federal interest in particular national projects or programs,\xe2\x80\x9d Massachusetts v. United States, \n\n435 U.\xc2\xa0S. 444, \n461 (1978)\n, and the conditional grant of federal funds may not \xe2\x80\x9cinduce the States to engage in activities that would themselves be unconstitutional,\xe2\x80\x9d Dole, supra, at 210; see Lawrence County v. Lead-Deadwood School Dist. No. 40\xe2\x80\x931, \n\n469 U.\xc2\xa0S. 256\n\xe2\x80\x93270 (1985). Finally, while Congress may seek to induce States to accept conditional grants, Congress may not cross the \xe2\x80\x9cpoint at which pressure turns into compulsion, and ceases to be inducement.\xe2\x80\x9d Steward Machine, 301 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 590. Accord, College Savings Bank, supra, at 687; Metropolitan Washington Airports Authority v. Citizens for Abatement of Aircraft Noise, Inc., \n\n501 U.\xc2\xa0S. 252, \n285 (1991)\n (White, J., dissenting); Dole, supra, at 211.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen federal legislation gives the States a real choice whether to accept or decline a federal aid package, the federal-state relationship is in the nature of a contractual relationship. See Barnes v. Gorman, \n\n536 U.\xc2\xa0S. 181, \n186 (2002)\n; Pennhurst, 451 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 17. And just as a contract is voidable if coerced, \xe2\x80\x9c[t]he legitimacy of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power to legislate under the spending power . . . rests on whether the State voluntarily and knowingly accepts the terms of the \xe2\x80\x98contract.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. (emphasis added). If a federal spending program coerces participation the States have not \xe2\x80\x9cexercise[d] their choice\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94let alone made an \xe2\x80\x9cinformed choice.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 17, 25.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCoercing States to accept conditions risks the destruction of the \xe2\x80\x9cunique role of the States in our system.\xe2\x80\x9d Davis, supra, at 685 (Kennedy, J., dissenting). \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he Constitution has never been understood to confer upon Congress the ability to require the States to govern according to Congress\xe2\x80\x99 instructions.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 162. Congress may not \xe2\x80\x9csimply commandeer the legislative processes of the States by directly compelling them to enact and enforce a federal regulatory program.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 161 (internal quotation marks and brackets omitted). Congress effectively engages in this impermissible compulsion when state participation in a federal spending program is coerced, so that the States\xe2\x80\x99 choice whether to enact or administer a federal regulatory program is rendered illusory.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhere all Congress has done is to \xe2\x80\x9cencourag[e] state regulation rather than compe[l] it, state governments remain responsive to the local electorate\xe2\x80\x99s preferences; state officials remain accountable to the people. [But] where the Federal Government compels States to regulate, the accountability of both state and federal officials is diminished.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, supra, at 168.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAmici who support the Government argue that forcing state employees to implement a federal program is more respectful of federalism than using federal workers to implement that program. See, e.g., Brief for Service Employees International Union et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93398, pp. 25\xe2\x80\x9326. They note that Congress, instead of expanding Medicaid, could have established an entirely federal program to provide coverage for the same group of people. By choosing to structure Medicaid as a cooperative federal-state program, they contend, Congress allows for more state control. Ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis argument reflects a view of federalism that our cases have rejected\xe2\x80\x94and with good reason. When Congress compels the States to do its bidding, it blurs the lines of political accountability. If the Federal Government makes a controversial decision while acting on its own, \xe2\x80\x9cit is the Federal Government that makes the decision in full view of the public, and it will be federal officials that suffer the consequences if the decision turns out to be detrimental or unpopular.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 168. But when the Federal Government compels the States to take unpopular actions, \xe2\x80\x9cit may be state officials who will bear the brunt of public disapproval, while the federal officials who devised the regulatory program may remain insulated from the electoral ramifications of their decision.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 169; see Printz, supra, at 930. For this reason, federal officeholders may view this \xe2\x80\x9cdepartur[e] from the federal structure to be in their personal interests .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. as a means of shifting responsibility for the eventual decision.\xe2\x80\x9d New York, 505 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 182\xe2\x80\x93183. And even state officials may favor such a \xe2\x80\x9cdeparture from the constitutional plan,\xe2\x80\x9d since uncertainty concerning responsibility may also permit them to escape accountability. Id., at 182. If a program is popular, state officials may claim credit; if it is unpopular, they may protest that they were merely responding to a federal directive.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOnce it is recognized that spending-power legislation cannot coerce state participation, two questions remain: (1) What is the meaning of coercion in this context? (2) Is the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s expanded Medicaid coverage coercive? We now turn to those questions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tD\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe answer to the first of these questions\xe2\x80\x94the meaning of coercion in the present context\xe2\x80\x94is straightforward. As we have explained, the legitimacy of attaching conditions to federal grants to the States depends on the voluntariness of the States\xe2\x80\x99 choice to accept or decline the offered package. Therefore, if States really have no choice other than to accept the package, the offer is coercive, and the conditions cannot be sustained under the spending power. And as our decision in South Dakota v. Dole makes clear, theoretical voluntariness is not enough.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn South Dakota v. Dole, we considered whether the spending power permitted Congress to condition 5% of the State\xe2\x80\x99s federal highway funds on the State\xe2\x80\x99s adoption of a minimum drinking age of 21 years. South Dakota argued that the program was impermissibly coercive, but we disagreed, reasoning that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress ha[d] directed only that a State desiring to establish a minimum drinking age lower than 21 lose a relatively small percentage of certain federal highway funds.\xe2\x80\x9d 483 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 211. Because \xe2\x80\x9call South Dakota would lose if she adhere[d] to her chosen course as to a suitable minimum drinking age [was] 5% of the funds otherwise obtainable under specified high- way grant programs,\xe2\x80\x9d we found that \xe2\x80\x9cCongress ha[d] of- fered relatively mild encouragement to the States to enact higher minimum drinking ages than they would otherwise choose.\xe2\x80\x9d Ibid. Thus, the decision whether to comply with the federal condition \xe2\x80\x9cremain[ed] the prerogative of the States not merely in theory but in fact,\xe2\x80\x9d and so the program at issue did not exceed Congress\xe2\x80\x99 power. Id., at 211\xe2\x80\x93212 (emphasis added).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe question whether a law enacted under the spending power is coercive in fact will sometimes be difficult, but where Congress has plainly \xe2\x80\x9ccrossed the line distinguishing encouragement from coercion,\xe2\x80\x9d New York, supra, at 175, a federal program that coopts the States\xe2\x80\x99 political processes must be declared unconstitutional. \xe2\x80\x9c[T]he federal balance is too essential a part of our constitutional structure and plays too vital a role in securing freedom for us to admit inability to intervene.\xe2\x80\x9d Lopez, 514 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 578 (Kennedy, J., concurring).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s argument in this case at best pays lip service to the anticoercion principle. The Federal Government suggests that it is sufficient if States are \xe2\x80\x9cfree, as a matter of law, to turn down\xe2\x80\x9d federal funds. Brief for Respondents in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, p.\xc2\xa017 (emphasis added); see also id., at 25. According to the Federal Government, neither the amount of the offered federal funds nor the amount of the federal taxes extracted from the taxpayers of a State to pay for the program in question is relevant in determining whether there is impermissible coercion. Id., at 41\xe2\x80\x9346.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis argument ignores reality. When a heavy federal tax is levied to support a federal program that offers large grants to the States, States may, as a practical matter, be unable to refuse to participate in the federal program and to substitute a state alternative. Even if a State believes that the federal program is ineffective and inefficient, withdrawal would likely force the State to impose a huge tax increase on its residents, and this new state tax would come on top of the federal taxes already paid by residents to support subsidies to participating States.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t13\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAcceptance of the Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s interpreta- tion of the anticoercion rule would permit Congress to dic- tate policy in areas traditionally governed primarily at the state or local level. Suppose, for example, that Congress enacted legislation offering each State a grant equal to the State\xe2\x80\x99s entire annual expenditures for primary and secondary education. Suppose also that this funding came with conditions governing such things as school curriculum, the hiring and tenure of teachers, the drawing of school districts, the length and hours of the school day, the school calendar, a dress code for students, and rules for student discipline. As a matter of law, a State could turn down that offer, but if it did so, its residents would not only be required to pay the federal taxes needed to support this expensive new program, but they would also be forced to pay an equivalent amount in state taxes. And if the State gave in to the federal law, the State and its subdivisions would surrender their traditional authority in the field of education. Asked at oral argument whether such a law would be allowed under the spending power, the Solicitor General responded that it would. Tr. of Oral Arg. 44\xe2\x80\x9345 (Mar. 28, 2012).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tE\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhether federal spending legislation crosses the line from enticement to coercion is often difficult to determine, and courts should not conclude that legislation is unconstitutional on this ground unless the coercive nature of an offer is unmistakably clear. In this case, however, there can be no doubt. In structuring the ACA, Congress unambiguously signaled its belief that every State would have no real choice but to go along with the Medicaid Expansion. If the anticoercion rule does not apply in this case, then there is no such rule.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe dimensions of the Medicaid program lend strong support to the petitioner States\xe2\x80\x99 argument that refusing to accede to the conditions set out in the ACA is not a realistic option. Before the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s enactment, Medicaid funded medical care for pregnant women, families with dependents, children, the blind, the elderly, and the disabled. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396a(a)(10) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). The ACA greatly expands the program\xe2\x80\x99s reach, making new funds available to States that agree to extend coverage to all individuals who are under age 65 and have incomes below 133% of the federal poverty line. See \xc2\xa71396a(a) (10)(A)(i)(VIII). Any State that refuses to expand its Medicaid programs in this way is threatened with a severe sanction: the loss of all its federal Medicaid funds. See \xc2\xa71396c (2006 ed.).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMedicaid has long been the largest federal program of grants to the States. See Brief for Respondents in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, at 37. In 2010, the Federal Government directed more than $552 billion in federal funds to the States. See Nat. Assn. of State Budget Officers, 2010 State Expenditure Report: Examining Fiscal 2009\xe2\x80\x932011 State Spending, p.\xc2\xa07 (2011) (NASBO Report). Of this, more than $233 billion went to pre-expansion Medicaid. See id., at 47.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t14\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t This amount equals nearly 22% of all state expenditures combined. See id., at 7.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe States devote a larger percentage of their budgets to Medicaid than to any other item. Id., at 5. Federal funds account for anywhere from 50% to 83% of each State\xe2\x80\x99s total Medicaid expenditures, see \xc2\xa71396d(b) (2006 ed., Supp. IV); most States receive more than $1 billion in federal Medicaid funding; and a quarter receive more than $5 billion, NASBO Report 47. These federal dollars total nearly two thirds\xe2\x80\x9464.6%\xe2\x80\x94of all Medicaid expenditures nationwide.\n\t\t\t\t[\n\t\t\t\t\t15\n\t\t\t\t]\n\t\t\t\t Id., at 46.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court of Appeals concluded that the States failed to establish coercion in this case in part because the \xe2\x80\x9cstates have the power to tax and raise revenue, and therefore can create and fund programs of their own if they do not like Congress\xe2\x80\x99s terms.\xe2\x80\x9d 648 F.\xc2\xa03d 1235, 1268 (CA11 2011); see Brief for Sen. Harry Reid et\xc2\xa0al. as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, p.\xc2\xa021 (\xe2\x80\x9cStates may always choose to decrease expenditures on other programs or to raise revenues\xe2\x80\x9d). But the sheer size of this federal spending program in relation to state expenditures means that a State would be very hard pressed to compensate for the loss of federal funds by cutting other spending or raising additional revenue. Arizona, for example, commits 12% of its state expenditures to Medicaid, and relies on the Federal Government to provide the rest: $5.6 billion, equaling roughly one-third of Arizona\xe2\x80\x99s annual state expenditures of $17 billion. See NASBO Report 7, 47. Therefore, if Arizona lost federal Medicaid funding, the State would have to commit an additional 33% of all its state expenditures to fund an equivalent state program along the lines of pre-expansion Medicaid. This means that the State would have to allocate 45% of its annual expenditures for that one purpose. See ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe States are far less reliant on federal funding for any other program. After Medicaid, the next biggest federal funding item is aid to support elementary and secondary education, which amounts to 12.8% of total federal outlays to the States, see id., at 7, 16, and equals only 6.6% of all state expenditures combined. See ibid. In Arizona, for example, although federal Medicaid expenditures are equal to 33% of all state expenditures, federal education funds amount to only 9.8% of all state expenditures.\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\t See ibid. And even in States with less than average federal Medicaid funding, that funding is at least twice the size of federal education funding as a percentage of state expenditures. Id., at 7, 16, 47.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tA State forced out of the Medicaid program would face burdens in addition to the loss of federal Medicaid funding. For example, a nonparticipating State might be found to be ineligible for other major federal funding sources, such as Temporary Assistance for Needy Families (TANF), which is premised on the expectation that States will participate in Medicaid. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7602(a)(3) (2006 ed.) (requiring that certain beneficiaries of TANF funds be \xe2\x80\x9celigible for medical assistance under the State[\xe2\x80\x99s Medicaid] plan\xe2\x80\x9d). And withdrawal or expulsion from the Medicaid program would not relieve a State\xe2\x80\x99s hospitals of their obligation under federal law to provide care for patients who are unable to pay for medical services. The Emer- gency Medical Treatment and Active Labor Act, \xc2\xa71395dd, requires hospitals that receive any federal funding to provide stabilization care for indigent patients but does not offer federal funding to assist facilities in carrying out its mandate. Many of these patients are now covered by Medicaid. If providers could not look to the Medicaid program to pay for this care, they would find it exceed- ingly difficult to comply with federal law unless they were given substantial state support. See, e.g., Brief for Economists as Amici Curiae in No 11\xe2\x80\x93400, p.\xc2\xa011.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor these reasons, the offer that the ACA makes to the States\xe2\x80\x94go along with a dramatic expansion of Medicaid or potentially lose all federal Medicaid funding\xe2\x80\x94is quite unlike anything that we have seen in a prior spending-power case. In South Dakota v. Dole, the total amount that the States would have lost if every single State had refused to comply with the 21-year-old drinking age was approximately $614.7 million\xe2\x80\x94or about 0.19% of all state expenditures combined. See Nat. Assn. of State Budget Officers, 1989 (Fiscal Years 1987\xe2\x80\x93 1989 Data) State Expenditure Report 10, 84 (1989), http://www.nasbo.org/publications-data/state-expenditure-report/archives. South Dakota stood to lose, at most, funding that amounted to less than 1% of its annual state expenditures. See ibid. Under the ACA, by contrast, the Federal Government has threatened to withhold 42.3% of all federal outlays to the states, or approximately $233 billion. See NASBO Report 7, 10, 47. South Dakota stands to lose federal funding equaling 28.9% of its annual state expenditures. See id., at 7, 47. Withholding $614.7 million, equaling only 0.19% of all state expenditures combined, is aptly characterized as \xe2\x80\x9crelatively mild encouragement,\xe2\x80\x9d but threatening to withhold $233 billion, equaling 21.86% of all state expenditures combined, is a different matter.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhat the statistics suggest is confirmed by the goal and structure of the ACA. In crafting the ACA, Congress clearly expressed its informed view that no State could possibly refuse the offer that the ACA extends.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe stated goal of the ACA is near-universal health care coverage. To achieve this goal, the ACA mandates that every person obtain a minimum level of coverage. It attempts to reach this goal in several different ways. The guaranteed issue and community-rating provisions are designed to make qualifying insurance available and affordable for persons with medical conditions that may require expensive care. Other ACA provisions seek to make such policies more affordable for people of modest means. Finally, for low-income individuals who are simply not able to obtain insurance, Congress expanded Medicaid, transforming it from a program covering only members of a limited list of vulnerable groups into a program that provides at least the requisite minimum level of coverage for the poor. See 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396a(a) (10)(A)(i)(VIII) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), 1396u\xe2\x80\x937(a), (b)(5), 18022(a). This design was intended to provide at least a specified minimum level of coverage for all Americans, but the achievement of that goal obviously depends on participation by every single State. If any State\xe2\x80\x94not to mention all of the 26 States that brought this suit\xe2\x80\x94chose to decline the federal offer, there would be a gaping hole in the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s coverage.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIt is true that some persons who are eligible for Medicaid coverage under the ACA may be able to secure private insurance, either through their employers or by obtain- ing subsidized insurance through an exchange. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa736B(a) (2006 ed., Supp. IV); Brief for Respondents in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, at 12. But the new federal subsidies are not available to those whose income is below the federal poverty level, and the ACA provides no means, other than Medicaid, for these individuals to obtain coverage and comply with the Mandate. The Government counters that these people will not have to pay the penalty, see, e.g., Tr. of Oral Arg. 68 (Mar. 28, 2012); Brief for Respondents in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, at 49\xe2\x80\x9350, but that argument misses the point: Without Medicaid, these individuals will not have coverage and the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s goal of near-universal coverage will be severely frustrated.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIf Congress had thought that States might actually refuse to go along with the expansion of Medicaid, Congress would surely have devised a backup scheme so that the most vulnerable groups in our society, those previously eligible for Medicaid, would not be left out in the cold. But nowhere in the over 900-page Act is such a scheme to be found. By contrast, because Congress thought that some States might decline federal funding for the operation of a \xe2\x80\x9chealth benefit exchange,\xe2\x80\x9d Congress provided a backup scheme; if a State declines to participate in the operation of an exchange, the Federal Government will step in and operate an exchange in that State. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718041(c)(1). Likewise, knowing that States would not necessarily provide affordable health insurance for aliens lawfully present in the United States\xe2\x80\x94because Medicaid does not require States to provide such coverage\xe2\x80\x94Con- gress extended the availability of the new federal insurance subsidies to all aliens. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa736B(c) (1)(B)(ii) (excepting from the income limit individuals who are \xe2\x80\x9cnot eligible for the medicaid program .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. by reason of [their] alien status\xe2\x80\x9d). Congress did not make these subsidies available for citizens with incomes below the poverty level because Congress obviously assumed that they would be covered by Medicaid. If Congress had contemplated that some of these citizens would be left without Medicaid coverage as a result of a State\xe2\x80\x99s withdrawal or expulsion from the program, Congress surely would have made them eligible for the tax subsidies provided for low-income aliens.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThese features of the ACA convey an unmistakable message: Congress never dreamed that any State would refuse to go along with the expansion of Medicaid. Congress well understood that refusal was not a practical option.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Federal Government does not dispute the inference that Congress anticipated 100% state participation, but it argues that this assumption was based on the fact that ACA\xe2\x80\x99s offer was an \xe2\x80\x9cexceedingly generous\xe2\x80\x9d gift. Brief for Respondents in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93400, at 50. As the Federal Government sees things, Congress is like the generous benefactor who offers $1 million with few strings attached to 50 randomly selected individuals. Just as this benefactor might assume that all of these 50 individuals would snap up his offer, so Congress assumed that every State would gratefully accept the federal funds (and conditions) to go with the expansion of Medicaid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis characterization of the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s offer raises obvious questions. If that offer is \xe2\x80\x9cexceedingly generous,\xe2\x80\x9d as the Federal Government maintains, why have more than half the States brought this lawsuit, contending that the offer is coercive? And why did Congress find it necessary to threaten that any State refusing to accept this \xe2\x80\x9cexceed- ingly generous\xe2\x80\x9d gift would risk losing all Medicaid funds? Congress could have made just the new funding provided under the ACA contingent on acceptance of the terms of the Medicaid Expansion. Congress took such an approach in some earlier amendments to Medicaid, separating new coverage requirements and funding from the rest of the program so that only new funding was conditioned on new eligibility extensions. See, e.g., Social Security Amendments of 1972, \n\n86Stat.\n1465.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress\xe2\x80\x99 decision to do otherwise here reflects its understanding that the ACA offer is not an \xe2\x80\x9cexceedingly generous\xe2\x80\x9d gift that no State in its right mind would decline. Instead, acceptance of the offer will impose very substantial costs on participating States. It is true that the Federal Government will bear most of the initial costs associated with the Medicaid Expansion, first paying 100% of the costs of covering newly eligible individuals between 2014 and 2016. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71396d(y). But that is just part of the picture. Participating States will be forced to shoulder substantial costs as well, because after 2019 the Federal Government will cover only 90% of the costs associated with the Expansion, see ibid., with state spending projected to increase by at least $20 billion by 2020 as a consequence. Statement of Douglas W. Elmendorf, CBO\xe2\x80\x99s Analysis of the Major Health Care Legislation Enacted in March 2010, p.\xc2\xa024 (Mar. 30, 2011); see also R. Bovbjerg, B. Ormond, & V. Chen, Kaiser Commission on Medicaid and the Uninsured, State Budgets under Federal Health Reform: The Extent and Causes of Variations in Estimated Impacts 4, n.\xc2\xa027 (Feb. 2011) (estimating new state spending at $43.2 billion through 2019). After 2019, state spending is expected to increase at a faster rate; the CBO estimates new state spending at $60 billion through 2021. Statement of Douglas W. Elmendorf, supra, at 24. And these costs may increase in the future because of the very real possibility that the Federal Government will change funding terms and reduce the percentage of funds it will cover. This would leave the States to bear an increasingly large percentage of the bill. See Tr. of Oral Arg. 74\xe2\x80\x9376 (Mar. 28, 2012). Finally, after 2015, the States will have to pick up the tab for 50% of all administrative costs associated with implementing the new program, see \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396b(a)(2)\xe2\x80\x93(5), (7) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), costs that could approach $12 billion between fiscal years 2014 and 2020, see Dept. of Health and Human Services, Center for Medicaid and Medicare Services, 2010 Actuarial Report on the Financial Outlook for Medicaid 30.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn sum, it is perfectly clear from the goal and structure of the ACA that the offer of the Medicaid Expansion was one that Congress understood no State could refuse.
