The Medicaid Expansion therefore exceeds Congress\xe2\x80\x99 spending power and cannot be implemented.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tF\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSeven Members of the Court agree that the Medicaid Expansion, as enacted by Congress, is unconstitutional. See Part IV\xe2\x80\x93A to IV\xe2\x80\x93E, supra; Part IV\xe2\x80\x93A, ante, at 45\xe2\x80\x9355 (opinion of Roberts, C.\xc2\xa0J., joined by Breyer and Kagan, JJ.). Because the Medicaid Expansion is unconstitutional, the question of remedy arises. The most natural remedy would be to invalidate the Medicaid Expansion. However, the Government proposes\xe2\x80\x94in two cursory sentences at the very end of its brief\xe2\x80\x94preserving the Expansion. Under its proposal, States would receive the additional Medi- caid funds if they expand eligibility, but States would keep their pre-existing Medicaid funds if they do not expand eligibility. We cannot accept the Government\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe reality that States were given no real choice but to expand Medicaid was not an accident. Congress assumed States would have no choice, and the ACA depends on States\xe2\x80\x99 having no choice, because its Mandate requires low-income individuals to obtain insurance many of them can afford only through the Medicaid Expansion. Furthermore, a State\xe2\x80\x99s withdrawal might subject everyone in the State to much higher insurance premiums. That is because the Medicaid Expansion will no longer offset the cost to the insurance industry imposed by the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s insurance regulations and taxes, a point that is explained in more detail in the severability section below. To make the Medicaid Expansion optional despite the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s structure and design \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98would be to make a new law, not to enforce an old one. This is no part of our duty.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Trade-Mark Cases, \n\n100 U. S. 82, \n99 (1879)\n.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWorse, the Government\xe2\x80\x99s proposed remedy introduces a new dynamic: States must choose between expanding Medicaid or paying huge tax sums to the federal fisc for the sole benefit of expanding Medicaid in other States. If this divisive dynamic between and among States can be introduced at all, it should be by conscious congressional choice, not by Court-invented interpretation. We do not doubt that States are capable of making decisions when put in a tight spot. We do doubt the authority of this Court to put them there.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Government cites a severability clause codified with Medicaid in Chapter 7 of the United States Code stating that if \xe2\x80\x9cany provision of this chapter, or the application thereof to any person or circumstance, is held invalid, the remainder of the chapter, and the application of such provision to other persons or circumstances shall not be affected thereby.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U. S. C. \xc2\xa71303 (2006 ed.). But that clause tells us only that other provisions in Chapter 7 should not be invalidated if \xc2\xa71396c, the authorization for the cut-off of all Medicaid funds, is unconstitutional. It does not tell us that \xc2\xa71396c can be judicially revised, to say what it does not say. Such a judicial power would not be called the doctrine of severability but perhaps the doctrine of amendatory invalidation\xe2\x80\x94similar to the amendatory veto that permits the Governors of some States to reduce the amounts appropriated in legislation. The proof that such a power does not exist is the fact that it would not preserve other congressional dispositions, but would leave it up to the Court what the \xe2\x80\x9cvalidated\xe2\x80\x9d legislation will contain. The Court today opts for permitting the cut-off of only incremental Medicaid funding, but it might just as well have permitted, say, the cut-off of funds that represent no more than x percent of the State\xe2\x80\x99s bud- get. The Court severs nothing, but simply revises \xc2\xa71396c to read as the Court would desire.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWe should not accept the Government\xe2\x80\x99s invitation to attempt to solve a constitutional problem by rewriting the Medicaid Expansion so as to allow States that reject it to retain their pre-existing Medicaid funds. Worse, the Government\xe2\x80\x99s remedy, now adopted by the Court, takes the ACA and this Nation in a new direction and charts a course for federalism that the Court, not the Congress, has chosen; but under the Constitution, that power and authority do not rest with this Court.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tV\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tSeverability\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act seeks to achieve \xe2\x80\x9cnear-universal\xe2\x80\x9d health insurance coverage. \xc2\xa718091(2)(D) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). The two pillars of the Act are the Individ- ual Mandate and the expansion of coverage under Medicaid. In our view, both these central provisions of the Act\xe2\x80\x94the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion\xe2\x80\x94are invalid. It follows, as some of the parties urge, that all other provisions of the Act must fall as well. The following section explains the severability principles that require this conclusion. This analysis also shows how closely interrelated the Act is, and this is all the more reason why it is judicial usurpation to impose an entirely new mechanism for withdrawal of Medicaid funding, see Part IV\xe2\x80\x93F, supra, which is one of many examples of how rewriting the Act alters its dynamics.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tA\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWhen an unconstitutional provision is but a part of a more comprehensive statute, the question arises as to the validity of the remaining provisions. The Court\xe2\x80\x99s authority to declare a statute partially unconstitutional has been well established since Marbury v. Madison, 1 Cranch 137 (1803), when the Court severed an unconstitutional provision from the Judiciary Act of 1789. And while the Court has sometimes applied \xe2\x80\x9cat least a modest presumption in favor of .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. severability,\xe2\x80\x9d C. Nelson, Statutory Interpretation 144 (2010), it has not always done so, see, e.g., Minnesota v. Mille Lacs Band of Chippewa Indians, \n\n526 U.\xc2\xa0S. 172\n\xe2\x80\x93195 (1999).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tAn automatic or too cursory severance of statutory provisions risks \xe2\x80\x9crewrit[ing] a statute and giv[ing] it an effect altogether different from that sought by the measure viewed as a whole.\xe2\x80\x9d Railroad Retirement Bd. v. Alton R.\xc2\xa0Co., \n\n295 U.\xc2\xa0S. 330, \n362 (1935)\n. The Judiciary, if it orders uncritical severance, then assumes the legislative function; for it imposes on the Nation, by the Court\xe2\x80\x99s decree, its own new statutory regime, consisting of policies, risks, and duties that Congress did not enact. That can be a more extreme exercise of the judicial power than striking the whole statute and allowing Congress to address the conditions that pertained when the statute was considered at the outset.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court has applied a two-part guide as the framework for severability analysis. The test has been deemed \xe2\x80\x9cwell established.\xe2\x80\x9d Alaska Airlines, Inc. v. Brock, \n\n480 U.\xc2\xa0S. 678, \n684 (1987)\n. First, if the Court holds a statutory provision unconstitutional, it then determines whether the now truncated statute will operate in the manner Con- gress intended. If not, the remaining provisions must be invalidated. See id., at 685. In Alaska Airlines, the Court clarified that this first inquiry requires more than ask- ing whether \xe2\x80\x9cthe balance of the legislation is incapable of functioning independently.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 684. Even if the remaining provisions will operate in some coherent way, that alone does not save the statute. The question is whether the provisions will work as Congress intended. The \xe2\x80\x9crelevant inquiry in evaluating severability is whether the statute will function in a manner consistent with the intent of Congress.\xe2\x80\x9d Id., at 685 (emphasis in original). See also Free Enterprise Fund v. Public Company Accounting Oversight Bd., 561 U.\xc2\xa0S. ___, ___ (2010) (slip op., at 28) (the Act \xe2\x80\x9cremains fully operative as a law with these tenure restrictions excised\xe2\x80\x9d) (internal quotation marks omitted); United States v. Booker, \n\n543 U.\xc2\xa0S. 220, \n227 (2005)\n (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]wo provisions .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. must be invalidated in order to allow the statute to operate in a manner consistent with congressional intent\xe2\x80\x9d); Mille Lacs, supra, at 194 (\xe2\x80\x9c[E]m- bodying as it did one coherent policy, [the entire order] is inseverable\xe2\x80\x9d).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, even if the remaining provisions can operate as Congress designed them to operate, the Court must determine if Congress would have enacted them standing alone and without the unconstitutional portion. If Congress would not, those provisions, too, must be invalidated. See Alaska Airlines, supra, at 685 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]he unconstitu- tional provision must be severed unless the statute cre- ated in its absence is legislation that Congress would not have enacted\xe2\x80\x9d); see also Free Enterprise Fund, supra, at ___ (slip op., at 29) (\xe2\x80\x9c[N]othing in the statute\xe2\x80\x99s text or historical context makes it \xe2\x80\x98evident\xe2\x80\x99 that Congress, faced with the limitations imposed by the Constitution, would have preferred no Board at all to a Board whose members are removable at will\xe2\x80\x9d); Ayotte v. Planned Parenthood of Northern New Eng., \n\n546 U.\xc2\xa0S. 320, \n330 (2006)\n (\xe2\x80\x9cWould the legislature have preferred what is left of its statute to no statute at all\xe2\x80\x9d); Denver Area Ed. Telecommunications Consortium, Inc. v. FCC, \n\n518 U.\xc2\xa0S. 727, \n767 (1996)\n (plurality opinion) (\xe2\x80\x9cWould Congress still have passed \xc2\xa710(a) had it known that the remaining provisions were invalid\xe2\x80\x9d (internal quotation marks and brackets omitted)).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe two inquiries\xe2\x80\x94whether the remaining provisions will operate as Congress designed them, and whether Congress would have enacted the remaining provisions standing alone\xe2\x80\x94often are interrelated. In the ordinary course, if the remaining provisions cannot operate according to the congressional design (the first inquiry), it almost necessarily follows that Congress would not have enacted them (the second inquiry). This close interaction may explain why the Court has not always been precise in distinguishing between the two. There are, however, occasions in which the severability standard\xe2\x80\x99s first inquiry (statutory functionality) is not a proxy for the second inquiry (whether the Legislature intended the remaining provisions to stand alone).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tB\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Act was passed to enable affordable, \xe2\x80\x9cnear-universal\xe2\x80\x9d health insurance coverage. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(D). The resulting, complex statute consists of mandates and other requirements; comprehensive regulation and penalties; some undoubted taxes; and increases in some governmental expenditures, decreases in others. Under the severability test set out above, it must be determined if those provisions function in a coherent way and as Congress would have intended, even when the major provisions establishing the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion are themselves invalid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tCongress did not intend to establish the goal of near-universal coverage without regard to fiscal consequences. See, e.g., ACA \xc2\xa71563, \n\n124Stat.\n270 (\xe2\x80\x9c[T]his Act will reduce the Federal deficit between 2010 and 2019\xe2\x80\x9d). And it did not intend to impose the inevitable costs on any one industry or group of individuals. The whole design of the Act is to balance the costs and benefits affecting each set of regulated parties. Thus, individuals are required to obtain health insurance. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa75000A(a). Insur- ance companies are required to sell them insurance regardless of patients\xe2\x80\x99 pre-existing conditions and to comply with a host of other regulations. And the companies must pay new taxes. See \xc2\xa74980I (high-cost insurance plans); 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg(a)(1), 300gg\xe2\x80\x934(b) (community rating); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x931, 300gg\xe2\x80\x933, 300gg\xe2\x80\x934(a) (guaranteed issue); \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x9311 (elimination of coverage limits); \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x9314(a) (dependent children up to age 26); ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa79010, 10905, \n\n124Stat.\n865,\n1017 (excise tax); Health Care and Education Reconciliation Act of 2010 (HCERA) \xc2\xa71401, \n\n124Stat.\n1059 (excise tax). States are expected to expand Medicaid eligibility and to create regulated marketplaces called ex- changes where individuals can purchase insurance. See 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71396a(a)(10)(A)(i)(VIII) (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (Medicaid Expansion), 18031 (exchanges). Some persons who cannot afford insurance are provided it through the Medicaid Expansion, and others are aided in their purchase of insurance through federal subsidies available on health-insurance exchanges. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa736B (2006 ed., Supp. IV), \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718071 (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (federal subsidies). The Federal Government\xe2\x80\x99s increased spending is offset by new taxes and cuts in other federal expenditures, including reductions in Medicare and in federal payments to hospitals. See, e.g., \xc2\xa71395ww(r) (Medicare cuts); ACA Title IX, Subtitle A, \n\n124Stat.\n847 (\xe2\x80\x9cRev- enue Offset Provisions\xe2\x80\x9d). Employers with at least 50 employees must either provide employees with adequate health benefits or pay a financial exaction if an employee who qualifies for federal subsidies purchases insurance through an exchange. See 26\xc2\xa0U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa74980H (2006 ed., Supp. IV).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn short, the Act attempts to achieve near-universal health insurance coverage by spreading its costs to individuals, insurers, governments, hospitals, and employers\xe2\x80\x94while, at the same time, offsetting significant portions of those costs with new benefits to each group. For ex- ample, the Federal Government bears the burden of paying billions for the new entitlements mandated by the Medicaid Expansion and federal subsidies for insurance purchases on the exchanges; but it benefits from reductions in the reimbursements it pays to hospitals. Hospitals lose those reimbursements; but they benefit from the decrease in uncompensated care, for under the insurance regulations it is easier for individuals with pre-existing conditions to purchase coverage that increases payments to hospitals. Insurance companies bear new costs imposed by a collection of insurance regulations and taxes, including \xe2\x80\x9cguaranteed issue\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9ccommunity rating\xe2\x80\x9d requirements to give coverage regardless of the insured\xe2\x80\x99s pre-existing conditions; but the insurers benefit from the new, healthy purchasers who are forced by the Individual Mandate to buy the insurers\xe2\x80\x99 product and from the new low- income Medicaid recipients who will enroll in insurance companies\xe2\x80\x99 Medicaid-funded managed care programs. In summary, the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion offset insurance regulations and taxes, which offset reduced reimbursements to hospitals, which offset increases in federal spending. So, the Act\xe2\x80\x99s major provisions are interdependent.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Act then refers to these interdependencies as \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility.\xe2\x80\x9d See ACA Subtitle F, Title I, \n\n124Stat.\n242 (\xe2\x80\x9cShared Responsibility\xe2\x80\x9d); ACA \xc2\xa71501, ibid. (same); ACA \xc2\xa71513, id., at 253 (same); ACA \xc2\xa74980H, ibid. (same). In at least six places, the Act describes the Individual Mandate as working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether with the other pro- visions of this Act.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(C) (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cadd millions of new consumers to the health insurance market\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(E) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9csignificantly reduce\xe2\x80\x9d the economic cost of the poorer health and shorter lifespan of the uninsured); \xc2\xa718091(2)(F) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9clower health insurance premiums\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(G) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9cimprove financial security for families\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(I) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to minimize \xe2\x80\x9cadverse selection and broaden the health insurance risk pool to include healthy individuals\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(J) (working \xe2\x80\x9ctogether\xe2\x80\x9d to \xe2\x80\x9csignif- icantly reduce administrative costs and lower health insurance premiums\xe2\x80\x9d). The Act calls the Individual Mandate \xe2\x80\x9can essential part\xe2\x80\x9d of federal regulation of health insurance and warns that \xe2\x80\x9cthe absence of the requirement would undercut Federal regulation of the health insurance market.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa718091(2)(H).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tC\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tOne preliminary point should be noted before applying severability principles to the Act. To be sure, an argument can be made that those portions of the Act that none of the parties has standing to challenge cannot be held nonseverable. The response to this argument is that our cases do not support it. See, e.g., Williams v. Standard Oil Co. of La., \n\n278 U.\xc2\xa0S. 235\n\xe2\x80\x93244 (1929) (holding nonseverable statutory provisions that did not burden the parties). It would be particularly destructive of sound government to apply such a rule with regard to a multifaceted piece of legislation like the ACA. It would take years, perhaps decades, for each of its provisions to be adjudicated separately\xe2\x80\x94and for some of them (those simply expending federal funds) no one may have separate standing. The Federal Government, the States, and private parties ought to know at once whether the entire legislation fails.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe opinion now explains in Part V\xe2\x80\x93C\xe2\x80\x931, infra, why the Act\xe2\x80\x99s major provisions are not severable from the Mandate and Medicaid Expansion. It proceeds from the insurance regulations and taxes (C\xe2\x80\x931\xe2\x80\x93a), to the reductions in reimbursements to hospitals and other Medicare reductions (C\xe2\x80\x931\xe2\x80\x93b), the exchanges and their federal subsidies (C\xe2\x80\x931\xe2\x80\x93c), and the employer responsibility assessment (C\xe2\x80\x931\xe2\x80\x93d). Part V\xe2\x80\x93C\xe2\x80\x932, infra, explains why the Act\xe2\x80\x99s minor provisions also are not severable.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Act\xe2\x80\x99s Major Provisions\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tMajor provisions of the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x94i.e., the insurance regulations and taxes, the reductions in federal reimbursements to hospitals and other Medicare spend- ing reductions, the exchanges and their federal subsidies, and the employer responsibility assessment\xe2\x80\x94cannot remain once the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion are invalid. That result follows from the undoubted inability of the other major provisions to operate as Congress intended without the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion. Absent the invalid portions, the other major provisions could impose enormous risks of unexpected bur- dens on patients, the health-care community, and the federal budget. That consequence would be in absolute conflict with the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s design of \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility,\xe2\x80\x9d and would pose a threat to the Nation that Congress did not intend.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\ta\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tInsurance Regulations and Taxes\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWithout the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion, the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s insurance regulations and insurance taxes impose risks on insurance companies and their customers that this Court cannot measure. Those risks would undermine Congress\xe2\x80\x99 scheme of \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility.\xe2\x80\x9d See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa74980I (2006 ed., Supp. IV) (high-cost insurance plans); 42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg(a)(1) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), 300gg\xe2\x80\x934(b) (community rating); \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x931, 300gg\xe2\x80\x933, 300gg\xe2\x80\x934(a) (guaranteed issue); \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x9311 (elimination of coverage limits); \xc2\xa7300gg\xe2\x80\x9314(a) (dependent children up to age 26); ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa79010, 10905, \n\n124Stat.\n865,\n1017 (excise tax); HCERA \xc2\xa71401, \n\n124Stat.\n1059 (excise tax).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court has been informed by distinguished economists that the Act\xe2\x80\x99s Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion would each increase revenues to the insurance industry by about $350 billion over 10 years; that this combined figure of $700 billion is necessary to offset the approximately $700 billion in new costs to the insurance industry imposed by the Act\xe2\x80\x99s insurance regulations and taxes; and that the new $700-billion burden would otherwise dwarf the industry\xe2\x80\x99s current profit margin. See Brief for Economists as Amici Curiae in No. 11\xe2\x80\x93393 etc. (Severability), pp. 9\xe2\x80\x9316, 10a.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIf that analysis is correct, the regulations and taxes will mean higher costs for insurance companies. Higher costs may mean higher premiums for consumers, despite the Act\xe2\x80\x99s goal of \xe2\x80\x9clower[ing] health insurance premiums.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(F) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). Higher costs also could threaten the survival of health-insurance companies, despite the Act\xe2\x80\x99s goal of \xe2\x80\x9ceffective health insurance markets.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa718091(2)(J).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe actual cost of the regulations and taxes may be more or less than predicted. What is known, however, is that severing other provisions from the Individual Mandate and Medicaid Expansion necessarily would impose significant risks and real uncertainties on insurance companies, their customers, all other major actors in the sys- tem, and the government treasury. And what also is known is this: Unnecessary risks and avoidable uncertainties are hostile to economic progress and fiscal stability and thus to the safety and welfare of the Nation and the Nation\xe2\x80\x99s freedom. If those risks and uncertainties are to be imposed, it must not be by the Judiciary.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tb\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tReductions in Reimbursements to Hospitals and Other Reductions in Medicare Expenditures\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Affordable Care Act reduces payments by the Federal Government to hospitals by more than $200 billion over 10 years. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71395ww(b)(3)(B)(xi)\xe2\x80\x93(xii) (2006 ed., Supp. IV); \xc2\xa71395ww(q); \xc2\xa71395ww(r); \xc2\xa71396r\xe2\x80\x934(f)(7).\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe concept is straightforward: Near-universal coverage will reduce uncompensated care, which will increase hospitals\xe2\x80\x99 revenues, which will offset the government\xe2\x80\x99s re- ductions in Medicare and Medicaid reimbursements to hospitals. Responsibility will be shared, as burdens and benefits balance each other. This is typical of the whole dynamic of the Act.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tInvalidating the key mechanisms for expanding insurance coverage, such as community rating and the Medicaid Expansion, without invalidating the reductions in Medicare and Medicaid, distorts the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s design of \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility.\xe2\x80\x9d Some hospitals may be forced to raise the cost of care in order to offset the reductions in reimbursements, which could raise the cost of insurance premiums, in contravention of the Act\xe2\x80\x99s goal of \xe2\x80\x9clower[ing] health insurance premiums.\xe2\x80\x9d \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718091(2)(F) (2006 ed., Supp. IV). See also \xc2\xa718091(2)(I) (goal of \xe2\x80\x9clower[ing] health insurance premiums\xe2\x80\x9d); \xc2\xa718091(2)(J) (same). Other hospitals, particularly safety-net hospitals that serve a large number of uninsured patients, may be forced to shut down. Cf. National Assn. of Public Hospitals, 2009 Annual Survey: Safety Net Hospitals and Health Systems Fulfill Mission in Uncertain Times 5\xe2\x80\x936 (Feb. 2011). Like the effect of preserving the insurance regulations and taxes, the precise degree of risk to hospitals is unknowable. It is not the proper role of the Court, by severing part of a statute and allowing the rest to stand, to impose unknowable risks that Congress could neither measure nor predict. And Congress could not have intended that result in any event.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThere is a second, independent reason why the reductions in reimbursements to hospitals and the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s other Medicare cuts must be invalidated. The ACA\xe2\x80\x99s $455 billion in Medicare and Medicaid savings offset the $434-billion cost of the Medicaid Expansion. See CBO Estimate, Table 2 (Mar. 20, 2010). The reductions allowed Congress to find that the ACA \xe2\x80\x9cwill reduce the Federal deficit between 2010 and 2019\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cwill continue to reduce budget deficits after 2019.\xe2\x80\x9d ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71563(a)(1), (2), \n\n124Stat.\n270.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThat finding was critical to the ACA. The Act\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility\xe2\x80\x9d concept extends to the federal budget. Congress chose to offset new federal expenditures with budget cuts and tax increases. That is why the United States has explained in the course of this litigation that \xe2\x80\x9c[w]hen Congress passed the ACA, it was careful to ensure that any increased spending, including on Medicaid, was offset by other revenue-raising and cost-saving provisions.\xe2\x80\x9d Memorandum in Support of Government\xe2\x80\x99s Motion for Summary Judgment in No. 3\xe2\x80\x9310\xe2\x80\x93cv\xe2\x80\x9391, p. 41.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIf the Medicare and Medicaid reductions would no longer be needed to offset the costs of the Medicaid Expansion, the reductions would no longer operate in the manner Congress intended. They would lose their justification and foundation. In addition, to preserve them would be \xe2\x80\x9cto eliminate a significant quid pro quo of the legislative com- promise\xe2\x80\x9d and create a statute Congress did not enact. Legal Services Corporation v. Velazquez, \n\n531 U.\xc2\xa0S. 533, \n561 (2001)\n (Scalia, J., dissenting). It is no secret that cutting Medicare is unpopular; and it is most improbable Congress would have done so without at least the assurance that it would render the ACA deficit-neutral. See ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa71563(a)(1), (2), \n\n124Stat.\n270.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tc\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tHealth Insurance Exchanges and Their Federal Subsidies\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe ACA requires each State to establish a health-insurance \xe2\x80\x9cexchange.\xe2\x80\x9d Each exchange is a one-stop marketplace for individuals and small businesses to compare community-rated health insurance and purchase the policy of their choice. The exchanges cannot operate in the manner Congress intended if the Individual Mandate, Medicaid Expansion, and insurance regulations cannot remain in force.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Act\xe2\x80\x99s design is to allocate billions of federal dollars to subsidize individuals\xe2\x80\x99 purchases on the exchanges. In- dividuals with incomes between 100 and 400 percent of the poverty level receive tax credits to offset the cost of insurance to the individual purchaser. \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa736B (2006 ed., Supp. IV); \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718071 (2006 ed., Supp. IV). By 2019, 20 million of the 24 million people who will obtain insurance through an exchange are expected to receive an average federal subsidy of $6,460 per person. See CBO, Analysis of the Major Health Care Legislation Enacted in March 2010, pp.\xc2\xa018\xe2\x80\x9319 (Mar. 30, 2011). Without the community-rating insurance regulation, however, the average federal subsidy could be much higher; for community rating greatly lowers the enormous premiums unhealthy individuals would otherwise pay. Federal subsidies would make up much of the difference.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe result would be an unintended boon to insurance companies, an unintended harm to the federal fisc, and a corresponding breakdown of the \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibil- ity\xe2\x80\x9d between the industry and the federal budget that Congress intended. Thus, the federal subsidies must be invalidated.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tIn the absence of federal subsidies to purchasers, insurance companies will have little incentive to sell insurance on the exchanges. Under the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s scheme, few, if any, individuals would want to buy individual insurance policies outside of an exchange, because federal subsidies would be unavailable outside of an exchange. Difficulty in attracting individuals outside of the exchange would in turn motivate insurers to enter exchanges, despite the exchanges\xe2\x80\x99 onerous regulations. See \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa718031. That system of incentives collapses if the federal subsidies are invalidated. Without the federal subsidies, individ- uals would lose the main incentive to purchase insurance inside the exchanges, and some insurers may be unwilling to offer insurance inside of exchanges. With fewer buyers and even fewer sellers, the exchanges would not operate as Congress intended and may not operate at all.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThere is a second reason why, if community rating is invalidated by the Mandate and Medicaid Expansion\xe2\x80\x99s invalidity, exchanges cannot be implemented in a manner consistent with the Act\xe2\x80\x99s design. A key purpose of an exchange is to provide a marketplace of insurance options where prices are standardized regardless of the buy- er\xe2\x80\x99s pre-existing conditions. See ibid. An individual who shops for insurance through an exchange will evaluate different insurance products. The products will offer different benefits and prices. Congress designed the exchanges so the shopper can compare benefits and prices. But the comparison cannot be made in the way Congress designed if the prices depend on the shopper\xe2\x80\x99s pre-existing health conditions. The prices would vary from person to person. So without community rating\xe2\x80\x94which prohibits insurers from basing the price of insurance on pre-existing conditions\xe2\x80\x94the exchanges cannot operate in the manner Congress intended.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\td\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tEmployer-Responsibility Assessment\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe employer responsibility assessment provides an incentive for employers with at least 50 employees to provide their employees with health insurance options that meet minimum criteria. See \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa74980H (2006 ed., Supp. IV). Unlike the Individual Mandate, the employer-responsibility assessment does not require employers to provide an insurance option. Instead, it re- quires them to make a payment to the Federal Government if they do not offer insurance to employees and if insurance is bought on an exchange by an employee who qualifies for the exchange\xe2\x80\x99s federal subsidies. See ibid.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor two reasons, the employer-responsibility assessment must be invalidated. First, the ACA makes a direct link between the employer-responsibility assessment and the exchanges. The financial assessment against employers occurs only under certain conditions. One of them is the purchase of insurance by an employee on an exchange. With no exchanges, there are no purchases on the exchanges; and with no purchases on the exchanges, there is nothing to trigger the employer-responsibility assessment.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSecond, after the invalidation of burdens on individuals (the Individual Mandate), insurers (the insurance regu- lations and taxes), States (the Medicaid Expansion), the Federal Government (the federal subsidies for exchanges and for the Medicaid Expansion), and hospitals (the reductions in reimbursements), the preservation of the employer-responsibility assessment would upset the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s design of \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility.\xe2\x80\x9d It would leave employers as the only parties bearing any significant responsibility. That was not the congressional intent.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\tThe Act\xe2\x80\x99s Minor Provisions\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe next question is whether the invalidation of the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s major provisions requires the Court to invalidate the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions. It does.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe ACA is over 900 pages long. Its regulations include requirements ranging from a break time and secluded place at work for nursing mothers, see \n\n29 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7207(r)(1) (2006 ed., Supp. IV), to displays of nutritional content at chain restaurants, see \n\n21 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa7343(q)(5)(H). The Act raises billions of dollars in taxes and fees, including exactions imposed on high-income taxpayers, see ACA \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa79015, 10906; HCERA \xc2\xa71402, medical devices, see \n\n26 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa74191 (2006 ed., Supp. IV), and tanning booths, see \xc2\xa75000B. It spends government money on, among other things, the study of how to spend less government money. \n\n42 U.\xc2\xa0S.\xc2\xa0C. \xc2\xa71315a. And it includes a number of provisions that provide benefits to the State of a particular legislator. For example, \xc2\xa710323, \n\n124Stat.\n954, extends Medicare coverage to individuals exposed to asbestos from a mine in Libby, Montana. Another provision, \xc2\xa72006, id., at 284, increases Medicaid payments only in Louisiana.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSuch provisions validate the Senate Majority Leader\xe2\x80\x99s statement, \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know if there is a senator that doesn\xe2\x80\x99t have something in this bill that was important to them. .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. [And] if they don\xe2\x80\x99t have something in it important to them, then it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t speak well of them. That\xe2\x80\x99s what this legislation is all about: It\xe2\x80\x99s the art of compromise.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Pear, In Health Bill for Everyone, Provisions for a Few, N.\xc2\xa0Y. Times, Jan. 4, 2010, p. A10 (quoting Sen. Reid). Often, a minor provision will be the price paid for support of a major provision. So, if the major provision were unconstitutional, Congress would not have passed the minor one.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tWithout the ACA\xe2\x80\x99s major provisions, many of these minor provisions will not operate in the manner Congress intended. For example, the tax increases are \xe2\x80\x9cRevenue Offset Provisions\xe2\x80\x9d designed to help offset the cost to the Federal Government of programs like the Medicaid Expansion and the exchanges\xe2\x80\x99 federal subsidies. See Title IX, Subtitle A\xe2\x80\x94Revenue Offset Provisions, \n\n124Stat.\n847. With the Medicaid Expansion and the exchanges invalidated, the tax increases no longer operate to offset costs, and they no longer serve the purpose in the Act\xe2\x80\x99s scheme of \xe2\x80\x9cshared responsibility\xe2\x80\x9d that Congress intended.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tSome provisions, such as requiring chain restaurants to display nutritional content, appear likely to operate as Congress intended, but they fail the second test for severability. There is no reason to believe that Congress would have enacted them independently. The Court has not previously had occasion to consider severability in the con- text of an omnibus enactment like the ACA, which includes not only many provisions that are ancillary to its central provisions but also many that are entirely unre- lated\xe2\x80\x94hitched on because it was a quick way to get them passed despite opposition, or because their proponents could exact their enactment as the quid pro quo for their needed support. When we are confronted with such a so-called \xe2\x80\x9cChristmas tree,\xe2\x80\x9d a law to which many nongermane ornaments have been attached, we think the proper rule must be that when the tree no longer exists the ornaments are superfluous. We have no reliable basis for knowing which pieces of the Act would have passed on their own. It is certain that many of them would not have, and it is not a proper function of this Court to guess which. To sever the statute in that manner \xe2\x80\x9c\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x98would be to make a new law, not to enforce an old one. This is not part of our duty.\xe2\x80\x99\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x9d Trade-Mark Cases, 100 U. S., at 99.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThis Court must not impose risks unintended by Congress or produce legislation Congress may have lacked the support to enact. For those reasons, the unconstitutionality of both the Individual Mandate and the Medicaid Expansion requires the invalidation of the Affordable Care Act\xe2\x80\x99s other provisions.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\xe2\x80\x83\xe2\x80\x83*\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court today decides to save a statute Congress did not write. It rules that what the statute declares to be a requirement with a penalty is instead an option subject to a tax. And it changes the intentionally coercive sanction of a total cut-off of Medicaid funds to a supposedly noncoercive cut-off of only the incremental funds that the Act makes available.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court regards its strained statutory interpretation as judicial modesty. It is not. It amounts instead to a vast judicial overreaching. It creates a debilitated, inoperable version of health-care regulation that Congress did not enact and the public does not expect. It makes enactment of sensible health-care regulation more difficult, since Congress cannot start afresh but must take as its point of departure a jumble of now senseless provisions, provisions that certain interests favored under the Court\xe2\x80\x99s new design will struggle to retain. And it leaves the public and the States to expend vast sums of money on requirements that may or may not survive the necessary congressional revision.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Court\xe2\x80\x99s disposition, invented and atextual as it is, does not even have the merit of avoiding constitutional difficulties. It creates them. The holding that the Individual Mandate is a tax raises a difficult constitutional question (what is a direct tax?) that the Court resolves with inadequate deliberation. And the judgment on the Medicaid Expansion issue ushers in new federalism concerns and places an unaccustomed strain upon the Union. Those States that decline the Medicaid Expansion must subsidize, by the federal tax dollars taken from their citizens, vast grants to the States that accept the Medicaid Expansion. If that destabilizing political dynamic, so antagonistic to a harmonious Union, is to be introduced at all, it should be by Congress, not by the Judiciary.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe values that should have determined our course today are caution, minimalism, and the understanding that the Federal Government is one of limited powers. But the Court\xe2\x80\x99s ruling undermines those values at every turn. In the name of restraint, it overreaches. In the name of constitutional avoidance, it creates new constitutional questions. In the name of cooperative federalism, it undermines state sovereignty.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tThe Constitution, though it dates from the founding of the Republic, has powerful meaning and vital relevance to our own times. The constitutional protections that this case involves are protections of structure. Structural protections\xe2\x80\x94notably, the restraints imposed by federalism and separation of powers\xe2\x80\x94are less romantic and have less obvious a connection to personal freedom than the provisions of the Bill of Rights or the Civil War Amendments. Hence they tend to be undervalued or even forgotten by our citizens. It should be the responsibility of the Court to teach otherwise, to remind our people that the Framers considered structural protections of freedom the most im- portant ones, for which reason they alone were embod- ied in the original Constitution and not left to later amendment. The fragmentation of power produced by the structure of our Government is central to liberty, and when we destroy it, we place liberty at peril. Today\xe2\x80\x99s decision should have vindicated, should have taught, this truth; instead, our judgment today has disregarded it.\n\t\t\t

\n

\n\t\t\t\t\n\t\t\t\t\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\xc2\xa0\n\t\t\t\tFor the reasons here stated, we would find the Act invalid in its entirety. We respectfully dissent.\n\t\t\t

Notes

\n\n1\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The most authoritative legal dictionaries of the founding era lack any definition for \xe2\x80\x9cregulate\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cregulation,\xe2\x80\x9d suggesting that the term bears its ordinary meaning (rather than some specialized legal meaning) in the constitutional text. See R. Burn, A New Law Dictionary 281 (1792); G. Jacob, A New Law Dictionary (10th ed. 1782); 2 T. Cunningham, A New and Complete Law Dictionary (2d ed. 1771).\n\t\n
\n\n2\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg is therefore right to note that Congress is \xe2\x80\x9cnot mandating the purchase of a discrete, unwanted product.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 22 (opinion concurring in part, concurring in judgment in part, and dis-senting in part). Instead, it is mandating the purchase of an unwanted suite of products\xe2\x80\x94e.g., physician office visits, emergency room visits, hospital room and board, physical therapy, durable medical equipment, mental health care, and substance abuse detoxification. See Selected Medical Benefits: A Report from the Dept. of Labor to the Dept. of Health & Human Services (April 15, 2011) (reporting that over two-thirds of private industry health plans cover these goods and services), online at http://www.bls.gov/ncs/ebs/sp/selmedbensreport.pdf (all Inter-net materials as visited June 26, 2012, and available in Clerk of Court\xe2\x80\x99s case file).\n\t\n
\n\n3\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0In its effort to show the contrary, Justice Ginsburg\xe2\x80\x99s dissent comesup with nothing more than two condemnation cases, which it says demonstrate \xe2\x80\x9cCongress\xe2\x80\x99 authority under the commerce power to compel an \xe2\x80\x98inactive\xe2\x80\x99 landholder to submit to an unwanted sale.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 24. Wrong on both scores. As its name suggests, the condemnation power does not \xe2\x80\x9ccompel\xe2\x80\x9d anyone to do anything. It acts in\xc2\xa0rem, against the property that is condemned, and is effective with or without a transfer of title from the former owner. More important, the power to condemn for public use is a separate sovereign power, explicitly acknowledged in the \n , which provides that \xe2\x80\x9cprivate property [shall not] be taken for public use, without just compensation.\xe2\x80\x9d\nThus, the power to condemn tends to refute rather than supportthe power to compel purchase of unwanted goods at a prescribed price: The latter is rather like the power to condemn cash for public use. If it existed, why would it not (like the condemnation power) be accompanied by a requirement of fair compensation for the portion of the exacted price that exceeds the goods\xe2\x80\x99 fair market value (here, the difference between what the free market would charge for a health-insurance policy on a young, healthy person with no pre-existing conditions, and the government-exacted community-rated premium)?\n\t\n
\n\n4\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0No one seriously contends that any of Congress\xe2\x80\x99 other enumerated powers gives it the authority to enact \xc2\xa75000A as a regulation.\n\t\n
\n\n5\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Of course it can be both for statutory purposes, since Congress can define \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d in its enactments any way it wishes. That is why United States v. Sotelo, \n, does not disprove our statement. That case held that a \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d for willful failure to pay one\xe2\x80\x99s taxes was included among the \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes\xe2\x80\x9d made non-dischargeable under the Bankruptcy Code. 436 U.\xc2\xa0S., at 273\xe2\x80\x93275. Whether the \xe2\x80\x9cpenalty\xe2\x80\x9d was a \xe2\x80\x9ctax\xe2\x80\x9d within the meaning of the Bankruptcy Code had absolutely no bearing on whether it escaped the constitutional limitations on penalties.\n\t\n
\n\n6\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0The amicus appointed to defend the proposition that the Anti-Injunction Act deprives us of jurisdiction stresses that the penalty for failing to comply with the mandate \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collectedin the same manner as an assessable penalty under subchapter B of chapter 68,\xe2\x80\x9d \n (2006 ed., Supp. IV), and that such penalties \xe2\x80\x9cshall be assessed and collected in the same manneras taxes,\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76671(a) (2006 ed.). But that point seems to us to confirmthe inapplicability of the Anti-Injunction Act. That the penalty is tobe \xe2\x80\x9cassessed and collected in the same manner as taxes\xe2\x80\x9d refutes the proposition that it is a tax for all statutory purposes, including with respect to the Anti-Injunction Act. Moreover, elsewhere in the Internal Revenue Code, Congress has provided both that a particular payment shall be \xe2\x80\x9cassessed and collected\xe2\x80\x9d in the same manner as a tax and that no suit shall be maintained to restrain the assessment or collection of the payment. See, e.g., \xc2\xa7\xc2\xa77421(b)(1), \xc2\xa76901(a); \xc2\xa76305(a), (b). Thelatter directive would be superfluous if the former invoked the Anti-Injunction Act.\nAmicus also suggests that the penalty should be treated as a tax because it is an assessable penalty, and the Code\xe2\x80\x99s assessment provision authorizes the Secretary of the Treasury to assess \xe2\x80\x9call taxes (in-cluding interest, additional amounts, additions to the tax, and as-sessable penalties) imposed by this title.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa76201(a) (2006 ed., Supp.IV). But the fact that such items are included as \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes\xe2\x80\x9d for purposes of assessment does not establish that they are included as \xe2\x80\x9ctaxes\xe2\x80\x9d for purposes of other sections of the Code, such as the Anti-Injunction Act, that do not contain similar \xe2\x80\x9cincluding\xe2\x80\x9d language.\n\t\n

\n\n13\n\n\n\t\t\xc2\xa0Justice Ginsburg argues that \xe2\x80\x9c[a] State .\xc2\xa0.\xc2\xa0. has no claim on the money its residents pay in federal taxes.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 59, n. 26. This is true as a formal matter. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the United States Government taxes United States citizens, it taxes them \xe2\x80\x98in their individual capacities\xe2\x80\x99 as \xe2\x80\x98the people of America\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94not as residents of a particular State.\xe2\x80\x9d Ante, at 58, n. 26 (quoting U. S. Term Limits, Inc. v. Thornton, \n (Kennedy, J., concurring)). But unless Justice Ginsburg thinks that there is no limit to the amount of money that can be squeezed out of taxpayers, heavy federal taxation diminishes the practical ability of States to collect their own taxes.\n\t\n

Using these numbers, 44.8% of all federal outlays to both state and local governments was allocated to Medicaid, amounting to 16.8% of all state and local expenditures from their own sources.\n\t\n
